"THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin' Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal to the price stated (which is
published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian addiess.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
Aut Diabolus Aut Nihil is the title of a very
remarkable short story which appeared in
Blackwood’s some years ago, and which
now supplies a name for a collection of
stories by the same author. All are good
and clever ; and the diabolical one, in par-
ticular, has a haunting power which has
very rarely been paralleled. Cloth, 4s.
Fromont Younger and Risler Senior is a
good translation of Daudet’s well-known
book. The work was crowned by the
Academy, but the suffrages of readers had
anticipated the honor. “Fromont Jeune
et Risler Ain6” is in the author’s best
manner—simple, dramatic, truthful, and
strong ; for Daudet never forces his effects.
Beautifully illustrated ; cloth, 6s lOd.
Heine’s Book of Songs, translated by
“Stratheir,” is into a second edition
(cloth, 4s). The translation is one of the
best (or, rather, least worst) obtainable,
and about the cheapest.
The Wages of Sin, “ Lucas Malet’s ” very
striking novel, is into a 12th edition
(cloth, 4s).
Pudd’nhead Wilson, by Mark Twain (cloth,
4s), may be read ; but the author’s inspira-
tion grows obviously scanter.
Round the Red Lamp is the title of Conan
Doyle’s new book of tales (paper, 3s. ;
cloth, 45.). The stories are carefully
worked, but there is considerable loss of
interest as compared with the fascinating
yarns about Sherlock Holmes.
Thomas Hardy receives sympathetic treat-
ment from Annie Macdonell in a critical
memoir (cloth, 3s, 9d.). Here is a com-
ment-sentence worth remembering : “The
only real immorality possible in art is un-
truth.”
A Child of the Age, by Francis Adams,
appears in the “Keynotes” series (cloth,
4s. 4d.). A peculiar intensity of workman-
ship makes it interesting, and it is one of
the works on which the author’s reputa-
tion must finally rest.
Australian Books.
For the Term of His Natural Life, by Marcus
Clarke. Cloth, 2s 9d. The best Austra-
lian novel.
Robbery Under Arms, by Rolf Boldrewood,
Paper, 2s 9d ; Cloth, 3s 9d. The second-
best Australian novel.
Poems, by Henry Clarence Kendall. Cloth,
Bs.
Poems, by Adam Lindsay Gordon. Paper,
2s 9d ; Cloth, 5s 3d ; Cloth gilt, Bs.
Poems, by J. Brunton Stephens. Cloth
gilt, Bs. Also, in new edition—paper, 3s
9d ; Cloth, 6s 6d.
A Golden Shanty, by Bulletin waiters.
Paper, Is 3d.
Tales of the Convict System, by Price
Warung. Paper, Is 3d,
The Last of Six, by Ernest Favenc. Paper,
Is 3d.
A Queenslander’s Travel-Notes, by A. G.
Stephens. Paper, Is 3d.
By Reef and Palm, by Louis Beake. Paper,
Is 9d. Strong and fascinating.
Seven Little Australians, by Ethel Turner.
Cloth, 3s. A charming big-children’s book.
The Australians, by Francis Adams. Cloth,
3s. The best criticism of Australian char-
acter yet written.
Selections from Henry Lawson’s works.
Paper, Is. 3d. The budding of an Aus-
tralian genius.
The Art of Living in Australia, by Dr. P. E.
Muskett. Cloth, 4s, Practical health
hints, with 300 valuable cookery recipes.
Views of Melbourne. Views of Sydney.
Beauty Spots of Sydney. Beauty Spots of
N.S. Wales. Four small albums, 7in. by
6in., with 12 to 24 pictures very nicely
printed from photos. Cloth, Is 2d each.
Pictures of New South Wales. This book
contains in album form 100 large photo"
engravings of N.S.W. scenery, shipping,
and other matters of interest, and can be
warmly recommended as the best and
most comprehensive collection of the kind
yet offered. As a gift to far-away friends,
it cannot be surpassed. The binding and
finish are excellent. Posted to any Aus-
tralasian address for 5s 8d ; foreign 6s 2d.
Mrs. Raw'son’s Australian Poultry Book.
Paper, Is 3d. This book was specially
written for Australian conditions by Mrs.
Lance Rawson, of Rockhampton, Q.
Index Map of New South Wales. 2s 6d.
Measures 3ft. by 2ft. 6in.; folds to pocket
form. Showing roads, Caves, tourist re-
sorts, etc.; well up to date.
Some Books Worth Reading.
Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle. Paper, 2s lOd.
Adventures of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan
Doyle. Paper, 2s lOd.
A Study in Scarlet, by Conan Doyle. Paper,
Is 3d.
The Sign of Four, by Conan Doyle. Paper,
2s lOd. First-class detective stories.
The Woman in White, by Wilkie Collins-
Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is. The anchor’s
best book, well printed.
The Cloister and the Hearth, by Charles
Reade. Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is.
Tess of the D’Urbervilles, by Thomas Hardy.
Paper, 2s KM. A masterpiece of English
fiction.
Crime and Punishment, by Fedor Dostoieff-
sky. Paper, 3s. One of the most re-
markable of Russian realistic novels.
The Anarchist Peril, by Felix Dubois.
Cloth, 6s 6d. A startling exposure.
Lorna Doone, by R. D. Blackmore. Cloth,
3s. A charming English romance.
Looking Backward, by Edward Bellamy.
Paper, Is 3d. The bane or the antidote.
Pictures of the Socialistic Future, by Eugene
Richter. Paper, Is 3d. The antidote or
the bane.
Geoffry Hamlyn, by Henry Kingsley.
Cloth, 4s. A readable tale about Australia.
Studies in Pessimism, by Schopenhauer.
Paper, Is. 3d. Delightfully cynical.
The Green Carnation. Paper, 45.-; cloth,
6s. 6d. Witty and amusing.
The Human Species, by Prof. A. de Quatre-
fages. Cloth, 5s 6d.
Yirginibus Puerisque, by R. L. Stevenson.
Cloth, 6s. 6d, Essays perennially charm -
ing.
My Lady Nicotine, by J. M. Barrie. Cloth,
6s. 6d, A book chief among ten thousand
and altogether lovely.
Noughts and Crosses, by A. T. Quiller Couch.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. Some of the quaintest,
sweetest stories ever penned, with art
reaching its acme in deliciously subtle
simplicity.
In a Canadian Canoe, by Barry Pain. Cloth,
4s. Sketches, verses and stories varying
in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
Prose Fancies, by Richard Le Gallienne.
Cloth, 6s, 6d. A recent book of idyllic
essays.
Post-Prandial Philosophy, by Grant Allen.
Cloth, 4s. Readable essays.
The Autocrat of the Breakfast Table. The
Professor at the Breakfast Table. The
Poet at the Breakfast Table. By Dr,
O. W. Holmes. A beautifully-printed
edition ; neatly cloth-bound, 2s. lOd. each.
Also, pocket size, paper covers, 9d. each.
The Manxman, by Hall Caine . (cloth, 45.).
The author’s new book, powerful and
interesting.
The Tales of John Oliver Hobbes (including
“Some Emotions and a Moral,” “AStudy
in Temptations,” “A Bundle of Life,”
and “The Sinner’s Comedy”). Piquant
and clever. A charming edition, with
portrait of author ; cloth, 6s. 6d.
“Everybody’s Books.”
Handy little miscellanies, cloth-bound, 9d
each.
Everybody’s Pocket Cyclopaedia : 228 pp., 16
of them colored maps. The best book of
the kind published.
Everybody’s Book of Jokes : 192 pp. Jokes,
anecdotes, puns, bulls, conundrums, and
children’s sayings. Over 3000, new and
old.
Everybody’s Scrap-Book of Curious Facts :
320 pp. A book of odd and interesting
information upon every subject.
Everybody’s Book of Short Poems : 320 pp.
Hundreds of short poems, for memorising
’ and recitation. Most of them nob found
in other collections.
The Minerva Library.
The books are among the best and cheapest
published. They are all well printed ; many
of them illustrated ; and the cloth binding is
strong and serviceable. Posted for 2s 6d
each,

however stubborn and chronic, and the proprietors are

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
The Editor’s Departure .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He had sat him down, the able
Tanktown editor to think :
Bottles twain were on the table,
And in one of them was ink.
He’d sent out his quarter’s bills, and
He had patiently sat through
The anxiety that kills, and—
The return was one pound two.
He thought sadly of the day when
Up to Tanktown first he came,
And ’twas, “ What's your drink?” and “Say
when ”
Each man wild to sign his name.
Then how fair his hopes did blossom,
Dreaming how with wealth he’d bloat—
Till one brought him an opossum,
And another brought a goat—
(Each in lieu of proper payment
Of subscriptions for a year).
Then he went and rent his raiment,
And got paralysed on beer.
Now he felt in all his fibres
Thirst for slaughter great and grim,
As he thought of those subscribers,
And the game they’d played on him.
Eor he had been lured to Tanktown—
To his most exceeding hurt—
As a festive and a frank town,
Where folk looked on coin as dirt.
Well remembered he the frenzy
Of their joy when he got there ;
And how Fighting Bob Mackenzie
At the banquet took the chair ;
And the speeches that were spoken,
Saying now that he had come,
And the ice was fairly broken
Tanktown would be sure to hum ;
And the fearful kind of asses
Which they all became that night,
With the chairman smashing glasses,
And inviting him to fight.
Well, he’d done his duty fairly
By the district since he came,
And if folk would not act squarely
Surely he was not to blame !
He had brought his fine and high-wrought
Style, that Buskin’s self might please,
Down to writing pars on dry-rot,
And on foot-and-mouth disease.
He had tried—a vain alliance !
To enlighten and amuse,
With the latest things in science,
Deftly mixed with local news.
Thus an article on solar
Spots would head a par. to note
Simpson’s babe had cut a molar,
And a dirge on Duggan’s goat.
And with snake-yarns he had peppered
O’er the Times, the tastes to please
Of the mad and lonely shepherd,
And the bushman in D.T.’s.
Freedom’s fight he’d fought defiant,
As a pen-armed Paladin,
And had pierced, like Jack the Giant-
Killer, many a tyrant’s skin.
As a plotter and garotter,
And a pirate full of guile,
He’d denounced the local squatter,
In a fiercely-scathing style.
Oft he had, too, when left stranded
By his staff, in drunken spleen,
Brought the Times out, single-handed
Bullocking at the machine.
It would tax a strength like Milo’s,
And an iron brain, to keep
Up such strain—to write on silos,
Politics, and scab in sheep.
Thinking thus within his tiny
Office, tears began to flow—
But if he had known his Heine,
As a journalist should know,
Then the words he would have spoken
Would have been, in whole or part—■
‘‘l retire with sword unbroken,
But, alas, with broken heart!”
But he said —“ This life debases.
I must chuck it —though disguised
I should go—l’m sick for faces
Of folk somewhat civilised !”
Vainly Tanktown in the morning
For its editor did ask—
He had gone, and left no warning,
Save an empty whisky-flask.
Days may come when pressmen clever
May respect for Tanktown feign ;
But the Tanktown Times will never,
Never come to life again.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage, added where required —-
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One t ouna
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every weelcfor
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney . In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by'mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. r.
Archibald.
‘ The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Talcs or Sketches (those dealing with A us-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tub Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous an-
other'topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions ivill be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Go., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal : — The Standard and Diggers
News Go., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also;— R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 26, o.
A Fallen Idol.
A pew days ago a brief paragraph in the
daily papers announced that an almost-
furgotten person named Thomas M‘llwraith
had again left for England in search of
health. Nobody took any special interest in
the event ; hardly anybody attended to
witness the departure of the man who was
once, for a brief season, the Tribune of the
People, and who was almost within measure-
able distance of being first President of the
Australian Republic. The idol was so utterly
fallen that scarcely anyone remembered
what he had once seemed to be, and what
be might have been ; if Dagon, when he fell
from his pedestal in Ashdod, had then and
there been broken up for road-metal, his end
would have been no more commonplace than
that of MTlwraith. All that was visibly
evident of the once great Dictator was avast
old man, prematurely shattered and very
feeble, sad of countenance, and otherwise
the mere wreck of his former self. But the
pathos of it lay in the fact that no single
individual, save a few conventional friends,
sympathised with the fallen politician. It
is the common belief that his political career
is over that he will never again be
strong enough, either physically or men-
tally, to appear in the Legislature where he
used to be almost absolute master ;. and he
shuffles off the stage as wholly unnoticed as
if he had been only a super instead of a star
actor of his day. In the period of his great-
ness M‘llwkaith sold his party, and his
alleged Republican principles, and his
country, and his political honor, for a price,
and the price remains unpaid to this day.
And his reward is that the nation which
once held him as a demigod is almost as
unaware of his nresent existence as if he had
never been.
» * *
It is a common saying that the Democracy
is ungrateful, and so undoubtedly it often is.
But, compared to the ingratitude of the
aggregate Fat Man, that of the Democracy
counts for nothing. MTlwraith sacrificed
his whole career for the sake of his own Fat
Man instincts and his fellow Fat Men of
Queensland. Ten years ago he was the
statesman who annexed New Guinea, and
who might possibly have held it if he had
been sufficiently in earnest. It was he who
sacked one British Governor, and compelled
another to knock under in a humiliating
fashion on a question of the rights of the
people’s representatives against those of the
imperial figurehead of state. He asserted the
sovereign rights of Queensland again by .arrest-
ing an Imperial naval officer on board the ship
which he commanded. None of these things
were of any real value to the country, but
they showed the strength of the man and
what he might be and do Were he so in-
clined. He was the first avowedly Republi-
can Premier who had ever held office in
Australia. This was the high-water mark
of his popularity. The country knew him
as a man who was strong and fearless and
capable, and who could do great things if he
chose, and for a little while he 100 .ed as if
he was really going to do something great.
During the period when he stood on the
topmost pinnacle of popularity he might, if
he had cared to say the word a id his party
had been only reasonably faithful to him,
have abolished black labor, founded a btate
Bank, ejected the foreign usurers who now
prey upon the country, imposed a graduated
land-tax which would have broken up the big
estates and rescued the finances from the
slough of despond in which they have been
embedded ever since, and abolished foreign
borrowing. He might even have done some-
thing tangible towards the Federation of
Australia, and if he could have brought it about
the Premiership of the Commonwealth
would have been his almost to a certainty.
There was one supreme moment in
MTlwraith’s career when he might have
done almost anything if he had been a Demo-
crat, as he professed to be, or if he had even
possessed sufficient acuteness to be a Demo-
crat by self-interest.
• * #
But the instincts of the huckster and the
commission-agent were in his soul, and he
did nothing of what was expected of him.
Higinbotham, Avith such power in his hands,
would have aimed at being the greatest
democratic statesman in Australian history,
and have tried to found an ideal state which
would have been a shining example to the
world at large. MTlwraith only saw an
opportunity to be the biggest land-jobber in
Australia ; to gridiron the country all over
with land-grant railways, and make a
stupendous boom, in which there would be
unlimited pickings for the Fat Man in gene-
ral ; to hustle great areas of fertile country
into the market, and make another boom ;
to borroAv large sums for alleged public
works, and make some more boom ; to start
importing cheap nigger-labor on a larger
scale than ever, and to live in an atmos-
phere of financial razzle-dazzle generally. He
quarrelled with his ministerial colleagues
chiefly over a remarkable “ deal” in certain
mining shares. Then he coalesced with his
bitterest opponent, Griffith, in order to
carry through his stupendous scheme of
jobbing away all available Queensland to
railway syndicates. He employed the public
finances to prop up the unspeakable Queens-
land National Bank. He acted as Griffith’s
evil genius wherever that flashy, ambitious
lawyer was not already his own evil genius,
and helped him along the downward path
until Griffith, who had intended to be the
deputy-Providence of Queensland, found
himself the most execrated public man, save
one, in the country, and retired to the
Supreme Court bench to escape from politics,
and his partner, and himself. And then
MTlwraith himself suddenly crumbled
away and no one noticed that he was gone.
* # *
He was never even paid the price of his
apostacy. His land-grant railways were
never built, for the expected British syndi-
cates were not forthcoming. His share
transactions were mostly unlucky. His un-
speakable bank suspended, and reconstructed,
and now leads a feeble existence as a mere
shadow of its former self. His borrowing
policy came to grief through the stringency
of the British money-market, and the land-
boom which was to make all the Fat
Men of Queensland rich didn’t eventuate.
MTlwraith to-day is vastly poorer than
he was in his proiesiedly Republican days,
and his comparative poverty, like that
of Queensland in general, is mostly caused
bv the great schemes for which he aban-
doned his Republicanism and deserted the
National Party. His further reward consists
in the fact that, while he was still remem-
bered of the people, he ivas the most widely-
execrated man in the North and in political
oblivion.
* * *
MTlavraith, a few years ago, rejoiced in
the reputation of being the one essential
man in Queensland politics. Had he proved
the Democrat that he professed to be he
would have been essential, for it was he who
brought the National Party together,
and in his better days he the
only alleged Democrat Avith sufficient
influence to keep that motley political army
in a state of discipline. The belief that he
Avas an indispensable force in Queensland
public life was pleasant to his vain and
inflated soul, but when he abandoned his
ephemeral creed that distinction went with
the rest. M‘llwraith, the Democrat, was
indispensable, for there was no other man
of sufficient strength who was prepared to
take the leadership under the same condi-
tions, and to profess the faith that he then
professed. MTlwraith, the unsuccessful
railway syndicator, the land-boom apostle,
the advocate of borrowed money and nigger-
labor and Imperialism, the magnate of an
unsuccessful bank and the director of an
unlucky tinned-meat company, was nobody.
Every Fat Man in Queensland was ready
and willing to take Ids place, and even the
most ordinary Fat Man was able to fail —
under the same hopeless conditions - just as
successfully as he did. Therefore, when
sickness compelled him to drop out of the
race he did not even create a vacancy. The
sun rose and set, and the shares of the Q.N.
Bank dropped steadily, and the land-boom
didn’t come, and the cheap nigger-trouble and
the shearing riot and the deficit all arrived
regularly, just as before. Parliament allowed
the fallen Dictator to draw his Ministerial
salary —as a sort of charitable allowance —-
for work which he didn’t and couldn t per-
form, and which he will probably never per-
form again, and which really doesn’t matter
in any case seeing that latterly it consisted
mostly in helpless waiting on the Fat Man’s
Providence ; but except that his name still
appears as a nominal member of the Cabinet
he might as well have never existed.
* # *
The moral of MTlwraith’s career, is that
political apostacy doesn’t pay, and that the
Fat Man is a worse master to serve than
even the Democracy. If the fallen potentate
of Auchenflower had been true to his trust
he might have done a great work, and when
he died he would have been followed to the
grave by the ephemeral regrets of all Aus-
tralia, and the people would have promised
him a stupendous monument of polished
marble, which, as likely as not, would never
have been forthcoming. As it is he will be
regretted by no one, and his monument
would be the curses of the entire nation, if
it were not that the unstable community has
already forgotten what he was, or what he did,
or what he left undone. His apostacy
shortened his lease of power ; it paid no
cash dividends ; it filled his life with bitter-
ness ; and, so far as his political importance
went, it made him a nothing. When he dies
he will be as little missed as if a cart-
horse had dropped on its daily round.
Therefore, The Bulletin repeats the solemn
warning—a warning which should be written
large over the door of every House of Par-
liament, and over the Speaker’s chair, and
the seat of the President of the Legislative
Council, and embossed on the Parliamentary
notepaper, and engraved on a brass-plate on
the Premier's door, and written everywhere
where every politician can see it—Apostacy
doesn’t pay.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Patriotism of Running Away.
As a fine sample of a purely Australian variety
of patriotism the following, from Sydney Tele-
graph, is worth quoting :
The Turner Government, instead of expiring with some
remnants of dignity clinging to it, is fattening itself on a
vegetarian diet, with a view to prolonging its graceless
days in the land. In other words, it is voraciously
devouring the leek flung in its teeth by the Legislative
Council, and asking for more. . . “1 1 view of the
attitude of the Legislative Council, it has decided to drop
its land-value taxation proposals for the present.” That
is the candid confession of the trembling officeholders
who are now administering Victorian views. . . Of
course the Government could hardly be expected to resign
over such a mere trifle as having forfeited the confidence
of Parliament. . . So the Government is on all-fours
before the dominant House of Have, and hopes, by
“going through” the public once more, to get enough
revenue without needing to finger the big private money
ba >-g. . . The already shrivelled and shrunken muni-
cipal subsidy is to be reduced by £lOO,OOO, so the country
districts will be filled with almost impassable roads, along
which unemployed maintenance men will flounder vainly
from place to place seeking work.
This is how young Australia is taught his poli-
tical duty by the particular darned fool who is
set up as his instructor. That the facts in the
above shriek are wrong in every separate
particular doesn’t matter so very much. The
Turner Government hasn’t forfeited the
confidence of Parliament; it has still a big
majority in the People’s Chamber, and it never
had, and was never expected to have, the con-
fidence of the Chamber of Property. It hasn’t
44 dropped” its land-tax proposals £ the situa-
tion is simply that the forms of Parliament
prohibit them being brought forward again this
session, and something must be done in the
interim. Also the reduction of the municipal
subsidy of £lOO,OOO means ,£lOO,OOO more land
and property tax, for the local bodies will have
to make up the loss by levying extra rates.
But the matter which calls for most execration
is the assumption made by so many cheap and
nasty patriots that a Government can only
show its purity and disinterestedness by re-
signing at the earliest opportunity and leaving
the country in the lurch. The Turner Cabinet
was returned with a large majority in the
Assembly, for the express purpose of squaring
the finances. It hadn t a majority in the
Council, for the nation, under the present con-
stitution, couldn’t give it one, but it had the
biggest support that the nation could give
it.° Then, being defeated in the Council, its
duty, according to the doctrine of cheap and
nasty patriotism, is to leave its majority, and
the country, and the finances in a worse state
than before, aud fly in order to save its
own mean little dignity. Its retirement
wouldn’t help the land-tax in any way, for no
Government which attempts to pass a laud-tax
will get any more support in the Council than
Turner’s has done ; it would only increase the
existing confusion and make the deficit worse,
and deprive Victoria of the services of about
the best Government which can be formed out
of the existing materials. The Telegraphs
theory which, unfortunately, is also the
theory of most of the ignorant demagogues of
Australia —is that every Government holds
office only for the sake of the salary, and never,
by any chance, has even a semblance of a desire
to help its country in any way ; and this cheap
and nasty variety of patriotism is one of the
many things which help to degrade Australian
politics. Victoria gave the Turner Govern-
ment a distinct mandate to stop the awful
accumulation of deficits. It tried to do so in
the best way possible—by levying a land-tax ;
but being blocked by an obstacle which even the
nation hasn’t the power to overcome, it proposes
to try what it considers the second-best ex-
pedient instead. Whereupon the Tory press,
and also a section of the press which calls itself
Liberal, declares that the only pure and truly
patriotic thing to do under the circumstances is
to bolt, like a Chinese army, after the first
defeat, and leave the country to the mercy of
Fat Man, without a second effort. Common-
sense would say that, so long as the Popular
Chamber supports him by a strong majority, it
is Turner’s duty to keep up his end of the
fight against the House of 48 Rich Landlords
to the bitter end for the sake of the country ;
but, according to the Telegraph and the rest
of the Fat press, the politician who stays to
see the trouble through is a sordid money-
grubber, and the only true hero is the politician
who runs away.
Borrowed Money and Produce-Prices.
Editor Bulletin, —Of the four propositions
you give me to consider the last shall be first : —
(4) “ The fault ia with the principle of the loans—not
the interest.” Which is exactly what Tub Bulletin said.
Why should “ F.” argue at such length and so indignantly
to prove that he agrees with this paper ?
As to argument, you heeded it, and that
absolves its guilt and pardons its error ; as to
indignation, that always goes with conscious
virtue, and I repudiate it. You set forth a
good test-question thus :
(1) When a country has to pay a fixed debt with pro-
duce that is steadily declining in value, how can produce
help being a fair criterion ?
Produce would be a fair criterion if the amount
of exertion needed to produce it remained
constant, and all monopoly - premium were
eliminated. Wheat, for instance, was 565. in
’73, and is only 265. to-day—but the difference
does not represent loss to the farmer, or we
would be face to face with the proposition that
in ’73 a profit of 30s. a quarter was made on
wheat. If we say that the farmer in ’73 made
15s. a quarter, and that the cost-price remains
the same—then the farmer of to-day is losing
15s. a quarter. (Vic. Tories are saying at this
moment something like this, so it may be taken
as disproved). There are at least four causes
for the drop in selling-prices of produce :
(i a) Increased competition.
(6) Cheaper and better machinery.
(c) Cheaper and quicker freight.
(d) Cheaper domestic supplies.
The latter three cheapen the cost-price as well
as the selling-price. The very influence which
cheapened our prices has enabled us to increase
our output. Here are figures showing the leaps
and bounds of the New South Wales wool
exports, not only in weight, but in value :
1872 .. 50,233,45311)8. .. £3,342,900
1887 .. 224,295,2091b5. .. £9,200,071
1892 .. •• £10.211,456
In wheat-production Maoriland sprang in 20
years from 3,391,634 bushels to 8,378,217
bushels, and Victoria from 4,752,289 bushels to
14,814,645 bushels. Queensland, which owned
1,343,093 cattle in 1873, owned 6,591,416 two
years ago. The New South Wales tallow
export, worth .£176,798 in ’72, sprang to
£432,322 in ’92. Till ’79 the exportation of
killed fresh meat from Australia and Maoriland
was nil, and now it is what it is. The average
Australian butter-export was 1494 pounds
avoirdupois during ’Bl—’9o; in ’94 it was
£500,000 sterling for Victoria alone. These
figures seem to show that if the interest is to
be paid in produce, then the produce is avail-
able. The joke of the tiling is that the people
who own the produce escape taxation for the
most part. In the past Australian Treasurers
have depended on
Revenue-taxes at the Customs, which penalise the
wage-earner principally ; . ,
And land-sales, which are a mortgage of posterity s
birthright.
To-day the Customs revenues are shrinking,
and the land is giving out. The time is come
for direct taxation. I do not think that the
land-tax would decrease production—in fact, I
think this almost a flaw in the land-tax. Aus-
tralia will regret going in so heavily for frozen
meat and dairy produce exports. In the matter
of wheat, Australia got a bad bargain. There
are other countries that can more easily feed
Europe. Argentina’s wheat product jumped
from a nominal amount in ’Bl to 3,986,000
bushels in 1884. And as it was with wheat, so
it will be with meat and butter. What is
wanted is an Australian National policy that
will eschew the export trade for its own sake.
Let exports be the accident, not the aim of
Australian industry. Protection (not revenue
customs) and Federation can give us an autonomy
free from the sweating vices of inferior races.
Then :
(2) Tub Bulletin never said that interest should drop
with the price of produce. If interest did, then it would
follow that the principal should drop also.
The Bulletin is poking fun at me. If produce
were a fair criterion of value, then a quantity
of produce fixed in accordance with values at
the time of floating the loan would be a just and
equitable return for both principal and interest.
If produce were a fair criterion, then, as the
gold-value of the produce dropped, the gold-
value of principal and interest should drop too.
(3) If The Bulletin had said so, still it doesn’t follow
that wages should drop also.
Employers are already attempting to use the
“fall in prices” to “bear” wages, and it is
because of this that I took the liberty of dis-
cussing my aspect of a certain Bulletin para-
graph. —F.
A Rousing Fight:
Several N.S. W. Legislative Councillors reckon
on stirring results from Attorney Slattery’s
proposed reference of the local Payment of
Members Act to the local »Supreme Court,
If the court sustain the interpretation now
sought to be given to the words of the Act, a


situation of unparalleled difficulty may be
easily created. And a strong faction in the
Council is determined to make the most of the
opportunity. This faction, regarding a struggle
with the Lower House as inevitable in the near
future, prefers to strike on a question which
will give it the advantage of defending the
supremacy of locally-administered law. If the
den mcrats of the Lower House insist on appeal-
ing to the Privy Council, they will put a rod in
pickle for their own backs afterwards,
and if they draw their salaries while
the appeal is pending they will father a prece-
dent of an obnoxious kind. There is no doubt
that the will of the country sanctions payment
of members, and that the Lower House repre-
sents the country in that particular ; hut the
soul of the constitution under which Parliament
lives is that the Lower House can give elfect to
the will of the country only along certain lines
of procedure. If Reid goes on paying and
“ never minding ” the Supreme Court decision
in a matter affecting his own pocket and the
pockets of his fellows, why does he not act simi-
larly in a host of other matters which involve
the interests of the community, but which are
at a standstill for want of legal sanction ? If
he re-introduce the Bill, it is certain it
will be rejected by the Upper House. It
passed that Chamber on the last occasion
by a majority of only one, and that one,
John Davies, declares he will now oppose
the measure tooth and nail. Then if the (pies-
tion be remitted to the country there will be
witnessed the weird spectacle of Reid, who
still asserts that he is opposed to payment of
members, calling on electors to remodel the
Legislative Council because it also is opposed to
payment. Nor is Dibbs in a better position,
aior is Parkes. Both are pronounced opponents
•of payment. The three chiefs of the Lower
House will consequently have to agree before
the constituencies—first, that payment of mem-
bers is wrong in principle; second, that the
Upper House is, in condemning it, also wrong ;
and, third, that they three who believe it
wrong, but who at the same time want it
‘carried into law, are the true saviours of the
•country. The rough and ready plan of paying
members, notwithstanding the adverse decision
•of the Supreme Court, will expose the members
•so receiving pay to prosecutions both protracted
•and expensive, and it is certain that those
prosecutions will be started as the Council’s
•reply to the move; while, as for creating a
batch of new councillors, there is confidence that
fthe new Bill, or the desired amendment of
'the Act, will be rejected by such a majority as
will make it impossible for Reid to swamp it.
Altogether, the situation appears to have been
•cunningly planned by a party which comprises
'some who fight for fight’s sake, others who
ifight in hopes of blocking legislation now in
band, and still others who fight because the
battle will provide rich spoil for the bandits of
(the bar and their hungry little jackals.
Queensland Separation.
'Ogden, Labor M.P. for Townsville, Q., counsels
“downright obstruction” until the Southern
majority in Parliament agrees in disgust to
'Central and Northern Separation. Well, any-
thing rather than the present policy of
•apathy—which to outsiders seems to justify
MTlwraith’s boast that “ Separation is dead.”
The burden of keeping up the agitation is now
•cast on the Labor-members, in spite of the dis-
inclination of the Southern Labor authorities to
•do anything which may lessen their influence
by creating equal and independent authorities in
the Centre and North. It is plainly of no use
for Separationists to trust to genteel silver-tail
politicians, whom it is easy for the Govern-
ment to nobble with a lectureship or a port-
folio, like Black and Philp, or to frighten
through the banks. Yet the desire and need
for Separation grow every day more obvious.
The only two journals of importance, north of
Brisbane, which oppose or would delay
it, are the Northern Miner at Charters
Towers, piping to the order of a capitalistic
proprietary ; and the Townsville Bulletin, a
timid exponent of monopolistic dread of the
Labour-party. Hardly another newspaper fails
regularly to point out that the revenues
of the Centre and North are being every
year misappropriated to make up the deficit
of the South; that Southern Government
of the Centre and North is unfair and
incapable, and must always remain so ;
and that the longer things are left as at pre-
sent, the greater will be the debt that is being
piled up by the South for Centre and North to
liquidate. The Labor-party, and Central and
Northern politicians generally, are making a
great mistake when they let Separation slide in
the anticipation that “ the justice of the cause”
will some day move the Imperial Government
to interfere. Separation should be put in the
forefront of the battle; it should take pre-
cedence of almost every other question ; and it
should be shouted in season and out of season
until the South is forced to yield assent to the
creation of two new independent self-governing
provinces to divide with it the immense terri-
tory of Queensland.
Tiie Rue Australian Resurrection.
The Rue Australian wheel is round again to
the old credulous notch, and a fresh consign-
ment of devotees is off to be sacrificed on the
altar of Lane. Some of them are wives whose
husbands are already in Argentine bilboes ;
others are belated pilgrims who paid their
cash down a year ago without getting a
passage, and have now borrowed more to
ensure it; and the remainder are people
who have apparently slept right through the
newspaper commotion about the jiggers, and
the flies, and the fever, and the vermin, and
the lost ideals, and the blank and utter desola-
tion of settlement life, and are sailing off into
the unknown with the old eternal hope of
better things in which the first emigrants
exulted before experience had taught them
sense. This sudden resurrection of the
Rue Australian corpse, after every sensi-
ble person had definitely interred it,
shows that no one should ever presume to
imagine he has plumbed the depth of native
folly in average humanity. To most people
Rue Australia was a very far back number, and
the only question of pressing importance in
connection with it was—when were the Aus-
tralian Governments going to bring back the
stranded mates ? Anil now the Sublime Crank
rises up and proves once more that the ordinary
rules of logic don’t apply to him, and that the
evidence of failure which convinces everybody
else is not enough to convince him, and that he
is, in fact, just the same old simpleton that he
always was. This perfectly natural and per-
fectly unexpected behavior has for a first result
just this : that the lone lorn Rue Australians
who have been represented as wringing
their hands on the South American shore,
and weeping for their passage-money back
to Australia, may continue to wring and weep
until further notice. For it is now plain that
until the horrid Rue Australian corpse admits
itself that it is dead, and washes itself, and sends
for the undertaker, it is of no use whatever
for any Australian Government to find the cash
for burying it. If it will persist in getting up
out of its grave and dancing in its shroud, it must
simply be permitted to dance until it is ex-
hausted. When there are found people so
obstinate or so stupid that they persist in going
oil to Rue Australia without in the least know-
ing how they are to get back again, it is plain
that, once a safe return is guaranteed in case
of emergency, half the Australian population
might wish to undertake the trip. The spectacle
of the Rue Australian Association continually
sending new adventurers away while Govern-
ment is continually bringing the old ones back,
cannot be contemplated with satisfaction by the
taxpayer who is asked to stump up a salvage
fund for the derelicts. Evidently Rue Aus-
tralia must stew in its own juice a little while
longer before it will deign to appear reasonably
grateful for being lifted out of the fire.
The Brotherhood of “Loyalty.”
The gilded Mr. Henry Wrixon read a paper
lately at the Colonial Institute—subject: “ The
National Significance of the Ottawa Confer-
ence ” (where he figured as a delegate from
Victoria) a dull, platitudinal, drivelling,
grovelling sermon upon the beauty of “ silken
bondage,” as understood by the Wrixon frater-
nity. “To my mind,” droned the oppressive
knight, “ the significant feature of the Ottawa
gathering was the warm feeling of common
brotherhood towards the Australians that it
evoked from the Canadians —a brotherhood
founded upon the fact that they were all sub-
jects of the same gracious Queen.” It goes
without saying that a person who drags the
fiction of Y.R.’s “graciousness” about with
him is a constitutional libeller of national char-
acter. The worthlessness of Wrixon’s evi-
dence is betrayed at once by the greasy
little word “gracious.” Nature has ordained
that Tory loyalty shall be blind to the
absurdity of its pet phrases, and deaf to the
titter which they provoke from the world at
large. The lack of all sense of the ridiculous
in “ loyal ” cant and customs is bound to hasten
monarchy’s doom—but this by the way. Mr.
Wrixon libels Canada most outrageously with
his assumption that the country greeted him as
a subject of an old lady in Great Britain.
Forty-nine out of every fifty Canucks cared no
more about Wrixon and the other delegates to
that Ottawa Conference than Australians in
general cared about a certain Parkin, who
came out here from Canada some years ago to
push the Imperial Federation League. Parkin
(the only man who ever wore a belltopper in
Far Northern Queensland) was banqueted and
cheered by little knots of Freetrade blather-
skites. He, too, revelled in the Wrixonic
delusion of a brotherhood based upon inter-
national reverence for the same gracious Queen.
He misrepresented Canadian sentiment among
people who entertained him with an exactly simi-
lar perversion of the truth as regards Australia’s
feeling towards the “mother country ” and its
figurehead. Parkin subsequently read papers
to imperialists who were ready to swallow his
“facts.” Wrixon has been doing likewise.
Also, it was a significant feature of the recent
Colonial Institute meeting that the opinions ex-
pressed by Wrixon’s paper tvere warmly
endorsed by Parkin aforesaid. The circle of
“colonial” drivel and grovel is so very small
that when W rixon had his yarn to tell Parkin
was beaming in the immediate vicinity. He
personified the w'arm feeling that the other
man spoke of. The Canada known to Wrixon
is almost entirely inhabited by Parkin, whilst
the Parkinian Australia is chiefly the land of
the late “Money” Miller’s son-in-law. The
two countries, as known to their respective
masses of population, are held loosely together
by ties of blood and language, especially bad
language re Messrs. P. and W., but the gracious
Queen is no factor in their mild friendship.
She is merely a link between Parkin and
Wrixon, nor can the brotherhood of those
dreary romancers be fairly called “ common.”
On the contrary, it is a highly genteel sort of
fraternity, attired in West of England cloth,
and remarkable for its regular attendance at
church.
Fat Man Finance.
Mr. Wynne, of Vic. Legislative Council,
was seized the other day with a great idea
whereby the province, he reckons, can save
.£150,000 annually and thus obviate the
necessity for any more land-tax for years to
come. Victoria is paying about £260,000 per
annum to a number of pensioners of the average
age of 63 years. If the country borrowed
£2,250,000 at 4 per cent, it could with that
amount either pay each man the cash-value of
his pension in a lump sum, or else, if he refused
the lump sum, buy him an annuity in a first-
class insurance society, and thus get rid of the
whole crowd. The interest on the loan would
be £90,000 per annum, and by adding 1 per
cent, for a sinking-fund the debt would be
extinguished in 50 years, the total cost of in-
terest and sinking-fund being £112,500 a year.
Probably it would be rather less, 4 per cent,
being an outside estimate. The country, as
already mentioned, is now paying £260,000 per
annum, so Wynne demonstrates that by this
great financial scheme he would save £150,000
every year. What he does not point out is that if
the province staggers on as it is doing at present,
most of its pensioners —almost all of them being
persons of great age—will be dead in ten years,
and the burden will be lifted for ever; while
under the new scheme it must go on paying for
half-a-century. The choice lies, practically,
between paying ,£260,000 annually for about 10
years (equal to £2,600,000), or £llO,OOO
annually for 50 years (equal to £5,500,000). It
is only one more development of the mean,
foolish, thriftless system of passing on the bill
to the next generation, and The Bulletin
hereby denounces Wynne as a financial ass and
a Micawber of the worst description. “ It is
right that posterity should bear part of the
burden,” says Wynne. Why is it right ?
The Cloven Hoof.
Thus the Argus on the present position in
Victoria :
The one task of the day is to put the interests of the
land in a healthy and prosperous condition, all other con-
siderations beiiijf subordinate to that.
This is a fairly frank and honest statement of
the Tory position, for the Argus. The re-
sources and energies of almost every community
can be bluffed into making the dominant
interest more dominant, and if bluff can win in
Victoria the Argus holds a handful of trumps.
When popular opinion is excited it declares
itself bound by democratic principles, and then
when re-action, which is languor, sets in it
asserts, without arousing derision, that the land-
owners’ cause is the cause of democracy. To
suggest that the State shall come to the
assistance of the men who have made bad
speculations in land is not midsummer madness
—in Victoria. It is magnificent bluff; and the
chances are that it will win for the present.
In the past the land-monopoly has had the
cream off Victoria’s milk —and now it wants
the skim-milk as well. The land-boom was
the Tory gospel of monopoly and greed put into
practice; the reconstruction-time was a fanatical
repetition of the same fanatical gospel—and
now, in the hour of her repentance and abase-
ment, Victoria is asked to re-affirm the hideous
lie that the community belongs to the land.
“ The one task of the day ” is to put the com-
munity into a healthy and prosperous condition.
It should be protected, in the first place, from
its inveterate enemy, the land-monopolist—and
the instrument to cut the creature’s claws is the
Land-tax.
Honor among Spiritualists.
Mrs. Besant’s excuse for not exposing the
cheat of the bogus Mahatma —that “she pre-
ferred to suffer under a charge of fraud rather
than divulge private documents ” —shows a
total misconception of “the point of honor.”
It is a misconception very much akin to that
which accuses Mr. T. S. Henry of ungentle-
manly conduct because he exposed the Mellon
spook trickery in Mrs. Mellon’s own house,
and without giving her warning, though he
had previously been on the very best terms
with the whole spooky fraternity. Yet
Mr. Henry unquestionably did right, and
Mrs. Besant as certainly did wrong. The
code of conventionality was never intended
to shield fraud. Nobody has any right, for
example, to become party to a crime by con-
cealing it, simply because knowledge of the
crime came under a pledge of secrecy. The
etiquette of private communications, of hospi-
tality, of friendship, is merely a kind of low
morality, by which those who cannot do chival-
rous things of their own motion are trained to
do them by force of habit and for the sake of their
fellows’respect. Butitcannot claim tohamperor
supersede the higher morality which forbids to
injure others by hiding from them truth which
they have a right to know. To see one’s best
friend engaged in gross deceit, gaining by that
deceit money or esteem from credulous persons,
and to make no sign, is to take the guilt of the
deception upon one’s own shoulders ; and the
friendship which demands such a sacrifice is
not friendship, and can never claim friendship’s
privileges. Would any man, knowing that
only by exposure he could save the victims,
hesitate to expose a friend who was a deliberate
poisoner ? And should there be more regard
for one who poisons men’s bodies than for
another who poisons men’s minds ?
The Logic of Tallow.
A person calling himself “ Storekeeper” writes
to the Sydney Telegraph to state that some
time ago he sent a quarter-cask of tallow and a
bundle of sheepskins 134 miles by rail, and the
freight (165.) nearly swamped the proceeds.
Therefore, Commissioner Eddy is a failure, the
system of State ownership is a failure, and if the
railways “ were worked by private companies
the Government would gain and the public
would be served in a far more efficient manner.”
This faith in private monopoly on the part of a
man who is presumably intelligent enough to
run a store is touching in the extreme. Aus-
tralia has more railway accommodation in pro-
portion to population than any other country
on earth—it has six times as much as Great
Britain, Ireland, France or Germany, about
eight times as much as Austria, Holland,
Belgium and Spain, twelve times as much as
Italy, fifty-three times as much as India, and
considerably more than the U.S. or Canada.
No country where railways are built solely by
private enterprise is one-third so well-served
as Australia, and yet the Government is con-
sistently cursed for not serving it better.
Despite this lavish service, in New South
Wales, last half-year, the expenses were cut
down lower than in almost any other country
in the world —much lower than those of the
United States, Canada, Great Britain, Ireland,
France, Germany, Russia, Austria, Italy,
Sweden, Norway, Denmark, Holland, Belgium,
Switzerland, Roumania, India, and South
America. And, as there were no dividends to
pay to any private shareholders, the rates were
also cut down as low as they could be cut —so low
that even with all this extraordinary cheapness
of management they didn’t quite pay interest
and expenses. And yet “ A Storekeeper ” who
believes that the world revolves round his own
quarter-cask of tallow, hasn’t' the slightest
doubt that “ private enterprise” would be a
vast improvement on this state of things. His
logic is that when a very small quantity of a
very low-priced product can’t be sent a very
long distance profitably, something dreadful
must be wrong with the whole system ; and he
never even dreams that in “ private enter-
prise ” countries there are abundance of pro-
ducts which can’t be sent profitably by rail for
exactly the same reasons as in N.S. Wales. A
good many Australians evidently don’t know
when they are well off.
*
The New South Wales railways and tramways
report for the half-year ended December, 1894,
is probably the last which will be issued under
Mr. Commissioner Eddy’s management. It is
also the best of the series. The revenue has
increased by £20,000 when compared with the
corresponding half-year of 1893, and the expen-
diture has diminished by £75,000, making the
net profit £95,000 better than it was a year ago.
The railways are now worked for the amazingly
low expenditure of 46J per cent, of the total
earnings—which is very nearly the lowest per-
centage in the world. A few years ago Com-
missioner Speight declared that it was impos-
sible to work the Vic. railways for an
expenditure of less than 05 per cent, of the
gross earnings, and the fact that Mr. Eddy
has reduced his expense account nearly
20 per cent, below this estimate and
has still kept the lines in splendid order
is a striking testimony to his business capacity.
The present New (South Wales Commissioner
has doubled the net earnings of his department,
chiefly by economy and skilful management, and
his total value to the province has been some-
thing like two and a-half millions in cash.
Under these circumstances it would be worth
the Government’s while to offer him <£lo,ooo a
year to remain. Or if he won’t remain, he
should be asked to nominate some man as like
himself as possible to be his successor. One of
the most abjectly pitiable aspects of the situa-
tion is the proposal, by the rule-of-thumb
“retrenchment” party —mostly consisting, by
the way, of notorious party politicians—to
reinstate Mr. Goodghap as sole commis-
sioner, chiefly on the ground that his appoint-
ment will save two or three thousands per
annum in salary. The prospect of saving .£2OOO
or £3OOO annually, and losing possibly £lOO,OOO
or £200,000 or £300,000 through feeble and
inefficient management, is not attractive to
anybody save that kind of retrencher whose one
idea is always to get the cheapest man, no
matter what his qualifications or want of quali-
fications may be. So far, Eddy is the one
thoroughly successful railway manager Aus-
tralia has had, and the only one who seems to
know what a railway manager should be.
At a Melb. suburban police-court lately a
woman proceeded against her husband for fail-
ing to pay up the maintenance-money agreed
upon under a deed of separation. The husband
offered instead to take her back and provide
her with a home, whereupon the wife explained
that she had refused to go home because her
husband was keeping another woman in the
house, and she would not accept the position of
housekeeper to her husband and the strange
female. The crowd in the court guffawed
cheerfully just here as at something humorous.
The defendant offered no denial of the charge,
but simply demanded that his wife should
come home. Then the Bench informed the
plaintiff that it was her duty to go
home to her husband—and live at peace
with the “other woman ” apparently. It was
pointed out that the husband had agreed
to the separation, and had pledged himself to
pay a certain sum for maintenance, and that
the court was only requested to enforce the
agreement, but all the court was prepared to
say was that it was “ her duty ” to go home.
Then the case was dismissed, and the wife has,
presumably, to choose between starving and
living peaceably with the other woman afore-
said. The New Woman has evidently not
arrived yet in that Melb. suburb, and the old
woman is still looked upon by the local police
authorities as a chattel and an article of mer-
chandise. Melbourne Herald, by the way,
called it “An Amusing Case.” God help the
Herald’s ideas of amusement !
It has been carefully explained in half-a-score
of leading Tory journals that Victoria is very
lucky to get Brassey for Governor, and the
Argus and S.M. Herald laid particular stress
upon the fact that the millionaire was quite
content with the honor and glory of the job,
and wasn’t at all particular about salary in the
crooked state of the province’s finances. So
Vic. Assembly cut the pay down to £5OOO a
year, which is still about 1000 times as much as
the job is worth ; and the indignant Brassey,
who, it is explained, doesn’t care for lucre, but
has a large fund of amour propre, has imme-
diately cabled out that if the figures aren’t raised
to at least £7OOO, he probably won’t come at
all. As this would relieve Victoria of much
worry, and save money which can now ill be
spared, The Bulletin exhorts Vic. Assembly to
stick to its resolution. Then, if the enraged peer
continues to sulk, the Colonial Office can be
informed of the ghastly truth that the Governor
is of no public utility whatever, and that a
junior clerk at £l5O a year can do all that is
needed in the way of transmitting documents.

A protest by the British hatters against the Victorian
duties on hats has reached the Customs department,
through the Secretary of State for the Colonies (Lord
Ripon), who asks the Government to report on the mail er.
The Hat Manufacturers’ Association, representing the
British trade, has appealed to the Board of Trade, asking
that representations be made to the Victorian Govern-
ment, with a view of securing a “ rearrangement of the
tariff on a more equitable basis.” They complain of the
duty on silk hats, and particularly that on felt hats, and
quote figures up to 1892 to show that each increase in the
tariff has reduced this trade with the colony, and the last
increase has been practically prohibitive, reducing the
importations from 23,000 dozen a year to about one-third
of the quantity. It is remarked that the tariff is evidently
imposed purely for the benefit of the Victorian manufac-
turers, and not for the purposes of revenue, which was
considerably larger when the duties were less. With fur
and wool on the spot, the Australian manufacturers should
be sufficiently proteoced with 12s. or 15s. a dozen duty in-
stead of 365., but an ad valorem duty for revenue only
would be the most equitable.

purely for the benefit of the Victorian hat
manufacturers.” Whose benefit, in the name of

A Single-Tax Community.
Certain of the agricultural districts of England
have arrived, by accident, within the last few
years, at something approaching that blissful
condition at which the Freetrade-cum-Single-tax
party aims, and the results are interesting in
more ways than one. England, so far as agri-
cultural produce is concerned, is a purely Free-
trade country, therefore one of ’the required
conditions has already been attained. In
Essex, a reasonably fertile county lying at the
very back-door of London, agriculture has
reached so low an ebb that good farms can now
be obtained rent-free, the sole consideration
being that the tenant will relieve the owner of
rates and taxes, and will cultivate the land and
prevent it relapsing into a state of utter desola-
tion. And even on these terms the land, in a
vast number of cases, cannot be let; where it
is fit only for wheat-growing purposes it cannot
be let in any case whatever. The taxes on
land, which the Essex farmer is so utterly
unable to pay, represent, perhaps, one-
third of his share of the total public and
local expenditure as it would be under the
Georgian policy; under the pure Freetrade
and Single-tax regime they would be at least
twice as heavy, and the agriculturist would
have to compete with foreign producers on
exactly the same disastrous conditions as at
present. The additional tax could not he
deducted from the landlord’s rent, as there
is absolutely no rent from which to make
deductions, therefore it must come as an addi-
tional burden on the tenant who is utterly
unable to meet his present burdens. Under
these conditions it is absolutely certain that
Single-tax-and-Freetrade would be the last
straw on the agricultural industry in England,
As a theory they are attractive in many
respects. As a reality Freetrade is disastrous
to all countries save those where wages are the
very lowest, and the Single-tax leaves the
ruinous pressure of cheap foreign competition
just where it was before. The Henry-George-
ites, who believe that a pure Single-tax system
would end all the woes of the industrial com-
munity, are hereby invited to consider the con-
dition of a class which, by a series of accidents,
has come to enjoy all the advantages, and only
half the disadvantages, of their proposed
system, and whose condition, nevertheless, is
one of general bankruptcy.
Queensland sugar output for the season just
closing is estimated at 92,000 tons—about
14,000 tons better than for ’93-4. The co-
operative mills have again done splendidly.
The small farmers rely almost equally with the
big planters on cheap colored labor, so their
gain profits the white employee almost as
little. But the joint stock mills have per-
formed good service in teaching exactly how
much it costs to make a ton of sugar—know-
ledge which the planters studiously concealed.
Hence the enormous profits of the sugar in-
dustry, in spite of low prices, can be seen ;
and advocates of “ Australia for the white
man ” are in a position to prove conclusively
that greed is the only thing which prevents
that industry from paying white men’s wages.
The veteran Saul Samuel is doubtless rubbing
his head in astonishment at the cloud which
has so suddenly risen on his peaceful horizon.
Saul is 75, but he looks likely to hang on to
New South Wales Agent-Generalship until he
is 95, unless interfered with; and Premier
Reid has given him a broad hint that
other feet are itching to fill his shoes,
and if he doesn’t gracefully retire he will
be brutally retired. It is an open secret
that G. ex-Republican Dibbs yearns to see his
friend Wales again and puff more big cigars,
and hankers badly after the billet. Premier
Reid is anxious to build a bridge of gold for the
flying enemy, reckoning that things will be
made much easier at home ; and the Reidian
organ smooths the way by pointing out Saul
Samuel’s disqualifications in a lengthy leading
article. Next, it will be proved that G. R. D.
is the only possible man for the job, and an
attempt will be made to raise the screw, to suit
his large ideas.
The Argus has broken out in a fresh place :
“ We would wish that the British Government
could see its way to provide for the payment of
colonial Governors. The cost would be little
. . . and no step probably would do more to
consolidate the Empire.” The treason of the
Argus is not often so deep. Australia does
not want England to provide the hire of her
Governors, because she does not want Gover-
nors at all, and does not care a rap about the
consolidation of the Empire. To abandon the
“privilege” of finding the Governors’ wages
whilst the monarchical spooks are foisted upon
us would be to throw over the one thing that
gives us a satisfactory feeling of bossing the
job in a sense. Certainly the Governors pro-
vided and paid by the ’Ome authorities would
be much more the puppets of ignorant and un-
wieldy Imperialism than those at present in-
flicted upon us, and any objections on the part
of Australia would be more likely to be regarded
as beastly impertinence by “ that man Rogers.”
The Argus idea is to enhance the consequence
of the regal effigies by elevating them above the
criticism of disloyal and unmannerly persons in
our Parliaments who are always mauling over
vice-royalty to see if it is worth the money.
By all means let us pay our Governors. The
less the better, but pay them.
+
Postmaster-General Cook, of N.S. Wales,
continues, with varying success, his efforts to
make the community moral by bursting open
and reading betting correspondence at the
Post Office. A number of the biggest and most
honest of the bookmakers are “ prohibited,”
and several dozen small, and often shady, ones
are not, therefore one result has been a mere
transference of the wicked gambling business
from one man to another. A few more have
been driven into Queensland, along with a num-
ber of “ sweep” promoters, and, P.M.G. Cook
nob being able to stop correspondence addressed
to another province, the business goes on as
prosperously as ever, the only change being a
serious loss of revenue to N.S. Wales. Mean-
while, the Post Office continues to carry the
correspondence of the obscene medical quack,
and the person who advertises for a lady to ac-
company him in an immoral capacity on a trip
to Melbourne, and the female horror who sells
mysterious compounds to ladies only, and that
Dutch firm which floods New South Wales with
obscene price-lists of obscene photographs, and
many other objectionable persons ; and the
saintly Cook hasn’t applied to Parliament for
authority to suppress any of them. This shows
a lop-sidedness and want of finish about Cook
which is aggravating to all right-thinking per-
sons. If the public morals can be improved in
any way by stopping letters ab the Post-office,
it is Mr. Cook’s obvious duty to go the com-
plete moral hog and do the work thoroughly,
and his failure in this respect is a cause of grief
in pious circles.

(Last Nights.)

Bijou “ The Foundling.”
Last week The Bulletin regretfully men-
tioned that it didn’t know Mr. Paul Grey, whose
“ superior comedy co.” had announced a season at
Sydney School of Arts hall, and the appearance
of that gentleman only cast more obscurity upon
him. There was a smattering of people present
on the opening night, and a scanty company, only
two of whom the present writer ever heard of
before. “The most original story yet seen upon
the stage,” as the advt. put it, proved to be a
straggling, hashy thing concerning a girl who was
engaged to a baronet, and who attempted to test
his sincerity by announcing that she and her
family had suddenly lost all their money. Where-
upon the noble-hearted baronet married her, despite
her alleged poverty, and the story came to a
sudden end at half-past nine. The aud'ence was
stunned in the early part of the performance, but
later on it recovered somewhat, and amused itself
by applauding furiously and cheering the company
on to destruction. When the show came to an
untimely end it called loudly for the author, out
of a natural anxiety, no doubt, to inquire where
he got that hat, or something to the same effect;
and in response a misguided young person, who had
played the part of a flunkey, fled across the front
of the stage without stopping to explain whether
he was the author or not. He certainly looked
very like a man who might be the author of such
a piece, but this is mere theory. Most of the co.
didn’t know its part, but the audience kindly
helped it out with suggestions at critical moments,
or else cheered until the stranded one got in motion
again. After everything was over, some of the
audience even went round to the back-door and
wanted to give three cheers for the author, and
one for the noble-hearted baronet, and another for
the piece generally, but these enthusiasts un-
fortunately lost their way in the back premises of
the building.
* * * *
In this issue appears an excellent portrait
of Miss Riy Jones, the leading lady of the panto-
mime “Cinderella,” at Sydney Lyceum. This
clever little woman is a native of Maoriland, and
has been a resident of Australia for six years
past. She was a pupil of Professor Ure and of
the late Madame Lucy Chambers. Her first
public appearance was at Prahran (Melbourne)
with the Philharmonic Society, when she played
the part of Selina in the cantata, “ Endymion.”
Miss Ray Jones was well-known as a singer at the
promenade concerts at Melb. Exhibition Building
in the boom days, and at many concerts held in
Melbourne Town Hall. She has also played a
long list of leading operatic parts, among them
being Germaine in “ Les Cloches de Corneville,”
Phyllis in “ lolanthe,” Gianetta in “ The Gondo-
liers,” Mabel in “The Pirates of Penzance,”
Josephine in “Pinafore” (with only three days
study), and Gretohen in “Rip Van Winkle.”
Altogether a remarkable record for so young an
artiste.
** * *
The Brough and Boucicault co. will re-
appear at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday, after a
long and painful absence. This is one of the
events which form a bright oasis in the great,
gritty desert of existence, and which are as re-
freshing as a cold tombstone on a hot-wind night.
Theco. includes all the old crowd which this com-
munity has revered in days gone by—notably
Brough, Titheradge, and Miss Grace Noble, whom
it has revered even more than the rest—and four
or five new personages engaged by Dot Boucicault
on his recent visit to the cold, damp bosom of his
mother country. The Sydney season is to last 12
weeks, and during that period it is intended to
produce half-a-dozen plays never yet seen in the
city of Parkes. “ Sowing the Wind” is announced
for the opening night, and after that come the
“New Woman,” the “Ideal Husband,” “John
A’ Dreams” (by Hadden Chambers), the “ Mas-
queraders,” and the “Case of Rebellious Susan.”
This is a powerful list, especially considering that
Australia has seen comparatively little save re-
vivals for the past year, and if the Lyceum doesn’t
have an outbreak of great prosperity, then there
is no justice left on this earth.
** * *
The “House that Jack Built” (at Her
Majesty’s) and the “Span of Life” (at Sydney
Royal) are both prospering exceedingly, and no
reports of a change of programme are heard from
either house. The same chaste and original remark
applies to the Kinetoscope exhibition, which is
still at its old address in Pitt-st., opposite the
Strand, and to “Nana,” the rather disappointing
picture of the lady in an absent bathing-costume,
which show, if The Bulletin remembers aright,
has been doing its very last week for three months
past, previous to which period it was going away
immediately. It is still doing its very last
week, and will probably continue so until further
notice.
The Sydney suburban tour of “Charley’s
Aunt ” commenced at Manly on Monday night,
as already announced, and very respectable busi-
ness was done in that eminently superior locality.
At time of writing the comedy was announced to
make its appearance at North Shore on Tuesday,
from which The Bulletin presumes that the
company successfully negotiated the boggy track
to the Spit, and succeeded in putting matters in a
favorable light to the ferryman. To-night (Thurs-
day) the show appears at Petersham, and as the
tramway arrangements are so wonderfully elabor-
ate that nobody from Petersham ever gets into
the city it ought to strike entirely new ground
thereabouts. Ashfield, a place where nothing
ever happens, except typhoid, is to be visited on
Friday; on Saturday the Aunt will make one
successive appearance at Wollongong ; and the
same estimable lady is due at Leichhardt on
Monday.
** * *
Woodloch’s Circus opened ab Redfern
(Sydney) on Thursday night to a vast audience,
and acquitted itself well. The Bulletin swore
off circuses some time ago as a species of enter-
tainment which only appeals to those who lightly
turn to thoughts of horse ; to a paper which
doesn’t yearn after horse, they are all very much
alike. Viewed from a serious and misanthropic
standpoint, however, Woodloeh’s is a remarkably
good circus. It contains nearly everything that
the writer has seen in every other circus, and as
the performers are mostly persons of much ability,
the results were all that any audience which is
fond of circuses could desire. The Bulletin’s
representative woke up frequently during the per-
formance, and every time he woke something good
was going on. The things which left a most
vivid impression on his mind were the gambling
pony, which won all its opponent’s money at
poker ; the house-breaking pony, which un-
fastened its hobbles with its teeth and then went
round and released its companions ; and the spec-
tacle of Rogers tearing round the ring on a ball
three feet in diameter, somewhat after the fashion
of Satan when he created sin by leaving his dirty
footmarks on the earth.
** * *
“ The Foundling,” which lately missed
its mark in Sydney, was played with much better
results at Melbourne Bijou on Saturday. No
company could make “The Foundling” appear
bright and original as regards dialogue and char-
acter drawing, but the present performance is for
the most part lively. Also it promised, on the
first night, to gather velocity as it went along and
achieve a passing reputation for simple naughti-
ness. This farce is at least consistent in its con-
tempt for realism. Each character in turn strikes
out an impossibly erratic line of conduct for him-
self or herself. When several lines cross one
another at a given point, they form a crazy situa-
tion, and a scene of wild disorder ensues. Upon
the spirit in which everybody rushes to a situation
and meets it when the point is reached, the laugh-
able absurdity or gloomy nonsense of “The
Foundling ” seems to depend.
« tt •
The Bijou people are sufficiently suc-
cessful, even though some of them chiefly succeed
in showing how their parts might have been
better played. Wilfrid Shine is the mitherless
bairn who stops out all night to oblige a lady, and
seeing that he has a voice in everything which
happens lie deserves much credit for keeping his
audience in good humor. All the same, Mr.
Shine is badly tailored, and much too rasping,
and a trick of dabbing: his physiognomy with a
colored handkerchief is very uncalled for. More-
over, be is slow in method as compared with,
say, Everard, the original young man of the
B. and B. company. Everard being a memory
of the past this comparison is not odious.
The Hucklebridge cad is a humorous inter-
loper in tiie important person of Elton, and
Miss Isabel Stuart, as the Tricky Maybud, is so
joyously comic at times that the extravagance of
her little ways can be forgiven. Besides, she
scores with a song and dance. John Forde and
Miss Clara Thompson will do better when they
tone themselves down. Miss Pelham and H. H.
Diver are well-placed in smaller capacities, and
the damsels, Miss Harrie Ireland and Miss Hardy,
again prove themselves a pair of well-contrasted
sunbeams. Sometimes Miss Ireland is called
upon to act, in addition to looking delicious, and
then she acts up to her appearance, which is say-
ing a good deal.

The run of “ The Foundling ” will be in-
terrupted next week, in any case. Lord Yar-
mouth has promised, under pressure, to give his
renowned entertainment at the Bijou for a season
of two consecutive nights—probably Thursday
and Friday—before he travels North. The un-
sympathetic tone of certain beastly newspapers is
not yet forgotten by the youthful earl, but in the
interests of his less fortunate fellow men he has
consented to appear again as aforesaid. For the
benefit of distressed actors and another local
charity the amiable Yarmouth will shake his legs,
and The Bulletin hopes he will derive as much
honest satisfaction from hH show as he will give
to the people who get the profits, which are likely
to be large.

He opened with Mrs. Lewis, and after a rocky

of the members of the Seraphina orchestra. This was
little David’s fate from the very moment when he and
Cope ptre undertook to preside over a bevy of highly
agreeable girls. One of them was bound to play upon the

It is now said, in all seriousness, that a recently-married
actress intends to sue for a divorce, on the ground of her
husband’s delicate health, or something to that effect.
The story doesn’t sound very true. But evidence that they
have ceased to form a united family is strong enough to
warrant the purfesh’s belief in a case to come.
Mary Anderson (Mdme. de Navarro) must look back
with regret upon the days when she thought she had
become “wedded to her art’’ and would never commit
bigamy by accepting a flesh-andblood hubby. Marriage,,
from the start, has been a failure, physically speaking,.
with beautiful Mary. At latest dates she lay dangerously
ill at her London residence, where she gave birth to a i
baby which lived only a few hours. For the past three or
four years, says an English paper, this once-envied actress <
has been a great sufferer.

Years ago, when the late W. H. Farnie was rehearsing
one of his adaptations of French comic opera, Joe Tapley,
Henry Braoy, and Arthur Roberts were late for rehearsal,
Roberts being, of course, the last to arrive. This much
annoyed Farnie, who said: “This is altogether too bad.
First Tapley is behind time, then Bracy comes in ten
minutes later, and now, Roberts, you crawl in third.”
“Dear, dear!” said Arthur, “and didn’t you back me
for a place, old man ?”—Ex.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
