The Wild-Cat Column.
The Melbourne Age now endorses the idea
which The Butletin advanced many months ago, and has
been advancing ever since, that the State should come to
the assistance of the reconstructed banks for a fair con-
sideration. It points out that the British Government
once issued *5,000,000 in State paper-money to assist a
crisis, ami made a profit out of the transaction, and that
the plan “ proved an enormous success.” Also, the
British Government has over and over again authorised
the Bank of England to pour out an almost unlimited
note supply foe the same purpose. “We are confronted
with the fact,” says the Age, “that between the years
1897 and 1901 the Australian reconstructed banks have to
meet demands amounting to £44,000,000,” and the fact
that they can’t settle the bill without State assistance is
tolerably obvious. But if there is to be State assistance
there should also be State supervision, and if the State ia
to give its valuable security then there should be a
reduction of interest, for State paper at 3 per cent, is
quite as good as hank paper at 4J, and 4J with a national
guarantee is simple extortion.
» * * *
The Commercial Bank of Australia’s balance-
sheet looks better in some respects than its predecessor,
and is probably about as good as any that the big bank
has produced since it started out in its own peculiarly
disastrous fashion to “restore confidence.” There is very
little to choose between it and the last one, and what
difference exists is partly for the belter and partly other-
wise. With £135,000 more capital the bank contrived to
earn just £62 more gross profit in the half-year, but by
hard economy expenses were so far reduced that the net
profit is £13,350, against £7900 for the previous six
months. The cash on hand has diminished by £300,000,
and now stands at £816,000; the note circulation has
increased a little, and the new deposits and current
accounts—which are, in a great degree, the measure of
public confidence—have dropped badly, from £1,772,000
to £1.492,000. The directors report that they have
“practically” paid off deposits falling due in 1898 to
the amount of £soo,ooo—not In cash, but by accept-
ing deposit-receipts in satisfaction of debts “ other-
wise doubtful,” from which it appears that the
C* mmercial of Australia only accepts its own securi-
tiis in settlement when it is dead sure that it can’t
get cash. There is no div., and the board has given up
the hollow pretence of apologising for its absence, which
is one good feature in the case, for the Commercial needs
to swear-off all divs. for five years, anyhow. As the whole
profits would only pay 15s. per cent, all round, the
advisability of saying nothing on this painful subject is
sufficiently apparent. The £53,000 which was fooled
away a year ago to keep up the pretence of paying a div.
on preference shares was the worst mistake this bank
ever made. Nothing is said, either, about releasing any
of the old fixed deposits, and no serious progress seems to
be made so far towards providing for the great mass of
liabilities which fall due in ’9B, ’99, and 1900. The interest
payable on deposits for the half-year is £17,000 less than
it was a year ago, but this relief produces no visible effect
on the profits. Also, the liquidation of the old bank gets
on rather slowly, there being still six-and-a-half millions
of its nominal assets which the new bank hasn’t taken
over because of their (at present) unsatisfactory condi-
tion. On the whole, things look, we are glad to see, a
little better than they did, but it is too soon to indulge in
any pleasant prophecies about Gyles Turner’s unwieldy
institution.
* « « «
The A. J.S. Bank proposes to pay off during the
current half-year one-half of that instalment of the recon-
structed deposits which falls due in June, ’97, on condi-
tion that the depositors grant a renewal of the other half
until 1902. In other words, it proposes to pay one-half
two years in advance on condition that the other half is
postponed for five years-which is equivalent to asking 18
months’ more time on the whole amount.
This either means that the A. J.S. is in rather a
oad way, or that it has a brainy set of directors who see
further ahead than most of their kind. In the first
instance it would simply mean that the bank couldn’t
meet the first batch of its engagements without further
concessions from its creditors, and nothing more, in which
case it might seem that the prospects of the other nine
instalments were none too bright. On the other hand, it
is already an admitted fact that very few of the recon-
structed banks can meet the immense mass of liabilities
falling due in ’9B, ’99, 1900 and 1901, and many of them,
when their heavy payments fall due, must ask for an ex-
tension. Therefore, it may be that the A.J.C. directors,
being far-sighted men, propose to pay a large proportion
of each instalment long before it falls due, as a reasonable
equivalent for an extension of the other half.
If this latter is the case the action is a wise one,
as they will certainly get better terms than those institu-
tions which have to ask for concessions at the last
moment without anything to offer in return. Every-
thing depends on whether the present move is part of a
well-ordered system, or merely a device to stave off the
first difficulty without any consideration for what lies
beyond, and as no independent authority can give a
definite answer to this question no reliable inference can
be drawn. A State audit would throw some light on the
matter, but nothing else seems likely to do so.
* r * * *
The Q.N. Bank brags that it has reduced its
tremendous debt to Q. Govt, by £1,163,650 since the 1893
collapse. But what has become of tins £1,163,650 ? Has
not the Govt, immediately handed all of it and a lot more
public money to the bank to take care of? If so, many
people would stigmatise the transaction as a mere piece of
ledger jugglery. For the hard fact is thatwhile the bank’s
indebtedness to the Govt, is being decreased, the
Govt, deposits in the bank are being steadily increased.
When the bank stopped payment, in May, 1893, it had
£2,442,000 of Govt, moneys in custody. On Dec. 31,
1894, the amount was increased to £3,600,000, including
£242,000 worth of Treasury notes. Thus the bank has
paid the Govt. £1,163,650, and the Govt, has handed
back to the bank about £2,321,000 ; and the bank is
just about £1,160,000 cash in pocket. So far, the re-
payments of the bank appear to have consisted in waiting
till the Government pays a sum of money into general
account, and then transferring part of it to the recon-
struction account and taking immense credit for present-
ing the province with a little of its own money. Mean-
while, the bank has charge of the money, or of the figures
which represent it, all the time, and “ gets there” with
the same old regularity.
♦ * ♦ *
The Bank of Victoria goes on the even foolish-
ness of its way as heretofore. For the half-year ending
31st Dec., ’93, it made a net profit of £31,600, and
“blewed” it all save £4OOO in paying a 5 per cent. div. to
preference and ordinary shareholders alike. For half-year
ending June, '94, it made a profit of £30,000, and spent it
all but £l6OO in the same fashion. Last half-year it earned
only £21,000, and spends it all but £6OO in paying 5 per
per cent, to preference and 2J per cent, to ordinary share-
holders. The profits are large considering ail the adverse
circumstances, and, if due allowances have been made for
possible losses, are evidence of fhe real soundness of the
bank ; but still these profits are obviously declining, and
the showy div. of last year can’t be kept up, and, in
any esse, a reconstructed institution with no reserve-
fund, a huge liability coming due in a very few years, its
pref rence shares at nearly 50 per cent, discount, and its
ordinary shares unsaleable, has no right to spend more
than half its profits at the most, in divs. The available
cash (£1,500,000) shows a substantial increase, and why
this enormous am unt of coin is kept on hand without
some attempt being made to release the first batch of
reconstructed deposits (due in June, 1898), and thus
reduce the htavy interest charge, passes comprehension.
The amount on hand is equal to all the possible immediate
liabilities twice over, and the fund is not only earning no
interest, but half of it is absolutely useless,

* * * *
In one reconstructed bank only one director is
a preference shareholder. All the others, while assuring
the public of the solidity of the institution, judiciously
drew out their cosh and dodged the coming calamity.
Every person interested in a reconstructed bank should
find out if the directors are preference shareholders. In
most oases, a director is either a preference shareholder or
a first-class liar.
* • • •
The Ballarat (Vio.) Banking Co. —about the
safest little institution in Australia—pays its usual div.
of 5£ per cent, on its little paid-up capital of £85,000, and
adds £l5OO to reserve, making it £48,500. As an instance
of what can be done by going slow, and keeping clear of
booms, and not hungering after big divs., the Ballarat
Bank is a good example to Australia in general.

The poor, little, anaemic Bankers’ Institute of
Australasia wears a worried look. Many of its ex-mem-
bers are away trapping the “ wild rabbee ” for the subur-
ban hawker, others are managing W.A. wild cat mines,
and still more are keeping their guinea subscription to
buy clothes with. The 8.1. of A. will elect people outside
the alleged “ profession ” as members, strictly in the
interests of its income account. Well, why not? The
trail of the boom is over us all.

The manager of a big Sydney book warehouse
traces a distinct connection between the hook-trade and
Treasury deficits. Books are the first things people leave
off buying in bad times. With his finger on the trade-
pulse he knows infallibly when the surplus is real and
when it is only cooked.

Dear Bulletin, —When Matt. Larkin, of Melb.,
was gaoled, the R.O. Church, to which he had given £5OO
or £lOOO, made a special effort and handed back the
amount to the liquidators of his estate. Now we have an
ex-magnate who granted himself an unsecured advance
(which was robbery pure and simple) from a now busted
bank and made it a gift to another church. Why does
not that church repay the amount ?—Unwashed.

As already intimated, the Balmain (Sydney) old
Steam Ferry Co. has decided to voluntarily wind up
under court inspection. The resolution to this effect will
be confirmed on 30th inst., after which date, it is under-
stood, the co. will cease operations. The directors’ report
is a most melancholy document; but it should be remem-
bered that they only took charge when the late directors
no longer possessed the confidence of the shareholders on
account of their obstinate blindness in failing to see the
trend of opinion among (he co.’s customers generally.
The losses for '93 and ’94 were £4049 10s. 2d. and
£4865 7s.—total, £8914 17s. 2d., or a net loss, after
deducting the '92 credit balance, of £6205 15s. Id.
The paid-up capital is £29,250, and liabilities such ns
debentures, deposits, and creditors come to £15,333
19s. 3d. Against this total of £44,583 19s. 3d., the co.’s
steamers are valued at £28,000 (apparently far too high),
whilst its other properties and sundry debtors bring its
complete assets to £38,378 4s. 2d. However, allowing for
depreciation, and granted can ful nursing of assets with
judicious liquidation, the co. ought to manage without
calL, and may, with luck in the sale of stermers and free-
hold, pay a few shillings in the pound to the unfortunate
shareholders—who are still liable for, say, another
£20,000.
* • ♦ ♦
For years past Maoriland’s exports have ex-
ceeded her imports to a greater extent than those of any
other province except Queensland. Here are the figures:
1888 excess of exports £2,911,355
1889 „ „ 3,955,714
1890 „ „ 3,480,581
1891 „ „ 2,521,310
1892 „ „ 2,628,839
1893 „ „ 2,667,206
It is estimated that Maoriland should export to the value
of £3,330,000 per annum more than she imports, in order
to meet her liabilities to outside creditors and absentees.
■Therefore, during six years in which she has been the one
bright example of economy in Australasia, she only
twice managed to meet her liabilities out of her own
resources. As an essay on the general hopelessness of
things this is worth thinking over.
* * * *
Hundreds of decent people, with good securities,
who would never dream of borrowing from an ordinary
money-lender, have been duped by a philanthropist who
advertises his anxiety to “financially help really desen ing
oases.” When the borrower gets a bit behind and feels
certain that this benefactor will not object to renewing,
he becomes shocked on learning that the philanthropist
has suddenly “ lost his all ; ” has had, indeed, to part
with those securities and bills attached to another party
who must be paid, as “ he is without the bowels of com-
passion.” All efforts to get any time from the “ holder ”
(who is really the philanthropist’s are futile,
and enormous profits are realised by the seizure of
securities that the borrower would never think of deposit-
ing for five or ten times the amount if borrowed from the
ordinary usurer.
• * * *
A N.S.W. bankrupt, the other day, gave as the
reason of his financial collapse his bad luck. He was a
caterer, and at almost every race-meeting he provided for
it rained oats and dogs. So unlucky was he in this
respect that whenever it was known he was the successful
tenderer for any booth, the rest were practically unsale-
able. What a treasure he would be to the clergy as a
pray-for-rain tipster 1

George Leake, M.P. for Albany, and for some
six years Westralian Crown Solicitor, has published a
letter re the Londonderry blocks jumping eases, charging
members of the W.A. Govt, with being interested and
insinuating crookedness. Premier Forrest denies the
imputation, and brother Alick(not In the Ministry), while
admitting his interest, says he was absolutely indifferent
to the result, as, being interested on both sides, he w as
bound to win anyhow. Well, the present writer happens
to know the extent of brother “ Aliok’s” interest on the
side of the original owners. So, if Alick F. will clearly
state his financial obligations on the side of the jumpers
—which, in justice to himself and his affectionate brother,
he should at once do—people will be able to size up
things.
♦ * * ♦
The trouble is nob over yet. Whether interested
or not the W.A. Govt, committed the outrage of support-
ing the jumpers by passing an Act in their favor and
making it retrospective so as to annul existing actions.
That deed will stick to the Forrest Ministry like pitch.
The Premier admits that in dealing with the Londonderry
jump the Cabinet was reduced by three, including the
Minister of Mines.
♦ * * ♦
Little Fitzgerald, one of the first at Kurnalpi,
is said to be coming into Coolgardie, from the North-
West, with “a ton of specimens as rich as the London-
derry’s.” He left Coolgardie, eight months ago, with
several camels and nine months’ rations, and told the
writer he would not be heard of again till he had found
something worth while. Fitzgerald is a camel-man, a
born desert-country prospector. He can go without
water and food for a day or two, and then make a com-
fortable meal of a snake or an iguana.
» » * *
W. A. Mercer, of Bewick, Moreing & Co., a
fashionable London firm of mining engineers whose name
is seen on every W.A, prospectus in the Big Smoke, has
come to Sydney, apparently to push the Coolgardie boom
along. He starts with: “There is a great future before
Coolgardie.” Amongst other things, he proposes to build
a 1000-head battery on the Swan and run a number of
subsidiary railways to feed the battery with ore from the
Coolgardie fields, some 300 miles away. This was con-
tractor Millar’s idea, so Mercer has merely revived a
scheme which will never be carried out. It is cheaper to
talk of a 1000 - head stamp mill than it is to
put up one of 10 heads. Mr. Mercer admits
that the mines are undeveloped; hence it is too soon
for the erection of great stamp mills. He also talks of
the difficulty of salt-water in amalgamation and of the
necessity of a thorough system of recovering gold from
the ore. The great want is more gold— there is no trouble
about saving it. In 99 cases out of 100 the gold is rough
or absent. Some months ago Moreing, of Bewick, More-
ing and Co., was reported to have declared, and repeated
in London, that there were 2ft. retfs at Coolgardie half
solid gold ; and this junk of heavy boom talk has since
been reprinted on a dozen Coolgardie prospectuses. Per-
haps Mr. Mercer can-explain how his friend came by that
gigantic story.

The S.M. Herald keeps up its end of the Oool-
gardie log. Its special correspondent, in his final article
on Hannan’s, starts at one end of the line and follows it
for miles, describing every lease as valuable, and every
reef rich. The first, the Star of the West, he says, will go
3oz. Well, the writer has seen it, and happens to know
that it has been condemned by several people because it
does not promise loz. average. Of the Croesus, the
S.M.H. man says it will go 4oz. (shareholders expect 10),
and, of Lake View, that the crushing then commenced
would go 6 to 9oz. It went a little over 2oz. ! He says of
the Golden Eagle (White Feather): “This is another
very rich property.” It is not thought so locally. “The
Nrraesis was sold for £3OOO and l-6th, hut if the bargain
was made after a certain shot was fired the price would
have been £lO,OOO ; that is how syndicates get hold of
rich properties.” That is how special correspondents
bite the ear of the S.M. Herald.

Hitherto it has been supposed that only £12,000
gold has been taken from the alleged Wealth of Nations
mine. A recent interview with prospector Dunn gives
another version. “ The first camel’s load I took in con-
sisted of about £B5OO worth of quartz gold, and the second
£13,000. The total value of the stuff brought in was
£22.000, and it includ'd one stone which weighed 1591 h.,
and contained about S00"z. of gold. We have npf ned the
lode up in three different places for a length of 12 chains
along the outcrop. One of the shafts is now down at 83ft.,
showing a well-defined lode 9ft. thick from wall to wall,
and carrying splendid gold all through. All the author-
ities w ho have examined the ground agree that the Wealth
of Nations is one of the best-defined lodes in Australia,
and the geological features are very favorable for a con-
tinuance of it to—any depth. It is schistose and diorilio
rock, striking north and south, and underlying west.”
Then Mr. Dunn went on to talk about the, “very good
property which he called the Brilliant” and sold to the
Messrs. Millar. Mr. Dunn apparently forgot to say what
happened to the Brilliant.
* * * *
Dunn, the Wealth of Nations hero, says he
returns soon to some “new thing,” as yet only seen by
himself and his camel. After what the writer hears of
the Wealth of Nations, judgment is suspended until the
camel has also been intervii wed.

The glory of Bayley’s has departed at least tem-
porarily. Announced that in future divs. will be paid
quarterly instead of monthly. Why? Because of the
want of gold. Shares dropped immediately to 11s. But
tor London, where there are nearly 2000 holders, they
would be down to 65., which is a little over their real
value.
* * * *
Fortune of War, Murchison, in trouble again.
This time she will hang up for a good while—the boiler is
burnt. It is not to be repaired on the field, but is to be
dealt with by men from the Melbourne foundry which
made it. It needs a Mt. Morgan to stand that sort of
thing. Terrible difficulty is experienced with the salt-
water. The salt formed on the boiler-shell is “asolid rake
Sin. thick and as hard as granite.”

♦ * • «
Efforts are being made to again start Parker’s
United, Gordons (Vio.), over which many thousands have
been spent, many fortunes made and lost—the two
opposite results being due to the fact that the stock has
for the past ten years been a Ballarat Exchange gamble.
For instance, a week before the old co. was sold up by
the London bank were run from 4d. to Is. 4d.,
and this sort of thing always occurred when the bulls and
bears had little to do. One of the weaknesses of
Parker’s lay in the weekly reports, which were con-
sistent in that each week saw a flat denial of that of the
preceding one. For three years, according to these
reports, the co. has only been 50 feet off gold, and this is
the main argument in the new prospectus. Strange to
say, the shares are being quickly taken up.
* * * *
Jeweller’s holes are still to be found near Balla-
rat. An old miner of 70 years admits having obtained
£l9OO in gold during the last six months. A grandson
was his mate. The gold was sold at five banks to arrest
suspicion. Twelve months ago a private subscription was
taken up for the old digger.

The majority of Steiglitz (Vic.) legal managers
appear to have spent their Xmas holidays in preparing
bills of-sale over the various properties in favor of sundry
banks. Strange that the whole of the co.’s we have heard
so much about should have hit upon the same idea
simultaneously.
*♦' * *
The sensation of the week on Ballarat ’Change
has been the persistent rise in the shares of Band and
Albion—one of the world’s greatest mines. The co.’s
original capital (£499,000) has been paid up, and a meeting
is now about to be held to increase it. A decision this
way is likely to be unanimous, as there are strong indi-
cations of an alluvial revival in the last shaft. The Band
has produced about three millions worth of gold and paid
one and a-half millions in divs.
* * * •
The Hetton colliery (Newcastle) is one of the
shallowest in the northern district. The workings are
for the most part under fhe harbor, and the miners at
work can distinctly hear the pulse of the ferry-boats
passing overhead. When a ship drops her anchor over
the mine (he thud on the bottom echoes through the
workings. Effective use of the fact was made during the
late struggle for the purpose of dissuading the “scabs,”
most of them inexperienced in mining, from accepting
work from the management. A unionist disguising him-
self joined in with the blacklegs, and casually dropped
remarks about the thinness of the roof and its consequent
dangers, with the result that the majority abandoned all
idea of working in the place.

In a Gympie street there lay, until last week, a

on dark rights. Last week a man, who had probably
read Mark Twain, picked up that boulder and got out of
it about £6O worth of gold.

There are versions of the wherefore of the
appointment of Mr. Alex. Stewart to the assistant-general
managership of the Proprietary which do not agree with
the directorial pronouncement. One of these connects
director Knox with -’a precipitate appointment to
the Mt. Lyell managership, ratification of which was pre-
vented by the subsequent obstinate refusal of that show
to boom. Color is lent to the story by the practical cer-
tainty that had the Mt. Lyell borm not jibbed at the
critical point, Mr. Stewart, as manager of the Tharsis
(Spain), would have got the billet. But Mt. Lyell may
boom heavily yet.

The prospectors’ claim at Struck Oil (Mb.
Morgan) continues to do well. In Nov. last 43 tons were
treated for 168 oz. Altogether there have been treated 98
tons for 4540 z. at £3 4s. 9d. per ounce. Proportionate
expenses, however, are somewhat heavy—battery at
Crocodile claiming £2 ss. per oz. It is claimed there is
stuff enough in sight to give two years’ work at least.

Said mine has been at work for 14 years, and produced
nothing but earth and rock, and £56,000 worth of calls.
The Vic. shareholder is an indomitable animal.

CLEMENTS TONIC.
The greatest renovator of a worn-out system
the world has ever seen, positively and per-
manently restores manly virility, to prove
which evidence is forthcoming from any quarter.
READ THIS CASE.
Mr. A. E, Barnes, Albury-street, Murrumburrah,
N.S.W., who writes on June 13th, 1893 I have
been subjected to indigestion for over twelve
months. Symptoms were headaches, w r ant of appe-
tite, a drowsy feeling, especially a few hours after
meals, and a heavy feeling on the chest, also a
want of energy. Hearing so much of Clements
Tonic as an invigorator and nerve strengthener, I
tried it, and am happy to say that in a very short
time all disagreeable symptoms vanished as if by
magic. I have never had a recurrence of the old
disagreeable feelings since. I am only too happy
to send tin's short testimony as to the great cura-
tive properties of Clements Tonic. Not alone
have I been benefited myself by the use of Clem-
ents Tonic, but a number of my friends have been
using it for some time with life great st success.
I recommend Clements Tonic <>n all occasions.—I
remain, yours truly, A. E. Barnes, storekeeper,
Albury-st., Murrumburrah.

and Observer, rather likes simultaneous occu-
pancy of a pair of chairs. When in Sydney, he

hadn’t enough to do.
The death of the “Hon, Went-
worth 0 a venagh-Main waring,” of
“ Whitby Hall, Staffordshire,” is cabled.
Formerly he was plain W. Cavenagh,
and was a land-agent in Adelaide, but his
wife succeeded to the Mainwaring estate,
worth about =O5OOO a year, owing to her
four brothers dying without issue.
Cavanagh was a mediocre person, and
at one time sat in S.A. Assembly and
was Commissioner of Crown Lands. The
present heir to the estate, J. G. Main-
waring, is an Adelaide solicitor, and
awhile ago made a sensation by contest-
ing Yatala for S.A. Assembly as the son
of his father, polling about 30 votes.
I was reminded of the flight of time
(writes an Australian in England to This
Bulletin) by seeing the other day two of
the sons of the late W. B. Dailey, one of
whom is studying for the Bar, and the
other coaching for the army. They are
rather small in stature, but possess some
of the “ ginger ” for which their father
was noted in his early career. They are
dare-devil horsemen and good drivers,
and were lately summoned for furious
riding in the Park ; but with W. B, D.’s
plausibility they managed to get off with
a caution—the policeman having des-
cribed them as “ nice, civil-spoken young
gentlemen.”
Grainger, M.P. |(S.A.), tells this
story against himself. When a young
man he had long flowing curls and
thought himself an Adonis. He was
bathing in the Thames one day when he
suddenly caught the sound of feminine
laughter. Ashe was in shallow water, he
rapidly bent down so that only an eye,
a nostril, and some ringlets were above
the surface. A boat, in which sat two ladies
and two gentlemen, drifted lazily past, and one
fair damsel, pointing to his floating curls, cried
out: “Oh, look at the nasty dead puppy. Pull
away quick!”

Miss RAY JONES —“ Cinderella ,” at Sydney
Lyceum.
mail, has just succeeded in kindling the ashes of
one of the oldest Bohemian clubs in London, the
“Vagabonds.” The first dinner under the new
regime was held last week, and brought together
a most wonderful array of novelists, artists and
journalists. F. W. Robinson was the chairman,
and Frankfort Moore the honored guest of the
evening. Ho made a most chirpy speech. Amongst
other things he defined a novelist as being “ a man
who tried to live by writing books that won’t.”
The pocket chronometer worn by Nelson
at the battle of Trafalgar is said now to belong to
Mr. Redmond, of Sydney, who claims blood-
relationship with the celebrated Admiral. It is
open-faced, with a silver-gilt case, and about twice
the size of the ordinary modern watch. The inner
works —cog-wheels, plates, &c. —are of brass,
heavily gilded, and, with the exception of the
main-spring (a quarter-inch broad), the works are
well preserved. Its maker is Arnold, of London,
and, great as is its age, and although it has not
gone for 21 years, a scientific amateur, a friend of
its owner, very easily succeeded in making it
again keep time.
FREETRADE MURRAY SMITH.
The forlorn hope of a [?] cause.
Mistah Pursey Chirnside delivered his
maiden speech in Vic. Assembly, on Wednesday
night week. He was beautifully arrayed for the
occasion, and had quite an array of pretty women
—including the divinely-fair Mrs. Percy—in the
gallery to hear the oration. The subject was the
reduction of the municipal subsidy. Specimen :
“ In mai constituency there is a great twaffic on
the—ah—er—woads, end if this wetwenchment—
er—is cawwied out—ah—we shall not be able to
wepair the woads, and that will be a dweadful—
er—state of things.” Pursey has a thin, gentle,
claret-and-lemonade voice, and a parliamentary
manner suggestive of an imminent intention to
ask the Premier if he may have the pleashah of the
—ah—next darnse.
Ivo Bligh, the captain of the English
cricket-team which visited Australia ten years
back, had a unique experience on his wedding-
night. He and his handsome bride, Miss Florrie
Morphy, went to a picturesque cottage on the side
of Mt. Macedon for their honeymoon, and, after
dinner, Ivo strolled out into the bush for a cigar.
His smoke finished, he started back, but, after
half-an-hour or so of an endless procession of gum-
trees, he was forced into admitting that he was
lost. He then made desperate endeavors to find
his way back, but only got further and further
into the bush. He had no matches to light a fire,
and all his hollering for help failed to bring assis-
tance. The upshot was that he had to sleep in the
scrub like a big babe in the wood, until the morn-
ing light enabled him to locate his residence, and
join his disconsolate and weeping bride.
The late ex-N.S. W. Chief Justice Alfred
Stephen, whose will has been probated for .£15,630,
was, like Parkes and Barton, very fond of the
phrase “For I am a poor man.” Journalist
Harold Stephen, who when he had thirty shillings
in his pocket felt as well as the Mikado, once
wrote to his uncle in reprobation of this habit.
“If you are poor,” wrote Harold, “keep it to
yourself. Hide it all you can. If I let the world
know exactly how I stand, I’d get nothing and be
despised besides. And, whatever you may say
about your own poverty, I beg you, when talking
to Ministers of the Crown about me, not to say I
am poor. The first thing asked when a man enters
Parliament is : ‘ Has he much stuff ?’ If they
think he has, Ministers and fellow-members go
out of their way to serve him ; if he hasn’t, they
leave him alone.” Alas, these were the days of
non-payment of members.
Mr. George Mayo, F.R.0.5., who died in
Adelaide the week before Christmas, had watched
the progress of South Australia almost from its
establishment. He was born at Seend, in Wilt-
shire, on January Bth, 1807; became a member
of the Royal College of Surgeons in 1829; and
reached Adelaide in the Lady Emma in 1837, a
year after Colonel Light and the first surveyors
arrived there. He returned to England, but finally
settled in the province in 1839; living and
dying on one of the original town acres. Mr.
Mayo did some volunteering in his middle life, and
retired as Lieutenant-Colonel from the command
of the Adelaide regiment in 1865. He took to
bicycling with enthusiasm; but when he reached
80, he added a third wheel, and, within a year of
his death, negotiated a 12-mile ride before break-
fast on the tricycle. A sturdy, sensible, old
fellow ; a quiet, peaceful, useful life.

Felix Faure, the new French President,
seems to be a moderate man whom nobody very
much likes and nobody very much dislikes—which
ia the recommendation of a weak character rather
than of a strong one. He is the third 1 rench

The young N.S.W. barrister, J. de Lacy
O’Reilly, who recently died at the early age of 28,
was, some time ago, a frequent and esteemed con-
tributor to The Bulletin over the pen-name
of “A. C. Fontaine.” He was formerly a pupil-
teacher in a N.S. W. State-school, and from that
humble position he qualified for the bar by his
own unaided efforts, and was admitted to practise
only one month before his lamented decease.
The official documents forwarded to the
Swedish consuls in Australia by last mail from
Stockholm show that little Norway with its two
millions of people would welcome a row. Nor-
way and Sweden are the typical examples of
Home Rule, each country having a Parliament,
but both Parliaments being under one king. But
even there the demon of discord appears to be
getting in his work. The animosity revealed in
the instructions lately received (which, indeed,
though emanating from the Swedish throne, were
written in French) looks, at this end, to have a
very small base, the consuls being simply informed
that their official title is “ Consul of Sweden and
Norway,” and that when Norwegian authorities
write to them as “Consuls of Norway and
Sweden,” they are to manifest their allegiance by
eturning the letters unread to Norway.
It is stated that a Chinese Hill End
(N.S.W.) doctor has successfully treated a case of
mouth-cancer. “Some months ago, an enginedriver,
after having a tooth drawn, developed a cancerous
growth which ultimately destroyed his palate and
spread over the right side of his face. He was
treated by medical men both locally and in
Bathurst, without receiving relief, and was admit-
ted into the Alfred Hospital, Sydney, in October.
He suffered terrible agony, and as nothing could
be done to help him, he was advised to return to
Hill End. There the Chinese doctor visited him
and applied a poultice to his face. Thereafter he
suffered no pain, a hard core has come away
through the roof of the mouth, and now the flesh
on the inside of the mouth seems to be healing,
and the flesh on the face looks healthier. The
patient eats heartily, though with difficulty, and
keeps in good spirits. He has of late taken a
short walk daily, and is so rapidly gaining
strength that he hopes co be at work before the
end of the month.” It will be interesting to note
the future history of this case. The Alfred Hos-
pital authorities say that the poor man was un-
doubtedly suffering from cancer —and add that if
he lives two years they are prepared to make
obeisance to the Chinaman.

GALLAWAY, the N.S.W. Cricketer .
Ed. Bulletin, —The correspondent who
refers to “ The Song of Father Duchesne ” quotes
some lines of a song which he heard in Tasmania
years ago. The lines he quotes are from the old-
time song of ‘‘John Hall, Chimney-Sweep,” which
details the sayings and doings of one John Hall,
condemned to death in the days of Jonathan
Wild. It is generally supposed to be the song
which gave such offence to Colonel Newoome (in
the first chapter of Thackeray’s book).—A. 8.,
Waverley.


“MAN WANTS BUT LITTLE, NOR THAT
LITTLE LONG.”
It was the above clever saying of Edward Young,
which brought about the conception of Erossard’s
Ca vour Cigars. It is indeed better to have a small
and good cigar—the packet of eight is sold for one
shilling—to enjoy its mild aromatic smoke, and
have done with it, than to puff away at a thing
like a telegraph pole, where you have quantity
instead of quality, and pay three or four times as
much.

H, H. Champion’s “social story,” running
on from Saturday to Saturday in Melbourne
Herald, is neither more nor less than a declara-
tion of the socialist faith by Champion to a small
circle of assorted critics. It explains away a good
many vague Tory objections; and ought to sell
hereafter in sixpenny book-form.
The Queenslander solemnly warns the
planters' to beware of the Jap, The Hawaiian
Gazette complains bitterly that the Sandwich
Japs continually strike and desert. “Dema-
gogic agitations are common among them, and the
only safeguard our planters have is not to rely ex-
clusively on any one class of alien labour.”
In 1881, for every 10,000 native-born
Victorians of the age of 21 and upwards, 550 were
arrested. In 1891 the proportion was reduced to
39(5 for each 10,000. Hayter should not be allowed
to publish statistics like this. No official should
be allowed to state facts that bring down con-
tempt on one of the pet theories of the churches.
Reid, the softgoodsman, made the best
sp ech delivered in Vic. Upper House against the
land-tax during the recent debate. The fact that
it was the best is interesting as showing how
melancholy were the rest of the orations. Reid’s
deliverance consisted of one wild, irrelevant, dis-
connected whoop about the greatness and glory of
England, with a peroration concerning the Union
Jack.
The Eddy (N.S.W. Chief Railway Com-
missioner) resignation evolves the following
alternatives : (1) Double Eddy’s screw and keep
him. (2) Let him go and appoint Oliver. (3) Let
him go and appoint Fehon. (4) Let him go and
appoint Goodchap. (5) Let all of them go and
appoint Goodchap. As the country will, if Good-
chap be appointed, be able to squash his pension
(*B6O a year) agreement this last solution awakes
a host of eager supporters. The N.S.W. railways
and tramways cost =£36,000,000, and what the
country appears to be most ly worried about is how
to get the cheapest possible man to muddle this
gigantic property into chaos.

The grim and grisly Patterson has been of
late so agreeable to the Turner Government that
people would like to know what billet the ex-
butcher is after, or what mischief he is concocting.
A Victorian Government is known chiefly by the
way J. B. Patterson objects to or approves of it.
Some people are urging Patterson’s claims to
railway-commissionship.
Can it really be true that N.S.W. Works
Minister Young lias been guilty of the gross in-
decency of retaining his own son in a permanent
position in his department while dismissing many
married men longer in the service ? If it is true,
then the merits of Mr. Young’s son are quite
beside the question, for no man can be a com-
petent judge in his son’s cause.
Ministers of the Crown have practically
carte blanche for travelling expenses when away
from the metropolis, and with some past minis-
terial excursions in mind it will be worth while to
ask the amount of N.S.W. Minister Garrard’s
bill when he returns from Maoriland. The infor-
mation he seeks could have been collected under a
shilling’s-worth of postage-stamps.
The Govt, arrangement with Chief-Justice
Way is that he is to act as Governor for .£5OOO a
year (the salary drawn by Lord Kintorejand fore-
go his judicial stipend of £2OOO. Many South
Australians think that they have now seen the
last of the imported vicegerents. Adding together
his different spells of office, Way has now
“ Governed ” South Australia for over two years.
Moral ?
Those members of Vic. Assembly who
loyally opposed the reduction of Governors’
salaries, because the community would suffer “in
consequence of the diminished circulation of
wealth,” were all eager enemies of the minimum-
wage, and delighted in brandishing the knife that
cut down the seven-bob a-day State servants. The
circulation of wealth is a splendid thing—when
I atman is made the circulating medium.
Some weeks ago the owners of the Zig-Zag
colliery, Lithgow (N.S.W.), issued an order to
their men to work on Sunday. Eighteen of them
did so, and were promptly charged with breaking
the good old Sabbath of Charles 11. If they had
refused to work they would possibly have been
“ sacked.” As they did work they were fined.
The owners who compelled them to break the law
weren’t lined—in fact, they weren’t even sum-
moned.
Compare Australian and French judicial
salaries. The President of the Court of Cassation,
the head of the French judiciary, receives 30,000
francs (£l2OO a year), the remuneration of the
other Judges of the Court varying, according to
rank, from £lOOO to £720. The President of the
Court of Appeal receives £lOOO a year, and the
other members from £550 to £440. Appeal
Judges in the provinces, £2OO. Magistrates in
Paris receive £320, and in the country from £2OO
to £72 per annum.
Payment of Members, in Victoria, was
precipitated by the Quieting of Titles Bill. This
measure secured a lot of shaky squatting titles to
land. A heap of members were bribed, as was
shown before a committee. One member, accused of
being instrumental as briber, was expelled; another
cautioned by Mr. Speaker. Three squatting
agents were put in gaol by Parliament, and
liberated by the Judges; but the Privy Council,
on appeal, upheld Parliament. Nevertheless, the
offenders were not again “lumbered.”

Sydney Daily Telegraph, arguing for
reductions in the salaries of Australian Governors,
seeks to efface the word salary altogether, and
makes it appear that the allowances of the past
and present were voted merely to meet incidental
expenses. _ Why is it that professing democrats
are so anxious to call by some other name what is
truly salary or wages ? When the bill for payment
of members was brought into the N.S.W. House,
one Radical after another declared the sum set
down was not to be considered payment for par-
liamentary services, but a sum towards meeting
expenses incurred, and the Act still carries some
such roundabout caption. The State should know
only a man’s work or his needs. If he needs, it is
his helper ; if he works, it owes him wages ; if he
neither needs nor works, it doesn’t notice him.

“ Steele Grey ” writes thus ;
Dear Bulletin,— When, a few weeks back, I sum-
marised in your columns the present state of knowledge
in re a possible antidote for snake-poison, I had no desire
to raise the wrath of the apostle of the strychnine treat-
ment. As, however, Dr. Mueller has chosen to attack me
in Tub Bulletin, and to become offensively personal, I
claim the right to reply. (I) Does snake-poison act upon
the blood only, on the nervous system only, or on both ?
Dr. Mueller says: “It is no blood-poison at all," by
which, I presume, he means that it acts on the nervous
system only. But, amongst other observers, Weir-
Mitchell, Reichert, and Brainard, in America, Fayrer, in
India, Halford, in Melbourne, and Martin, in Sydney, all
note that in most fatal oases of snakebite the blood
remains fluid after death instead of coagulating. Does
not this point to a chemical change in the blood due to
the venom ? Also, Dr. George Harley, of London, after a
post-mortem on a dog bitten by an African puff-
adder, says, “everything pointed to blood-poisonin.'.”
Dr. Martin, of Sydney, likewise remarks, “some of the
haemoglobin is dissolved in the plasma.” Moreover,
Dr. Mueller himself (on Snake-Poison, p. 23) states that
the blood becomes surcharged with carbonic acid, loses
its haemoglobin, and is thin and dark after death. All
this evidence proves that snake-poison does act on the
blood. (2) That snake-poison affects the nerve centres is
also perfectly true, but, to use the words of J. Arthur
Thomson, of Edinburgh, “snake-poison acts through the
circulation on the nerve centres, and also affects the
blood itself." (3) Dr. Mueller derisively queries, “ Who
ever heard of snake-poison, when injected into the blood-
stream, causing blood-clots to form, that fill up the cham-
bers of the heart?” Well, I, for one, have seen intravas-
cular clotting ; but, since Dr. Mueller maj' think ray
evidence worthless, I will quote others. Brainard, in 1854,
wrote: “ Wtien death occurred immediately in animals
bitten by rattlesnakes, the blood was found at the post-
mortem. examination to be clotted.” This was confirmed
by Weir-Mitchell in 1860. Here also are a few sentences
from a paper by Dr. Martin, of Sydney University, in vol.
XV. of the Journal of Physiology, on the effects of poison
of the Australian black snake: —“ In my previous experi-
ence with rats, rabbits, guineapigs, and dogs, the blood
post mortem was always coagulated. . . Immediately
after the introduction of the venom, coagulability in-
creases, and culminates in intravascular clotting of
greater or less extent. . . Intravascular clothing is
produced most readily if the dose be rapidly thrown into
a vein near the heart . . . the blood in the left ven-
tricle and aorta being solid. . . The moment of clot-
ting in the arteries is indicated on the tracing by the dis-
appearance of the heart-beats. . . The same dose [of
tissue-fibrinogen] given to an animal in full digestion pro-
duced clotting extending to the general venous system,
the right heart, and even into the arteries: I have shown
reason for believing that the blood of animals in full
digestion exhibits the same increased sensitiveness to
the venom.” All of which means that snake poison
can produce dotting in the venous system and
heart, thereby producing heart-stoppage. (A) All the
above evidence justifies my contentions— (a) that snake-
poison may kill by stoppage of the heart's action either
by paralysis of the nerve centres or from clotting of
blood in the heart and vessels ; (b) that, since venom not
directly injected into the blood stream acts chiefly on the
nervous system, in such oases a nerve-stimulant like
Mueller’s strychnine would be appropriate treatment ;
(c) that, since venom injected directly into the blood-
stream acts chemically on the blood, in such cases a
chemical antagonist like Calmette’s chloride of lime
would be appropriate treatment; (d) that, since in any
given case of snakebite it would be practically impossible
to say whether the venom was injected directly into the
blood-stream or not, the best thing to do would be to use
both strychnine and chloride of lime. (5) Coming now to
Dr. Mueller’s personalities. As to my “ bits of knowledge
picked up promiscuously,” the worthy doctor isatfault.for
I have not only read a good deal on the subject, but have
also been an eye-witness of many experiments on rabbits
and dogs both by Dr. Martin, of Sydney, and Professor
Halford, of Melbourne. As to my being “jealous of the
success of the strychnine treatment," why should I he
jealous? It is just this, Dr. Mueller claims that strych-
nine is a universal antidote to snake-poison, but this is
denied by the majority of scientists, ( a) because strychnine
is only a nerve-stimulant, and could not counteract the
chemical action on the blood ; (6) because Dr. Mueller’s
reasoning is not logically sound, being open to the accusa-
tion of post hoc ergo propter hoc (or, as Dr. Mueller doesn’t
like ray ‘ ‘bits of Latin,” let me rather say the fallacy known
as “false cause’’); (c) because Dr. Mueller has not proved his
case scientifically, his conclusion being based on clinical
observation and not sufficiently verified by experimental
research. As to his assertion that lam mentally asinine,
1 take leave to point out that the apostle of strychnine is
passing a hasty ex pat te judgment on a man whom he
has never met and of whose identity he is entirely
ignorant ; also, that I am quite as well justified in agree-
ing with such men of light and leading as Weir-Mitchell,
Reichert, Brainard, Fayrer, Harley, Thomson, Calmette,
Halford, and Martin as I would be in adopting the “ not*
proven ” theory of, to use Dr. Mueller’s own description
of himself on page 79 of his hook, “ an obscure Australian
country practitioner.” — Yours truly, Steele Grev.

During the days when the remains of the
late Czar lay in state, 180,000 persons filed past
the corpse and kissed its clammy hand. Pity
the poor osculators at the tail of the procession !
But possibly it was a case of kissing or Siberia.
Mr. Joseph Evison, equally well-known in
Maoriland as a lecturer and as a pressman, has
arrived in Sydney with a view to settling in Aus-
tralia. As a humorist, Mr. Evison had no rival
on the M.L. press; his capabilities as a story-
teller may be judged from “Tommy Armstrong,”
lately printed in The Bulletin.
Italian lemons compete so successfully
with Australian that an attempt is actually being
made to open a Sydney trade for Italian oranges.
The Australian fruit-grower goes far too much by
rule of thumb. He sticks his trees in anywhere,
rarely cares for them, pulls the fruit in a hurry,
packs it clumsily, sends it to market at the wrong
time, and complains because people prefer a
foreign article, grown and marketed with brains.

The first public airing Vic. of trousered
women, which took place in recently, did not create
a very favorable impression owing to the general
bony and unshapely aspect of the legs revealed,
and the palpable home-made atmosphere which
hung around the bifurcated The suc-
cess of this dress-evolution depends entirely on the
choice of pretty, well-built pioneers with limbs of
stately grace. It is a bad thing, by the way, for
the bloomer brigade that Dudley Hardy’s dashing
and very feminine picture “Oh that Girl!” has
been so extensively-circulated in Australia. Men
mostly like their girls in that shape and dress.
However, many Melb. women are keen to
substitute baggy breeches for cumbrous skirts.
The originator of the recent “rational-dress”
picnic to Fernshawe declares that directly he
mentioned the matter to his female friends they
accepted the notion enthusiastically. Since then
he has been besieged by “revolting’ maidens,
who want to show their legs at the Easter picnic,
which, it is expected, will be attended by at least
thirty rationalised females. Dressmakers are
anticipating the craze by advertising patterns of
the double-barrelled garment in all sorts, shapes
and sizes.
* # *
The Girl of Yarmouth, who, according to
a London paper, has come here “to acquire a
larger acquaintance of men and manners than is
obtainable in the militia or in London ballrooms,”
has put up at Squatter Armytage’s Toorak man-
sion, which is now the converging point of gush-
ing Melb. femininity. The other day lie arranged
a ladies’ cricket-match. The moneyed maidens
rolled up in hordes, in anticipation of seeing the
youthful earl display his noble legs under a
shimmer of petticoat-lace, but expectations were
sadly dashed when the titled visitor went on to
bowl (left-handed) in ordinary every-day cricket
breeks.
• * *
The collapse of the Australian Eleven on
New Year’s Day is now said to be at least par-
tially connected with a certain promise made by
them to see the old year out at the ancestral home
of a fellow-player. They failed to appear, but
about 5 a.m. next morning, the cook, who had
cried herself to sleep at the thought of a fatted
calf and trimmings overdone to tinder, was
awakened by a belated chorus of “ We won t go
home.”
The experiences of a
milk-condensing company
in Illawarra (N.S.W.) dis-
trict form cheerful reading.
The manager talks of sus-
pending operations because
the dairymen cannot be
induced to supply clean
milk on any consideration.
“We find fowls’ droppings
in the cans,” he sadly re-
marks ; “ also corn, and all
sorts of rubbish.” Then he
tells a weird tale of a
farmer who puttied the
holes in his milk-tins with
soap, and, on being remon-
strated with, told the ob-
jector that he was fat too
particular for Illawarra.
There are also histories of
“ manure sinks,” from
which the cattle drink in
dry seasons; and, on the
whole, it is made pretty
clear that the sooner that
pending discovery of science
is consummated, and milk
is made direct from grass
without the intervention
of either the cow or her
proprietor, the better it
will be for the wretched
consumer of the unmen-
tionable abominations of
the milk-can.
• *
In a certain Southern
district the mortality
among children suffering
from, say, diphtheria, be-
came alarmingly great.
One doctor, the leading
local practitioner, attended
95 per cent, of the cases.
The increasing loss of life
aroused suspicion as to his
method of treatment; and
the medico, without any
publicly - assigned reason,
ceased practising. Shortly
afterwards he died ; and it
leaked out that for 18
months previously he had
been insane, and had
treated his diphtheria
patients on a new and
original plan evolved out
of his insanity—hence the
funerals.
The alleged seduction, under peculiarly
Lusty circumstances, of a Sydney society lady by
the usual dashing militaire has formed the subject
of a military enquiry on which Gov. Duff will
decide the action to be taken. And now a naval
hero (some say, however, that the Lothario
belongs to the mercantile marine) is alleged to
have eloped with a well-known Sydney lady very
much his senior. Nothing like “ a well-considered
system of national defence ” as a school for society
scandal.
• • #
Complaints are still rife re the alleged local
feminine fondness for the English cricketing
colours. After all, it seems that the so-called alien
badge is a combination of the M.C.C. and N.S.W.
Association colours, adopted by the visitors as a
compliment to those who brought them out.
• • •
Because the Chief Justice insisted on
taking the chair at the citizens’ farewell to Gov.
Kintore in the Adelaide Town Hall, on Jan. 14,
the Mayor and all the Corporation stayed away.
To save expense the militia were not called out,
but the escort and guard of honor were formed of
policemen, some of whom had been on duty the
whole of the night before. There was no extra
pay.

The Braw Kintore leaves South Australia a somewhat
sadder man than he entered it. In one of his first
speeches he declared himself anxious to govern “ bis
people ” with discretion, and the discovery that he had
no people there except the wife and family who accom-
panied him from Home was a blow from which the
chieftain never quite recovered.

That, remarks a Bulletin correspondent,
is a strange, yet sensible, fashion which leads so
many Ballarat girls to cling to the snowy sun-
bonnets of their childhood. Some of the faces you
discover under the juvenile hoods are hard enough
to crack cocoanuts on, but in the majority of cases
the baby-frills hide lovely complexions, and often
rarely-beautiful faces.
* * *
A Northern “society ” family lately placed
its residence at the disposal of a visiting sprig
of nobility who gave a large and elaborate dance.
The “ host” coolly filled his programme with the
pick of the pure-merino damsels present, and
utterly ignored the three daughters of the house,
who hung about trying to catch his eye with an
apparent carelessness that was truly pathetic.
The next lord who comes to Queensland will either
have to hire a hall or cadge another mansion tor
his hop, so far as that family is concerned.
# * *
O’Hara, the Melb. surgeon militant, finds
that a popular baritone voice makes too many
calls upon his time, and he talks of letting out his
brawny left at the very next miscreant who tries
to book his warble for somebody’s benefit. Every
professional man gets tired of having two capa-
cities, especially when one of them is strictly
honorary. Edwin Oldfield, the fashionable tenor
tooth-doctor, lately notified concert promoters
that from Jan. Ist he would have to refuse to
address Pretty Jane, Maud, or|any other ballacb
heroine on a public platform. Oldfields nigh O
was beginning to swamp the fame of his achieve-
ments in the torture-chamber, so, being about to
start a dental clinic, he scratched himself for all
vocal engagements,
* • *
Another contingent of Rue Australians, 31
in number, has started for the land of the mando-
line, and the orange-groves, and the strange beasts
that bury themselves in your feet and have to be
dug out with a fork. Some of them are wives
who were left behind in the scrambling exodus of
1893 ; and one is a man who paid £274 into the
funds of the ill-fated colony and didn’t get Ins
passage after all. He has now paid £l7O more
and expects to really get there this time, so alto-
gether he will have paid £444 to arrive at a place
which is hardly worth half that amount all told.
Another squadron of emigrants is expected to go
out in March, so it would appear that the Hue
Australia myth isn’t quite dead, even now. Lhe
new arrivals are going by steamer via Monte
Video, and during their brief stoppage in that city
they might find a chance to do a kindly act by
supplying a feed and a bit of tobacco to the stone-
broke “mates” who are hoofing it along the weary
back-track from Don W. Lane’s paradise.

The estate of poor “ Champagne Beaney
suffered to the tune of ,£853 through the appeal
to the Privy Council, which reversed Vic. lull
Court’s decision re the famous Canterbury bequest.
How much more money was mopped up by the
two sets of proceedings in Melbourne the lawyers
only know. And now it is suggested that the
Canterbury £llO,OOO will not be devoted to the
founding 1 of a Beaney Institute at all, in which
case the Melbourne charities will take action to
obtain the aforesaid bequest for the “residue.”
But this sounds like too much joy for the wigs.
The Canterbury Corporation can scarcely be such
fools us to dream of departing from the terms of
the dead man’s bequest, seeing that they would
thereby risk an order for the restitution of mis-
applied funds, and costs, out of their own pockets.
Cardinal Moran has a secre-
tary, a thoughtful, meek-looking
young man, with a fine Roman
nose, and everywhere the Cardinal
goes that young man with the
Roman nose is sure to go. When
the Cardinal is on the Manly boat
that young man is on the Manly
boat. When the Cardinal is in his
carriage, that young man is also
in the Cardinal’s carriage. That
young man, in fact, is always on
hand. The Cardinal, however,
has never yet been seen to speak to
that young man or to anybody else
in public. It’s a beastly thing to
have to Vie always dignified ; and
what a slow time that young man
must have! We have seen him
hundreds of times with the Car-
dinal, but we have never once met
him in the bald-heads’ row in the
theatre, or on the lawn at the races,
or gaily footing it round with his
coat off at a Chowder picnic, or
doing the kitchen-lancers at'Cony's
pavilion, or swinging his girl round
at Clontarf. Never has he been
observed by any member of the staff
blowing the froth off the sanctified
tanglefoot brewed by Sir James
Toohey, K.S.G-. And yet that
young man has gladsome
aspirations in his heart of
hearts, for we lately saw
him carrying round a copy
of The Bulletin—carefully
wrapped up in the Sydney
Morning Herald.

* * •
Phil May and Ugo Catani are said to be
working up a Bohemian Club in London on
strictly temperance principles. No drinks, no
cards, no billiards, no nothing save lounging-
cliairs, and tobacco, and intellectual repose. It is
to be called “ The Long Livers’ Club.” The idea
was probably suggested by George Ashton to
Catani in the first instance, the dapper little
Italian carrying it along to May, who thought it
rather a good joke. George Ashton, when in Mel-
bourne, always preferred a feast of reason and a
flow of tea to a splitting headache next morning,
and Catani, whose studio was on the same floor,
used to drop in and gaze at his friend’s healthy
appearance with sincere admiration. The thought
that he himself didn’t look robust seemed to haunt
little Cat., though the society girls took kindly
to his feminine complexion and were apt to call
him “ quite a darling.”

Dear Bulletin, —“ Samuel Ballarat ” ap-
pears to have been floundering round the battered
old ecclesiastical assumption that the conscience
is something more than “artificial.” How’s
this for an analysis ? Mental kink, due to early
inculcation, '5O. Unreasoning amenability as
gregarious animals—to the prevailing opinions of
our kind, '2O. Imaginative self-presentment of
general reprobation, T 5. Superstition—an intel-
lectual spine-disease from which no man is abso-
lutely free, TO. Physico-moral cowardice, '5.
Total, 100— what we call “conscience.”— Heretic.

Editor Bulletin, —A week or two ago,
the curtain fell upon the last act of one of those
mysterious and characteristic tragedies which com-
pose almost the only history of the solitudes of
this vast continent. The steamer Nile was en
route tor Balranald, and when a few miles up the
Murrumbidgee from its junction with the Murray,
the helmsman observed a man, whom he took to
be asleep, lying on the broad of his back, not far
distant from the bank. A gun lay across his
shoulder, a dog by his side, and a boat at the
water’s edge. No particular notice was taken
of those signs, but when, on the steamer’s
return a fortnight later, they remained
almost unaltered, a party put off to investi-
gate. The dog, the only evidence of life,
welcomed them with delight, and led the way to
his master’s corpse. Part of the remains was
missing. A deep indentation on the side of the
head, round which a handkerchief was tied ; and a
discharged gun-barrel, pointed to the probable
cause of death. There was plenty of food in the
boat, with the exception of meat; there were also
a few song-books, but no papers or anything else
likely to lead to identification, save perhaps the
name of the boat. That, distinctly painted on
the bow, was “ God Knows Who.”- —F.M.L.

KINTORE’S FAREWELL.
The Lord Kintore from Austral’s shore
Has tore himself away ;
Before he went that raw-boned gent
Found lots of things to say.
On either hand a chosen band
Assisted at the scene,
When Kintore sly, witli humid eye,
Said : “ Never try to break the tie
That binds you to the Queen ;
I mean
Your Mother Country’s Queen.
“ If, soon or late, you federate,
Still prate about the Throne ;
For, bless my heart! you mustn’t start
A nation ‘ on your own.’
Meantime, restrain your liking vain
On Parliament to lean.
And do, I pray, by night and day
Take heed alway to sing or say
‘ God save our Gracious Queen,
Serene,
Our Mother Country’s Queen ! ’
“ That raggy- 1 gag,’ the British Flag,
Should drag your trade in tow ;
In fact, in short, you must import
From Hingland, d jnoherknow.
Now, fare you well ! I go to dwell
Where aye my heart has been.
Oh, do not cry, or sob, or sigh,
But loyally maintain the tie
Which binds you to the Queen ;
I mean
Your Mother Country’s Queen.”

A Melbourne undertaker complains that
the local cadaver is now almost invariably content
with a stained “ piner.’’ Circumstances alter
“ cases.”
# * •
A Melbourne woman, proceeding for
maintenance, told the Prahran Court that her
husband used to take bottles of fleas to bed to
tease her. At least she would not suffer alone.
The world has yet to discover the man who could
sleep in the same bod with a woman and a flea.
# # «
Minister Carruthers attributes the failure
of Pitt Town (N.S.W.) settlers to “badland,”
Now, the land was chosen by one Govt, expert,
approved by two others, and consistently praised
by Board of Control, superintendent, and settlers,
until it became evident the settlement was
doomed. Dr. Roseby’s famous panegyric of the
“ beautiful, simple, Arcadian ” life was illustrated
by a variety of vegetables posed in triumphant
proof of the quality of settlement soil—which, in
parts, is at least as good as that from which un-
subsidised settlers around have carved out com-
fortable homes. But any excuse serves to cover
up the hideous deficiencies of the pauperising
settlement system.
* * #
Why don’t the Railway Commissioners
remove the upper guard-bar from the windows of
Sydney suburban carriages ? The two lower are
a sufficient protection against accident, and the
upper, which quite prevented egress in the late
disaster, adds a terrible risk to the next one.

Of 481 Sydney typhoid cases last year, one
in every eight was fatal.

A leper from the Alligator River (N.T.)
district was brought into Palmerston, the other
day, and isolated at the lonesome leper camp
“ across the river.” The finder of this specimen
reported that he could supply 100 leprous abori-
ginals from the same district at very short notice.
* * #
The latest Tichborne legend is that the
real Roger, heir to over £30,000 a year, lived and
died in a stealthy sort of fashion as a pauper at
Geelong (Vic.), apparently for no other reason
than that he liked to be a pauper. The story rests
on the assertion that he communicated his secret
to a priest in his last illness. Which reminds the
present writer that many years ago an old man, in
his last illness, acknowledged to him that he was
really Queen Elizabeth. Probably the Geelong
pauper of the fifties was Queen Elizabeth also.

Ed. Bulletin,—lt is high time the police
put down the blackmailing “ foxers’ ” game at
such resorts as Mosman’s Bay and Coogee. One
black-bearded ruffian, who carries a heavy crop-
handle as an “ inducer,” spends half his life on his
hands and knees, creeping about Coogee shore-
lands in search of loving couples. One day he’ll
be found full of bullet-holes, and then some fool
of a lawyer will try to persuade a level-headed
jury it was a case of murder, when all the time
they know it to be simply an execution. —N.S.F.

Dear Bulletin, —I met in Melbourne, the
other day, a man from the French Island Village
Settlement. He informed me that, in conse-
quence of supplies not arriving from the mainland
(owing to rough weather and official negligence),
the settlers had, upon one occasion, to live for a
fortnight on boiled fern-leaves and salt. I heard
the same story from another person—a woman
who, with her children, has returned to Melbourne
to starve in a somewhat less savage fashion.
This, remember, did not happen in Rue Aus-
tralia. —C.R.

• # *
Archbishop Dunne (Q., says that the
church has the same right to exclude, from the
expounding of the Scriptures in schools, persons
not commissioned by her, which the State has to
exclude from the law-courts men who would
practise as barristers without having passed exam-
inations and been called in due order. The
analogy is unhappy, from whatever standpoint it
be examined. But what will the archbishop say
when, as in the course of time must come to pass,
the courts will give audience to all men competent
to discuss the matters in dispute, irrespective of
wig, gown, or a “ call in due order ?” Will the
right the archbishop claims now expire with that
of his analogue?

• • •
Theosophists say that the two principal
Mahatmas now reside in an oasis of the Desert of
Gobi. Probably a misprint for the Desert of
Gobemouche. But even Gobi isn’t remote enough
now for the Mahatma to reside in, with a blissful
assurance that no one will come along and prove
that he isn’t there. Two Yankee bicyclists crossed
it recently, and the Mahatmas will soon have to
move further back.

Two or three members of the present Eng-
lish team drink beer as freely here as they are
accustomed to do in England—with the result
that they are learning that English draughts of
English beer cannot be taken with impunity
during the Australian summer.
* * • .
The Saturday half-holiday, say the police,
is highly appreciated by a sodden section of the
Sydney community. Between noon and midnight
numbers of swollen-faced men get drunk three
times, sobering up by a sleep in the park after
each bout. They do not carouse or expand in
jollity or cultivate excitement ; they simply swal-
low (mostly beer) till drunk, stagger into the
park, sleep themselves back to reason and repeat
the process.

William Doherty : The meaning of “Men who

A commercial dictionary, date 1819, shows
that Protectionist England levied a duty of 6d.
per lb. on imported bacon. The duty on striped
linens was ,£172 10s. per the £lOO value, if allowed
to enter the country at all. Some kinds of linen
were seemingly prohibited in England at this
time. Cotton threads and woollen piece goods
were subject to 50 per cent duty, and pictures,
ranging between 2ft. and 4ft. square, paid £6 16s,
each at the Customs. Eor anything over 4ft.
squ ire the tax was £6 16s. There were no oleo-
graphs to offend the eye in those days, which, by
the way, were the good old days, so much admired
by Australian Freetraders as a rule.
Dear Bulletin, —It was unnecessary for
the late Judge Stephen to remark that Mrs.
Stephen “ didn’t always err on the side of mercy.”
We have only to listen to the feminine gabble
over any great crime to be convinced that woman
is a vastly more unmerciful animal than man;
partly because the emotional is crueller than the
logical, and partly because an inferior power of
generalisation and a narrower contact witli human
nature render the average woman infinitely less
Capable than the average man of making with the
offender that imaginative exchange of individu-
ality without which there would be uo such thing
as mercy.—A.M.


Mr. H. J. Samuell informs us his various
publications are selling well. Geo. Robertson and
Co. are his principal agents, and report large sales
of “ 1 was Dreaming,” song and waltz, “Nitouche
Galop,” and Harrison White’s “ Slumber Song,”
“Violets Musical Album,” and Mrs. H. J.
Samuell’s “ Household Cookery Book while
Messrs. Anthony Hordern and Sons, Sydney,
report the largest sale of Mrs. H. J. Samuell’s
“ Ladies’ Home Companion ” ever known in Aus-
tralia. It contains 120 pages on the complexion,
the hair, teeth, hands, &c., and it is mailed any-
where for one shilling. In fact, ladies cannot do
without it. It makes a woman of 60 look 18.
