January 22, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
It was deeply interesting at Bong Bong (Moss
Yale) races, which were the social event of last
week, to see how happy the ’Ome brigade looked
splashing round in the mud, which is, after all,
an Englishman’s native element. Gov. Duff, in
baggy riding breeches and long yellow boots, stood
out in the Scotch mist, on Bong Bong Cup day,
looking as much at home as a duck in a frog-pond.
Lady Duff, in a Wonga-pigeon-colored suit with a
white vest and a toque hat weirdly reminiscent of
the pinky-topped cake you meet at a birthday-tea,
also ignored the dew-drops ; but Lady Hopetoun
(all the men were loud in admiration of her
granite-blue-colored serge suit with pale-pink
necktie and neat white sailor hat with a pink band)
spent most of her day in the judge’s box—a toll-
bar like structure with only one opening. This
strikes me as being a most humane idea for vice-
regal accommodation in public places—on the
well-known principle that a man can always fight
beat with his back against a wall.
Seeing how sloppy the day was the dresses on
view at this little sporting fixture—generally as
dressy as a little Melbourne Cup, although the
lawn is only about the size of a pocket-handker-
chief —were not very fussy, though, as most of the
gowning was strictly suitable, it was necessarily
in good taste. Mrs. Archie Young, who enter-
tained the vice-regal party at luncheon, wore a
black gown with bands of white silk covered with
black insertion topped off by a wide black hat,
and her sartorial antithesis was a lady gotten up
in green satin covered with black net—a lace
hat in black and pink, a pink chiffon ruffle, a big
pink rose, all alive oh ! (as Bland Holt would say)
in her bosom, and a grey fur boa. Fur, lace, and
flowers. You can see for yourself how the com-
ponent parts of her get-up absolutely swore at one
another. A pretty girl in a heliotrope pongee silk
gown and a leghorn hat was a most fetching mem-
ber of the budding brigade. From Sydney came,
amongst others, Miss Maude Roberts in navy-
blue, and Miss Bessie Tarrant also in dark blue.
Two pretty girls in cinnamon-brown riding-habits
of the “ box-bottom ” cut, which looks so dashing
up, but reveals so much on the near-side when
Diana is afoot, suggested the first phase of the
New Woman in N.S.W. when you saw them left-
side on. I also wonder why a very tall girl dressed
herself in vine-green ?
Captain Wallington was there. I hear he joins
the Duff household after he has superintended the
embarkation of the Hopetouns, and Governor
Duff should shake hands with himself thereupon.
“Pipple say,” indeed, the captain is subsidised by
the British Government to hang about down
under as bear-leader to strange Governors feeling
their official feet, and, considering that he came
with the Caringtons and has pottered about Aus-
tralia ever since, it really looks as if there was
something in the rumor. At any rate, it is signi-
ficant that Captain Wallington has never been
associated with a vice-regal frost, and if Gov.
Duff doesn’t appreciate him he is a doomed poten-
tate.
Captain Wallington aforesaid, in ginger cheeks
and bright yellow leggings, on Bong Bong lawn
was a sight for the gods; but, indeed, all the men
were in mufti, and I shall never forget one local
nabob in pale emu-green with a sallow-colored
necktie to match his complexion and yellow
leggings on his spider shanks. He could have
gone to any bal masque and made a great hit as a
canvas-back duck. Though, perhaps, the funniest
man was a well-known youth in cream-colored
pants, light coat, big pearl buttons on his vest, a
pink shirt, black tie, tan boots, and a burnt
cabbage-tree hat the shape of a strawberry pottle.
He was a novelty, though, of course, every true
Australian knows that cabbage-tree hats (often
costing many guineas apiece) are like meerschaum
pipes—the dirtier they are the more distingue.
Still, the ensemble of this festive youth suggested
the title-page of a comic song.
A novelty at Bong Bong this year was the dis-
play of the prizes, which'were, for once, not all
jewellery. Is it a sign of the times that the club has
taken to crowning the victors with silver salad-
bowls and entree-dishes —utility as well as
ornament?—set out in glittering array on the
grand-stand. The ladies’ bracelet—a rather ugly
gold curb-chain, set with four jewels, was carried
off by Mr. A. Cordeaux, who won the Bong Bong
Cup (a massive silver service) with the same horse,
“Clive.” Talking about horses, the French of a
prominent sporting man must be very staggery, or
was it the printer’s fault that Mr. Blank Dash’s
chestnut mare, “ Merveilleux,” figured pro-
minently in the pink programme?
The second day at Bong Bong came down even
a worse soaker than the first. No vice-regalities
turned up (not even Lady Hopetoun), but still the
races were a success, if only for the champion
opportunity the many rnud-puddles afforded for
Bowral belledom to exhibit its credentials for the
front row of the ballet. One strawberry blonde,
in gas-green with tan stockings, was especially
careful of the hem of her frook. The girl with
lemonade-bottle legs is always much less of a
draggle-tail than the syren who goes through life
on a pair of axe-handles.
There was only a pinch of dressing on the second
day. The prizes were given by Mrs. Young
in the absence of Lady Duff, and then the
curtain fell over the annual sporting carnival of
the Moss Vale district, which was quite a success,
in spite of the naughty weather. The only draw-
backs I could see were the absence of music (even
a concertina would have been thankfully received)
and the presence of the whitewashed lawn-fence.
Anybody in dark colors wrecked her appearance
for the day if she leant against it.
From the general atmosphere, Gov. Duff seems
to be about as popular in his Sutton Forest retreat
as he is at the top of Bent-street. Ever since
his speech (it was at the first test cricket-match,
wasn’t it?), in which he alluded in a vague and
puzzled sort of way to his suite having once
played a game with the Moss Yale butchers, he
believed—said “butchers” being the ultra-fashion-
able Moss Vale and Bowral Club men—he has
seemingly seen fit to regard these gay and festive
blades (who rather enjoy the situation, I believe !)
as the “ butchers ” hereinbefore alluded to.
At a cricket-match at Hill View, last week, for
instance, between the aforesaid club (whose pretty
club-house and grounds are one of the neatest
things in Australia) and the Government House
young men, guess the amused surprise of the
former when, instead of being asked to meet Lady-
Duff and Lady Hopetoun and the rest of the
feminine interest at afternoon tea, the gilded
youths aforesaid were forced to refresh themselves
with coarse mugs of tea set at tables draped in
newspaper ! However, the visiting team should
be humbly grateful for even that little improve-
ment. All reforms move slowly. Last year, it is
averred, His Ex. served them out “colonial ’’ ale
in watering-pots.
The Sydney military scandal (I understand that
the full particulars are too Zolaesque for publica-
tion) is doubtless a very disgusting affair, taken on
its merits, and yet there is a comical side to the
whole thing. I allude to the ill-concealed un-
easiness of a great many gallant warriors (andl
possibly a great many gallant warriors’ sisters,
cousins and aunts) at the unparalleled attitude
taken by the Chief Military Authority in the
affair. It has always been tacitly understood in
barracks that all scandals should (if the thing was
humanly possible) never be allowed to go beyond
the sentries, and for a Commandant to set his own
private emissaries on to the case and even inter-
view the lady in proper person is absolutely with-
out precedent, and is a very worrying develop-
ment in his general r6le of martinet.
The wife of a well-known “ river ” Lothario
lately got to hear of certain alleged proceedings
of I)is, and set out one evening in a surprise-party
of one for the usual trysting spot. Mrs. Lothario
left the steamer at the wrong place ; and, seeing a
shadowy girlish form looming sylphlike in the
twilight, she never paused for further evidence, but
broke her best brolly over the hussy’s head strictly
on the Irish principle. But imagine her horror
when she found she had been fruitlessly damaging
the young and innocent che-yild of a resident
official !
Touching on the Flossie subject, a correspondent-
reminds me of the pantomime ladies who are fairy
queens and other diaphonous creatures in the
coming pantos., and who are beginning to throng
the streets on their way to and from rehearsal o’
mornings. “ What becomes of them,” mournfully
queries my correspondent, “ when they lose their
juvenility, when old age creeps on them and
wrinkles take the place of the curves of beauty ? ”
What becomes of them?—the same thing that
becomes of dead donkeys. You never saw an old
ballet-girlout of a job in your life —they are all in the-
back and middle rows. People in search of strik-
ing instances of sprightly longevity and models of
a green old age are advised to apply at the stage-
door during the run of any show with tights in it.
Indeed, when you come to look at the thing all
round, the chorus is the only place of safety for
aged females with something of a kick left in
them. And, as to the query about how they keep
body and soul together in the off-season, I can’t
help thinking that’s nobody’s affair. You see
books which promise to tell you how a ballet-girl
wears diamonds on 30s. a week, but that goes with
the wicked-Jook yarns. The plain work-a-day
coryphee is mostly a well-meaning old lady who
knits babies’ boots in the greenroom and takes in
washing between her glittering engagements.
An interesting agony advt. from a Sydney
daily :
rpALL Lady, grey dress, came late Rev. ’s
meeting Sunday evg., please write 1. J.W., Q.P.O.
If there is much more of this sort of thing, the
bare benches of the evangelical enthusiast will be
as promising a happy hunting-ground for the
golden-haired brigade as The Strand after Bp.m,
The suspicious wives of “business-men ” will look
askance at hubby when he wants to get his little
soul insured seven nights a week, and the wise
matrons, who go along too, will spend so much
time in watching to see whether the plump en-
quirer in pink really winked at Thomas or not
that they might as well be at a music ’all, for all
the spiritual benefit they get out of it. That is
the worst of large public gatherings, with no sort
of espionage at the door. A nice young man is
never sure that the demure, strange damsel who
shares his hymn-book with him won’t persist ia
calling him “ ducky ” under cover of the music.
Max O’Rell’s far-fetched story about the pro-
cession of hansom cabs with drawn blinds has
stirred Potts Point to the very depths, because
Max describes the criminals who travel in these
dubious vehicles as belonging “ to the superior
classes.” Max says the procession moves at 10
a.m., as a general thing, and that the women are
closely veiled. But what about the men? Are
they veiled, too? Or does the frisky fat man (the
local Lotharios are generally bald and fatherly) go
out brazenly in the myriad-eyed morning with a
mysterious female in a common cab, and risk his
pansy-blossom appealing to Judge Windeyer ?
I don’t mean to argue for a moment that Sydney
is altogether innocent of the true use and applica-
tion of a cab with the blinds down. I would say
so if I thought anybody would believe me, just to
give the moral prestige of the city of the Beautiful
Harbor a lift; but it is certainly a fact that blind-
drawn cabs aren’t so wholesale as Mons. Blouet
was led to believe. You can nearly always tell a
dubious cab by the attitude of the cabman. He is
generally flat on his face collecting evidence
(which, to do him justice, he hardly ever uses,
these little affairs being professional secrets) while
the horse drives itself along the bushy suburban
road. I know it isn’t much use bringing forward
the fact that in only one Sydney cause ce!6bre the
blinds-down hansom played a prominent part.
Cabmen, like waiters, are not as other witnesses
are, but still the fact goes for something when you
consider how hungrily the private ’tec. haunts
Venus and Adonis nowadays.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
When Giffen won the toss in Adelaide, Stoddart
gave the Australians 400. “ They are a weaker team than
the ones we met in Melb. and Sydney,” said Stoddart,
“ because they have only one bowler ; but they can hat,
anyway.” George Giffen was delighted at the heat of the
day, but he never dreamt of the disasters that came.
Bruce gave M‘Laren a catch —he dropped it. “ Now he’s
right lor 100,” said Briggs, but he played a ball on to the
wicket next over.
Trott was run-out while batting beautifully.
Then Iredale, Darling, Gregory, Harry, and Worrall were
out for 15 runs between them 1
On the first day there was only one fine catch,
but it was worth going miles to see. Jarvis, who was
batting well, stood up and drove Lockwood hard back,
and the bowler sprang at it, catching the ball in one
hand. It was the catch of the match.
With 9 wickets down for 157 the Australians
were given 160. “It must be a bad wicket,” was the
verdict of the crowd. Then Albert Trott and Callaway
trounced the bowling, especially Lockwood. Ten after
ten mounted up until Callaway, who carries a stone and
a-half more flesh than he did a year ago, became com-
pletely exhausted and collapsed to Richardson. 239 all
out.
Blackham said the Englishmen would not make
200 on the wicket, which was a little fiery. Giffen gave
them 250 at the most. They would have made 300 only
for brilliant fielding. First, Bruce electrified the crowd
by catching Ward ; then Harry male a wonderful one-
handed catch, dismissing the dangerous Brockwell. Later
Worrall made a unique catch, a contortionist act which
alone was worth the admission money, and the same
brilliant player caught Richardson beautifully. The
Australian fielding was voted by the Englishmen the best
they have seen anywhere, and they frankly admit their
inferiority in that respect.
Giffen clean-bowled Stoddart, in Melb., for 173.
In Adelaide he clean-bowled him for one. It was a real
duel for supremacy, but very brief. Stoddart played
two balls confidently, two gingerly, and the fifth clean-
bowled him—a plain, straight ball, but it was “ Giffen’s
fast ball."
Perhaps not for safety, but for brilliancy,
Bruce’s 80 was a beautiful innings, and was almost as
rapidly made as Lyons’ great score at Lord’s, in ’94. It
was the most brilliant innings the writer has witnessed
on Adelaide Oval, and he has seen Lyons make most of
’ his big scores there. Bruce, when set, was dragging Peel
from the off-stump to square-leg with impunity.
* * * »
The Bulletin estimates that a good half of the
Adelaide victory comes through relying, for once, on men
rather than on reputations. Their performances showed
that several of the old heroes (who have done nobly in
their day) should have ceded to others the place of pride
.in the two previous test-matches. But they were picked
(or picked themselves) on the strength of their glorious
past, and cut a very poor figure in the inglorious present.
The justification of the new policy is its brilliant success.
* » * *
Dear Bulletin, — One Australian characteristic
—perhaps, after all, it's a general human charac-
teristic—is to take a beating badly and to be over-exultant
In victory. I was in Sydney when Beach beat Hanlan,
and remembrance of the wild delirium that ensued will not
soon fade away from my memory. If you ventured a
word in Hanlan’s favor it simply meant a plug in the eye.
1 think we have all been a little too cock-a whoop over the
Adelaide win. Let us comport ourselves with moderation
in the presence of so game and modest a sportsman as
Stoddart.—Emin.
* • • *
It is noteworthy that the Adelaide wicket, on
which the Englishmen have twice been badly beaten, is
the fastest in Australia, and consequently the one most
opposed to English conditions.
* ♦ ♦ *
The three test-matches have all been sensa-
tional, and the greatest sensation is the performance of
A. E. Trott in the last one. He scored 38 (not-out) in
the first innings, 72 (not-out) in the second (110 in all
without losing his wicket), and then as a bowler sent
eight Englishmen to the right-about for 43 runs.
» * • * *
When an Australian bowler does wonders, Aus-
tralia wins a test-match, and Trott's performance merits
the hope that the phenomenal bowler that Tub Bulletin
prayed for the other week has indeed arisen in his might.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
If Blaekham’s wisdom be commensurate with
his years, he will now resign the wicket-keeping premier-
ship. Age cannot be denied; and the father of modern
wicket-keepers should, after his brilliant 20 years, retire
in favor of younger men who, having learnt the art from
him, have the vigor of youth to enforce their claims.
Jarvis has certainly proved himself in the third test-
match to be more adroit than Blackham is now.
« ♦ • »
Dear Bulletin, —If Donnan comes off in
the Interprovincial match, New South Wales v.
Victoria, it will be hard to keep him out of
the Australian team for the next match. When
Donnan played with N.S.W. against the English, he
played two faultless innings for 37 and 39 respectively,
and secured two wickets for 44. Why Donnan was not
picked to play in Adelaide against the Englishmen is
rather a puzzle, for since the ’9O-’9l season he has come
off in the interprovincial matches there to the tune of
50 runs an innings. Last time he scored 24 and 48 not
out, against Giffen and Co., and Blackham, speaking of
this, said that of all batsmen he knows, Donnan is most at
home with the great Giffen. —W.
• * • •
Umpire Briscoe, representing N.S.W. in the
recent interprovincial matches against Victoria and
South Australia, had thus the best of opportunities for
forming an estimate of A. K. Trott. “Heis a grand bats-
man," says Mr. Briscoe, “ and a most wonderful field ;
if I wished to praise a cat’s nimbleness I should say it was as
j nimble as Trott when he brought off his two marvellous
catches in our match against the Vies. But, as for his
bowling, I think that his 8 for 43 against the Englishmen
is no criterion of his real merit; it only shows that it was
his day out. Give me Giffen, who got 2 for 57, far in
preference to Trott. Trott is the beat of the Vic. bowlers;
I saw him get 5 for 55 against our men in the Vie. match.
He holds the ball similarly to the generality of bowlers
who bowl an off break ; but his best ball, perhaps, is his
fast yorker, which is often red-hot. He is employed on
the ground by the East Melbourne Cricket Club, looking
after the ground in the morning, and in the afternoon
doing professional work for the club. Twenty-three or 24
years old, he is the embodiment of strength and supple-
ness.”
• * • •
Umpire Briscoe says that Callaway is in splendid
form, and although Adelaide ground suits his bowling, he
deserves all the wickets he seemed, even his 5 for 37
against the Englishmen. Briscoe thinks that the hall
which hit Ulinman oti the mouth won the match for
Adelaide against N.S.W. Callaway sent Blinman a fast
one which, gliding off his hat, struck the batsman with
great force in the mouth, knocking him s useless. Had
the ball missed Blinman's face, it. would have been caught
and Blinman would not tiave made bis fine stand. As it
was, no one could reach the ball in time. Blinman, when
he recovered consciousness, never thought of his wound,
which split botli Ids lips for three-quarters of an inch ; he
only asked, “ Am I out ? ”

Syd. Gregory lias been quite ill of late; “ too
ill to play,” so his father says.
* ♦ • ♦
Adelaide ground, with its low brick wall in
front of the grand-stand, and its asphalt path encircling
it, is very severe on cricket balls. In the int [provincial
match, N.S.W. v. S.A., the first hall used was knocked to
pieces after 47 runs had been scored.
♦ ♦ * ♦
Lord Sheffield’s eleven came out with the avowed
intention of enlivening Australian hatting, and, in a
general way, they scored about 40 per cent, faster than
our men. Matters are quite otherwise with the present
team. Stoddart has slowed down surprisingly, and the
new dashing batsmen of the company, Ford, MacLaren
and Brockwell, are apt to get out as soon as they try to
dash much.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The insobriety of one of the Englishmen is said
to have called forth Capt. Stoddart’s threat to oust the
offender from the team, under the behavior clause in their
agreement, unless he steadies himself.
* * » *
The rather absurd appearance given to Syd.
Gregory’s testimonial by his batting subsequent to his
big Sydney score hasn’t deterred a number of hysterical
busybodies from attempting an Albert Xrott testimonial.
This sort of thing justifies new-chum sneers at Australian
“ blow.” Time enough for gilding young Albert T. when
he has repeated his big performance in the next two
matches. What has become of the fast ball that Briggs
used to bring out occasionally in days of yore?
* * * *
Says Umpire Briscoe, of N.S.W. : “Ask me
who is the best of Australian batsmen, and I shall say
George Giften. Ask me who is the best of Australian
bowlers, and again I shall answer George Qiffen. George
Giffen is no more nor less than a phenomenal combination
of cricketing backbone and muscle. He will commence
to bowl at 12 o’clock and, keeping on till 5, his arm will
then come over with as tireless a swing, and his length
will be as good as at the day’s commencement. His pre-
cision is magical. In the interprovincial malch in
Adelaide, when Callaway came in, Giffen, knowing Calla-
way’s excellent off-stroke, said to me, ‘lt won’t do to
pitch them on the off to him.’ Nor did he; every bA.II
he sent down to Callaway was pitched on a line between
the batsman’s legs and the wicket. Then again, Moses,
during his long stay for 77, never got a ball from Giffen
to cut.
• • ♦ ♦
Giffen fought hard to secure the inclusion of
Lyons in the Australian team for the third test-match, bui
Turner and Blackham were obdurate.
♦ ♦ * ♦
As Worrall went out to field on the Saturday in
the first innings of the Englishmen in the Adelaide test-
match, a ragged little urchin standing near the pavilion-
gate called to himl ‘‘Now, then, Jack, catch three of
them out to make up for your duck.” When the Austra-
lians returned, three hours later, aftr the foreign stumps
had ail fallen for 124, the same juvenile prophet cried out
encouragingly; “What did 1 tell you, Jack?’’ The
happy cricketer smiled on his barracker benignly.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Umpire Briscoe places N.S.W. batsmen in this
order—Gregory, Moses, Iredale.
* * « *
A. E. Trott bowls an off-break, which, on a real
good wicket, doesn’t nip back more than two or three
inches. Howell, of N.S.W., with the same medium-pace,
same good-length ball, comes back an inch or two more.
♦ ♦ * *
Callaway, of N.S.W., is one of the best-tempered
cricketers alive, and is, therefore, one of the most popular
with cricketers themselves. He plays the game with an
earnest fun ; if a fieldsman drops a catch off him, he will
laugh at the blunder, and bowl away once more as merrily
as ever. Turner, on the contrary, shoots a glance at a
delinquent fieldsman which causes the blunderer to pray
for the earth to yawn and take him, and the climax of
thundering scowls is reached when Turner drops Donnan,
or Donnan drops Turner. These two bowlers, however,
are singularly unfortunate, and Dorman’s luck particu-
larly would at times ruin the temper of an angel.
* * * •
On their Adelaide performance the English men
haven’t a 5 to 1 ohance of winning the fourth test-match.
The all-round completeness of their failure on the big
Oval is, in fact, the best excuse that one can offer for pre-
dicting a win for them in Sydney. They were too much
beaten the other week. Young Trott and Callaway had
previously bowled against them without remarkable suc-
cess. They seem to be unduly frightened of Giffen, yet
they have scored heavily in Adelaide, Sydney, and Melb.
when Giffen was on the job. The bowling of the Aus-
tralians is the thing to be most considered in estimating
the chances of the next game, for phenomenal fielding,
such as they showed in Adelaide, doesn’t get much oppor-
tunity to assert itself when the batsmen are fairly at ease.
No catch of importance was missed whilst Stoddart and
co. njade their 475 runs in Melbourne ; the trouble was
that the Australians didn’t get enough chances. Nor
can the batting of the Australians be expected to win
them this fourth match—upon the Adelaide “form.”
The bowlers are their best card, and if that doesn’t collar
the other side’s batting it will be anybody’s game. Thus
far, the Australian scores have always been benefited by
the Britishers’ loose fielding, but they may presently im-
prove in the catching department. After saying all which,
we find ourselves remembering the old sporting truism
that “ Latest form is the best form to judge by.”
* * * •
Blackham, when he advocated Albert Trott’s
claims for the test-match, said, “ He’s a coming chain-
Sion.” “Yes,” said Giffen, “I’ve batted against his
owling, and he’s batted against my bowling, and I think
the same.”
• * * *
The best fieldsman in the third test-match was
Brown. He is the finest point yet seen in Australia.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The Australians have made a bowling record.
In the first English innings, Trott was taken off and then
Callaway and Giffen bowled unchanged. In the second
innings, Callaway went off and Giffen and Albert Trott
secured the ten wickets. Where is there such a record
for a fine-weather match ?
♦ » * »
The Englishmen still stick to it, that Turner is
even now the prince of Australian bowlers.
m * ♦ *
Moses surprised everybody, and no one more
than himself, by his brilliant fielding in Adelaide.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
Certain prominent and high-toned barrackers
for the English cricket-team should certainly have better
taste than to openly ascribe the Adelaide defeat to “ the
sale of the game by those demned professionals.” One
good point about the Australian sport—it is free from the
everlasting snobbery which pervades the Hinglish article
from root to branch.

match—the first made by any member of the team in
the tour.
* ♦ * *
Coningham complained to a Brisbane inter-
viewer that he was not given a show to bowl by Giffen,
who only allowed him two or three overs at a lime. He
declares he will play no more under Giffen.
* * * *
In the three test-matches, Australia scored 1857
runs and England 1579, leaving 278 runs in favor of ,Aus-
tralia. The Australians’ batting average is 28 per man,
and the Englishmen’s not quite 24.

When the Bth man goes m how many wickets
are there to fall? ” This innocent query has caused much
trouble In Melh. lately. The cricketer who answers “3 ”
is met with hilarious scorn, and the following argument
usually convinces him that “4” is the proper answer:
When the Blh man goes in one “ not out” man is already
at the wickets—the 3 “ not out ” men still in the pavilion
make the total up to 5 4 wickets are, therefore, still to
fall. This argument is, of course, fallacy if we say “ Bth
ram in ” with the usual signification. The “Bth man in ”
is really the 9th-seeing that "2nd man in” follows 2
others and is the 3rd. The correct cricket answer to the
query is 3.

J. Carpenter, a Richmond River (N.S.W.)
aboriginal, in a cricket-match between two aboriginal
teams, the other day, took 14 wickets for 17 runs.
♦ ♦ * *
H. : “ Hear me out over cricket. I call it a
boy’s play-game. Suppose we heard the Americans had
gone balmy over a baseball match, what would we think?
In a parlous time like this, in poverty-stricken Melbourne,
it is absurd to see the population smoking its pipe round
a cricket-ground. Cricket is not a healthy game. No
very eminent cricketer has been long-lived. The knocks
received are mischievous. What, after all, is the great-
ness of a Giffen or a Gregory ? In the latter half of life
it is followed by blank disappointment and futility of
existence. All of us can recall examples.”
* * * *
The grey and grizzled lob-bowler, Humphries, is
the singer of the English team. When in Ballarat he
lifted up his tenor in a church choir.
* * * ♦
Ned Gregory has been caretaker of Sydney’s
chief cricket-ground since the inauguration of the Associa-
tion 17 years ago. His caretakership dates back for really
aquarter of-a-century, in the old “civil and military”
time. Gregory has tried many different soils in the pre-
paration of his famous wickets. Soil has been brought
from Kiama, N.S.W., and proved none too good; from
Merri Creek, Vic., whence to this day comes the soil for
the Melb. wicket; from Richmond, N.S.W. (it was on a
wicket of this that Murdoch compiled his 321 against Vic-
toria), and this Richmond soil always made a durable
wicket. The soil now' used, and which has been used for
the past three seasons, was brought from Bulli, N.S.W.
It is a brownish clay, and when sown with couch-grass
makes the best of wickets. Gregory says "It absorbs the
water like a sponge and lets it percolate ; it won’t let the
sun in and doesn’t crack.”
Gregory uses couch-grass on the wicket in pre-
ference to the combination of English rye and clover,
which makes the Melb. and Adelaide wickets faster than
his. "It is the couch, not climatic, influence, which makes
my wicket slower; but then mine is ever so much more
durable.” The fielding-ground isalso clothed with couch,
although varied with many patches of clover. The wicket
is rolled with a 36owt. roller, and mown—Gregory can’t
say how often. The fielding ground, as a rule, is mown
once a week, except in the month of February, when,
with ordinary good seasons, the grass grows more rapidly
than at any other time, necessitating mowing twice a
week. The ground is elliptical, its longer diameter being
176 yards and its shorter 165, and to mow it takes a little
over a day. The elliptical curve is flattened most in front
of the grand-stand ; this was done for the sake of the
grand-stand lawn.
Gregory’s horse is one of the most familiar
cricket patrons in Sydney. He dates his ser-
vices from the advent of the Cricket Association itself, so
that for 17 years Gregory has been his master. He was a
Maitland colt four years old when he commenced his
cricket-ground career, so he has now 21 years opposite
his name. Gregory’s house is at the ground, but the site
being low, and a large, foul-smelling pond being near,
two of his sons (including batsmen Syd.) and all his
daughters have at one time or other had typhoid.
Gregory says he has two splendid wickets in readiness for
the interprovincial and international matches.
» * * *
If rowing is dead in Port Jackson, sailing is
very much alive, taking a line from the number of craft
which turned out on Saturday to witness the first inter-
provincial 22-footers’ champion race. Nineteen boats
started —five from Queensland, and fourteen from N.S.
Wales, the Esmeralda, Irex, and Vanina, all local boats,
finishing first, second, and third. The wind was fluky,
veering from South-east to East. The boats meet again
next Saturday at the Anniversary Regatta, when it is to
be hoped there will be a wholesome North-easter to give
the flyers a fair test “on the wind.” The Esmeralda was
built by James Hayes, of Balmain, and was sailed by
" Billy ” Bead.
* * * •
“Nick” Johnson, who owns the Sydney 22-
footer Irex, and lias sailed her with such signal success,
looks after his boat with the most scrupulous care. He
keeps her covered up in a shed at Lavender Bay, and
every day goes all over her with chamois leather, to
remove the faintest trace of damp. If he has been sailing
her on a Saturday afternoon and intends to sail again on
the coming Sunday, he nevertheless pulls her up into the
shed and covers her up for the Saturday night.
* * * *
Sydney Rowing Association has notified that it
will object to any oarsmen who may have cycled for
money prizes being included in the next Vic. Inter-
provincial eight. This objection is unmistakeahly aimed
at Dawson, the brilliant how of the Melo. Mercantile’s
senior four, for, though many of the Vic. oarsmen who
have rowed, and still row, without question as amateurs,
are also cyclists, none but Dawson stands a chance of
inclusion in a representative crew. Technically, the
Sydney Association is right. Victorian cyclists, though
they may term themselves "cash amateurs,” are never-
theless professionals distinctly ; but the question arises
whether it is right for a man who is a professional in one
sport to he debarred from competing as an amateur
in others. The rule of the Vie. Association is
just as stringent as that of N.S.W., hut, as a matter of
fact, it is never enforced any more than that of the Foot-
ball Association, which, on paper, prohibits the profes-
sional peds. and pugs., that sprinkle every team, from
taking part.
As amateur cycling is dead as Julius Cassar in
Vie., and any bov who can sit a machine properly imme-
diately enters the pro. ranks, the sooner this regulation
is changed the belter. Under its jurisdiction a man bom
at Hannan’s who lias won a 10s. wood-chopping competi-
tion, and who has probably never seen more than a panni-
kin of water all at once in his life, would he deemed a
professional oarsman and unfit to associate with the tony
amateurs of the Eastern cities. Let amateurism and pro-
fessionalism he kept as much apart as possible, hut don’t
let the former make a bigger fool of itself than it can help.
The bedrock of imbecility was reached by the rowing
scribe of the Australasian, who asked for certain oars-
men to be disqualified because they took part in an
amateur walking contest for a trophy, which happened to
be held on the M.O.G. the day of the Austral wheel race.
The N.S. Wales League promises the biggest
cycling show ever held in Australia in March next—£4oo
to be given away in prizes. Properly worked up, the
show should revive the boom of the Lee, Wood, Bennett
period of upwards of a decade since.
♦ » * •
Young Rome, son of Tom Rome, so well-known
in Queensland, is a tine specimen of a young Australian,
writes a London correspondent to The Bulletin. He is
at Sandhurst, and is going for a commission in the
cavalry. He is the best rider, football player, runner,
cricketer, boxer, and all-round athlete at the College.
He has just been made an under-officer, vice one of the
Teck boys, and is so popular, and has such a name in the
service, that he has been offered the choice of three crack
regiments. Young Rome is a strapping fellow of six feet
one.
♦ * * •
Dear Bulletin, —Re “G.W.M.’s” quotation
from Gatlin. The Mandan Indians’ swimming stroke is
nothing more or less than the “double-overarm” of
civilisation. Gatlin can’t have known the first thing
about his subject or he’d have known that it is the very
severest and the hardest to sustain of all styles, while the
ordinary breast-stroke, which he considers puts “ a
serous strain upon the chest, ’’ is the least-fatiguing one
in -lie. As a proof, it is the one used by all long-distance
swimmers throughout the world, however they may
revert to the more fanciful styles when spurting.—
Yours, F.

The unwearied efforts of the Melb. Sabbatarians
have at length produced fruit. The Vic. Ministry has
issued a proclamation prohibiting the pursuit of fishing—
with a seine nejt—on Sundays. But mark the duplicity of
this Godless Government! It does not forbid the Bad
Boy of the Sunday-school books, who goes out fishing on
[ the Sabbath and is drowned, from fishing as usual. On
the contrary, it conserves the fish for him, because there is
nothing said in the proclamation against the use of rod
and line. We want to be a boy again 1
Sam Hordern ought to take to pony-racing,
instead of horse-racing, judging from the number of little
’uns he has in training. As well might Griffo betake
himself to the Jackson-Corbett class.

At Alpha (Q.) a jockey named Markwell met
with a strange accident. While riding, a number of
matches and crackers in the boy’s pocket exploded, burn-
ing him cruelly about the thighs. He rode the race out
with the utmost pluck, notwithstanding.
» ♦ * »
The somewhat notorious galloway, Riot, now
running in Melbourne, is probably the oldest racehorse in
training in Australia. He is about 14 years old, and has
been racing continually since he was two. He hails from
Maoriland, where he won some good races ere coming to
Australia.
* * * *
M.L. A. squatter Russell was the big gun and
fairy god-father of Ballarat Hunt Club, and kept that
peculiar organisation most “select.” When it owed its
secretary two years’ salary an appeal was made for funds.
Air. Russell pleaded the necessity of retrenchment, in
view of the proposed Land-tax, and sent nil. Next week
the club sold its packs of hounds for £76. One pack,
presented by Mr. Russell, cost £lOOO.

Lucknow he was heavily backed, and ran nowhere.

Leishman, a German sailor, was fined £2 at
Willijinistown, Vic., the other day, for wanting to fight a
person named Smith with revolvers. Leishman informed
the Bench that he could not use his hands, and carried
the revolver to fight Britishers with. Leishman is deserv-
ing of sympathy. Some day, perhaps, it will be illegal for
a man to be able to use his hands.
At Goolma (N.S.W.) race-meeting recently, a
denizen of far-back took the odds on a horse and offered
the bookie a dozen wallaby-scalps in lieu of cash. These
were refused with many contemptuous colored adjectives,
to the great surprise of the bushman ; for no resident ever
dreams of refusing scalps at Goolma—not to mention
Goolmangar, Gooup, or Goonoo Goonoo.
• ♦ * *
The unlawful popularity of totalisators in Mel-
bourne is wonderful, and, from the proprietors’ point of
view, entirely satisfactory. A mere youth down Colling-
wood way, once a leading athlete, who, two years ago,
threw up his billet to run one, has on his own showing
cleared £5OOO each year. He views with equanimity a
matter of £3OO which has gone in fines and legal expenses.
One evidence of the general faith in his straightness is
the fact that every week hundreds of artisans and others
walk long distances to plank their shillings and half-
crowns on the machine—and then walk back again to save
tram-fares.
* * * *
The death of one of two Englishmen who lately
had a water-drinking match is recalled by the recent
painful death of a well-known Melb. cricketer, who
wagered that he would eat six ice-creams after playing
cricket all the afternoon. He got through four, and,
after being carried home, died the following day of peri-
tonitis.
* » » «
It was very shocking of the M.C.O. captain to
declare an innings closed after wickets were down in
order that the other side might do some more batting on
soft ground. But didn’t Stoddart’s tail-end men play a
reckless, forcing game in the first innings of the Melb.
test-match, with the result that there was enough time
left for disposing of the Australians before the Sunday
rest enabled the wicket to get quite dry? However, the
cases were not on all fours. The Englishmen, having
batted first, couldn’t be accused of “besting” their
adversaries, anyway. If they made haste to get out after
luncheon it was a fair piece of generalship.

for piles is most remarkable, large quantities
being sent to all parts of the colonies from the
depot at 257 George-street, Sydney.

CARBINE.
Because his racing days are o’er
Shall we so soon forget
Those laurels that he won and wore
Are his for wearing yet ?
Because we’ll never see again
The blaze-face forging through
When the wild cry of “Carbine wins I"
Shakes all the vault of blue ;
Because they say that times are hard
And money bad to get,
When Carbine comes into the yard
Shall we so soon forget ?
If we should hold our pearl too cheap,
There’s some beyond the sea
Will dare to dip their Angers deep
To purchase such as he.
Then stand to Carbine, sportsmen true,
And rally round the ring,
And bold and high make bid and buy,
Whatever price he bring ;
For it will be a scorn and shame,
A dire disgraceful day,
If honor fail and gold prevail
And Carbine’s sold away.

Our Trade is Big! Our Store is Big !
Our Ideas are Big!
We get bigger every day; on Saturday night
we are bigger than on the p rev i° us Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil we are planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a mess of the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.
Most People Like Us! All Believe in Us!
Not a Few Swear by Us!
It’s cupboard love. We suit them, make their
money go a long way, and generally render the
path of life an easy one. There’s no sentiment
about it. It’s real biz, and no mistake. In that
way we are beloved throughout Australia, the
measure of affection each one bestows upon us
being gauged by the amount of benefit received.
The more you buy at our store the greater your

IMPRISONED ON A SHIP.
The steamship Normannia, from Hamburg,
arrived in the port of New York on Saturday,
Sept. 3rd, 1892, with cases of cholera on board.
Many of the ship’s company had died on the pas-
sage. At Hamburg and elsewhere in Europe the
disease was raging. The authorities in America
were alarmed lest the scourge should be introduced
into that country. Hence they quarantined the
Normannia, with every soul of her passengers and
crew. The writer was a passenger. It was an
awful time. Death was among us and on all
sides of us. Nobody knew who next would fall.
We were imprisoned. Liberty never seemed so
fair, nor so far. We could neither fight nor fly.
There we were—hundreds of us —perfectly well,
and yet bound together as with chains, that the
health officer of the port might see whether the
plague would not yet break out in our midst.
When at last—after weeks of this —we were set on
shore, men lifted their hats and reverently said,
“ Thank God ! ”
This was being shut up under conditions to
make it horrible and fearful. Yet, any form of
incarceration is bad enough. Here is a woman,
for example, who says, “i never moved a yard
r>m my own doorstep for twenty weeks! ” Her own
house was a prison to her. Who had sentenced
her? A judge? No; a power greater and more
pitiless than any judge.
Her tale runs thus : In April, 1882, whilst living
at Lasher’s Farm, Old Sampford, Essex, a fire
broke out, and the family were burned out of
house ami home. We have no call to remark on
such a calamity. The very thought of it is fit to
make one shiver with dread. For most of us it is
like the world coming to an end to experience such
a disaster.
Well, what happened after that the lady shall
tell in her own fashion—the best of all fashions,
because it is plain and straight to the point. She
says; “Owing to our bedding being damp from
exposure, I took a bad cold, which brought on
rheumatic fever. For fourteen days I was con-
fined to my bed, and for twenty weeks I never
moved a yard from my own doorstep. After a
time the fever abated, leaving me weak, languid
and low. At first I had a sickening taste in the
mouth, and a poor appetite. No matter how
simple and light the food was, I was afraid to eat,
for it was sure to give me pain at the chest and
sides ; so I often had to loosen my corset and un-
dress mys ‘lf during the day. I could not bear the
weight of my clothing.
“I was constantly spitting up a sour, frothy
fluid, and had a gnawing pain at the pit of the
stomach—like hunger, and yet different. It was
with difficulty I voided the kidney secretion, and
my bowels, ankles and legs began to swell. I got
worse; I was in agony night and day, and could
not put my foot on the ground. Soon afterwards,
a husky cough took me, and my throat filled with
a thick phlegm. I could not sleep, and was never
easy. Later on, I had often to sit up in bed, fori
felt as if I should choke.
“ Year after year I continued to suffer in this
way, growing worse and worse, until I despaired
of ever being well again. But who can tell when
trouble will come, or when relief? A wonderful
Providence is over all.
“One day in June, a book came by post de-
scribing Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup and what
it had done for many poor sufferers. I got a
bottle from Mr. Suckling, medicine dealer, and
after taking it for a short time all pain left me,
and I gradually gained strength. By taking an
occasional dose I have since kept in good health,
and can eat and digest any kind of food. (Signed)
Mrs. Lydia Gkkkn, Moor End, Great Sampford,
via Braintree, Essex, Aug. 24th, 1892.”
Now, in order that Mrs. Green’s clear and
truthful statement may be of use to others (as
she desires it to be), we must add a word or two.
The bad cold she caught at the fire no doubt
“brought on” the rheumatic fever (as she
relates), but there was something back of the
cold, for a cold never causes rheumatism. The
r leumatio seeds, or poison, must already lie in
t ie blood ; and that poison is always created by
p -e-existing indigestion and dyspepsia, whether
the sufferer knows it or not. This is proved by
the fact that Mrs. Green’s chief ailment for ten
years after the fire was not rheumatism, but
indigestion and dyspepsia and dropsy, which is
one of its results and symptoms. When the
digestion was finally righted by the remedy she
alludes to, all her apparent maladies ceased
together. Why? Because she had but one, as we
have said.
Ah, yes, Disease is a stern gaoler. And how
sweet (and cheap) is liberty, obtained by Mother
Scigel’s help.
Winter’s Tail Melb. Standard staff.
A Melb. suburban boarding-place is called
“ Heaton House.”
Policeman X., defending himself from an
everyday Australian aspersion : —“ Let the papers
say what they likes about our watching the back-
doors of public-houses on Sundays, while thieves
make off with overcoats from the halls of the
boardinghouse-keepers—we’re doing the best thing
for the country. If we catch the publican, it's £3
fine into the State Treasury ; if we catch the thief,
it’s a month’s gaol or thereabouts, which means
£3 loss to the Treasury. I count hiding in the
back-yard of a pub. on Sundays the most patriotic
duty a number av the foorce can discharge. D’ye
moind me now?”
Dear Bulletin — Be the “Collins” and
“Salt” controversy, “Brigham Brown,” the
trenchant, doesn’t appear to have noticed that I
based my support of the saline end of the stick on
the ground that, whereas the gallant “Titus”
used the term “immorality” with reference to
mere breach of arbitrary conventions, the doughty
“Tom” had apparently in his eye that fulfilment
of sex-function which, as “Dr. 8.8. ” himself
admirably puts it, is no more inherently unclean
“ than the sparkle of a dewdrop or the rustle of a
leaf.” Excess—being abnormal —is, ipso facto,
injurious, but there may be an excess of repression
as well as of freedom, and the undue mortifica-
tion of any natural impulse is physiologically as
much a vice as its super-gratification. To
“Dr. B.B.” it will doubtless be no news
that while the “fasting,” for instance, of the
religious ascetic may, by diminishing vital force,
diminish, pari passd, certain physical appetites, it
balances matters by increasing to wolfish pitch
the not any less animal desire of hunger. Asceti-
cism, in a word, if it spiritualises with one hand,
brutalises with the other; and those atypical
persons to whom the exercise of the reproductive
function is professedly abhorrent stand—not, as
they flatter themselves, upon a higher plane of
evolution than their more normally constituted
fellows—but upon a lower ! They belong, in pro-
portion to the extent of their psycho-physical
aberration, to the domain of teratology, and are
far more akin to the two-headed calf than to the
elemental spirit. The octogenarian vigor of such
an abundant propagator of his species as the
just-dead De Lesseps is but one of many proofs
that rationally-exercised sexuality is a wholesome
thing, and that to unduly avoid the transfer of
vitality is not the best way to conserve it. —
Heretic.

figurements. Sulpholine developes a lovely skin.

The ex-legislator had long been a notorious
teetotaller—a reclaimed brand plucked from the
alcoholic burning. He had been noted as a boil-
ing geyser of vituperative eloquence, and could
move human masses with his platform oratory.
When election time came around, he was asked to
contest a much-coveted seat. He didn’t want to,
but a political agency dangled before him the bait
of <£loo for election expenses. The chance was
too tempting, so he resolved to cut in. He was
Mentor to a Telernachus, but for the nonce he
chose to reverse their mutual relations. Having
induced Telernachus to buy for him a bottle of
brandy and a pannikin, the pair retired to a
secluded spot in a popular reserve. “Telernachus,”
said Mentor, “I’ll tell you a secret. The public
credits me with possessing a strong stream of con-
vincing oratory. The public, as usual, is wrong.
I can’t spout worth a crow’s croak unless I’m
stoked up with alcohol. Sober, I experience stage-
fright in public. But I have now been a teeto-
taller for two years, and I’ve forgotten how much
brandy it takes to bring my eloquence to Plim-
soll-mark. I want you to note the number of doses,
and to mark the variations in my conversational
brilliancy. I will imagine you to be a public
meeting ; you may interject as impertinently as
you choose, and I will retort as the spirit inspires
me. This is not giving way, mind you, to the old
tyranny of alcoholism; this is merely using
liquor for a defined purpose. Now, for my first
dose.” . . . Six snifters, each three fingers
deep, had been imbibed. Mentor was rhapso-
dising on the inevitable and glorious destiny of an
Australian Republic. “Bah! you’d sell your
principles for a title and a pension,” sneered
Telernachus. “The long-eared idiot who inter-
jected that remark has no principles to hypothe-
cate,” retorted Mentor; “he has pawned his
lickspittle psychological conundrum for the husks
of a slavery which he is not intelligent enough to
despise ; he is a congener of the cheap, yellow
Chow, and of the murky Hindoo, and his filthy
soul is as grimy, as greasy and as odoriferous as a
blackfellow’s carcase.” “ You’re a blank—dash—
parenthesis asterisk exclamation liar, and a
” “I’ll break your this-and-that,”&o. . . .
Telernachus and Mentor were each next morning
“ fined £l, or ten days,” for “langwidge” and
drunkenness.

/The N.S.W. elopement-case, in which a|
young blacksmith marched off with the mother of |
ten children, one of whom he was allegedly court-
ing, recalls a Bulletin story in which the hero,
having married his mother-in-law, became known
as “ the bold McShane.”
In Australia, anyhow, King Cophetua,
who marries and bediamonds the beggar-maid,
mostly repents of his bargain in the long run.
The Bulletin has been counting up the Aus-

The Oountesa of Warwick— late Lady
Brooke, A. Edward’s beautiful friend — says that
it is a mistake to say that the British aristocracy
is rich. “ Many of us are deadly poor,” she says,
“little better than splendid paupers.” A little
while after she said this, she accidentally left her
gable cloak in a carriage. It was valued at .8800.
A Melbourne man was arrested at Ballarat
last week on a charge of abducting the children of
a Chinaman, with whose wife he had eloped. An
interesting case. The Australian matron couldn’t
relinquish her Chow unless she took their children
away with her—and the other man didn’t jib 1
True Love is indeed a great institution.

Two men tried to smuggle spirits from the
ship Oambuswallace, stranded near Brisbane.
Both were caught by the Customs. One was a
big commercial magnate, who used his “ yot.”
He was fined .£5. The other was a poor devil in
pay of a publican. He was fined .£50 —£5 for
smuggling, and £45 because he did it in a hired
skiff.

Shanty,” by Bulletin writers, is now supplied in
neat cloth binding at 25., post free to any address, j
lb contains some of the best and most original

famous, and no Australian library is complete
without it.
The other day, at a very representative i
Melb. R.O. meeting, Archbishop Carr in the chair, [
an aggressive young barber denounced the local j
Catholic press. “Its only characteristic,” re- I
marked that barber, “is its well-ground scissors
and its well-filled gum-pot! ” Lo and behold, the
very next number came out with a two-column
report of a deputation to Dr. Carr himself, clipped
from the local Presbyterian organ !
The complainant in a late Melb. mainten-
ance case stated that upon one occasion when her
husband threatened to shoot her, she had been able
to save herself only by pointing to a new costume
lying on the bed and imploring him to “ let her
wear it once before she died.’ 1 It was probably a
Bloomer suit. The husband was weak enough to
grant her wish, and now, instead of being a cold
corpse, she draws 15s. per week from him for main-
tenance. Moral (an old one): Never draw unless
you mean to shoot.
A beautiful case of family affection was
seen the other day in a Melbourne police court.
A small boy, aged 11, was in trouble for burglary
and other misconduct, and his two smaller
brothers, aged nine and five respectively, turned
up in court and wanted to cheer and otherwise
express their gratification. Being ejected, they
hung round outside until the heir was despatched
in a cab to the reformatory, and then they ran
alongside the vehicle as far' as their short legs
would carry them, and told him how glad they
were generally. The scene was a valuable moral
lesson to any man who thinks of getting married
and becoming the father of three small boys.

“Handsome Charley” Lett, writes Mr. A.
0. Torrens, from London, “is one of the redeem-
ing points of the Imperial Institute, for he is
apparently the only man about the place who
knows anything about N.S. Wales, or other parts
of Australia. He works like a nigger, especially in
the interests of the N.S. W. meat-trade, and his
services are in great request by visitors.”
A sensible labor suggestion has been made
with the New Year. The executive Council of the
Australian Workers’ Union are asked, at the ap-
proaching Conference, to abol sh the practice of
appointing publicans as resident-agents of the
A.W.U. There is no goody-goodiness in this;
it is the common-sense prudence of sane men, who
don’t want to mix their labor reforms with their
liquors.
At the instance of Cr. Banfield, Ararat
(Vie.) borough Council has adopted a minimum-
wage, which has been fixed at ss. a day for
laborers and 7s. a day for artisans. It was over
this question that the feud arose between the
Hamilton Council and its Mayor. The latter was
in favor of the minimum-wage, whereas the Coun-
cillors opposed it tooth-and-nail. Since then the
Mayor has grown into a sort of local hero, and in
years to be—if, in the meantime, he does not be-
come asphyxiated by a rush of glory to the head —
he may be regarded as the tutelary demigod of
Hamilton.
Dear Bulletin, —Re “Slewed,” in a
recent Bulletin. J. J. Miller, the well-known
trotting man, has a private track at his residence,
Bayswater, Vic., and as it is situated on an angle
of a rough bush road strangers frequently do a
preliminary of two or three miles before they find
out their whereabouts. When a village settle-
ment was planted in the neighbourhood the poor
settlers on their way home from the Gully pubs,
spent many a weary night walking round and
round, until at last the compassionate J. J. had
movable hurdles put up to stop them. But some
of them wouldn’t be stopped, and one man next
morning told how he had lost his way home, and
had climbed at least 100 shaky fences, and he
didn’t think much of the men that put them up.—
W.M.

Mr. J. W. Johnston, the new Sydney
city alderman, should form a very decided addi-
tion to the brains of the corporation. Mr. John-
ston is an accountant of the deepest dye and a
level-headed business man, whose quickness and
concreteness of expression have given him a front
place in all the numerous and successful com-
mercial enterprises with which he has been as-
sociated.

“Dolly Dialogues,” related to Gov. Hopetoun ?
His portrait in the last number of the Idler shows

John Earle, a Scotchman, bom 1779, is
the oldest living man in Britain. Ha is a High-
land shoemaker, and has three unmarried daughters
with him, aged respectively 91, 87, and 79!
“Queen Victoria,” says a snow-dropping
London weekly, “has written several books, and
you cannot flatter her more adroitly than by
affecting to treat her as a member of the republic
of letters.” Disraeli, like the wily Israelite he
was, once started a sentence : “ Authors, madame,
like your Majesty and myself.”
This is a time for giving thanks
To Providence —this is a time
When managers of brittle banks
Sing cheerily a song sublime.
An angry country may condemn
The Premier who dropped his Bill—
That matter matters not to them ;
Thank Heaven they’ve a Council still !
A gathering of forty-eight
Large representatives of Land,
Four-dozen pillars ”of the State
Who lovingly united stand
To block the course of simple right
And set aside the common will.
Whilst Harper, on his knees at night,
Thanks Heaven there’s a Council still !
“ All hope abandon,” Fat Man cries,
In warning tone, with frown severe ;
“ All hope abandon, he who tries
To pass direct taxation here.”
As well one might expect to see
A river winding up a hill.
To cast it forth they all agree—
Thank Heaven they’ve a Council still !
Thank Heaven for this chance, indeed,
To show the mass of careless fools
How Justice bows the knee to Greed
In places where old Fat Man rules,
And all the People’s voting strength
Is suddenly reduced to nil;
The sight shall make them rise at length
Thank Heaven they’ve a Council still !

Women and Politics.
Tins (from an autograph copy) is the sorb of
thing Caldwell, M.P., inflicts on his brother b.A.
legislators :
They are coming 1 they are coming I
Every day we see them come I
And in the Speaker’s gallery
The ladies seem at home.
Although their dresses look so gay,
Their faces look so charming,
Their presence in the Chamber now
Is pleasantly alarming.
So gracefully they sit and smile,
Those fascinating wenches;
They lure the very Ministers
From off the Treasury Benches.
The learned doctor often leaves
His colleagues when he's seen them,
And Jenkins, too, onr G.P.W.,
Glides off and sits between them.
But will they be contented there,
I wonder more and more ?
Or will the dearies soon demand
A seat udoii the floor?
If so, farewell to serious thought,
As Paddy Coglin said,
Men will no longer have control
Of either heart or head.
Dear ladies, you are very dear,
While still you smile afar ;
But, in the interests of mankind,
Oh, do not cross the Bar 1
Caldwell is the member who proposed emascu-
lation as a remedy for certain offences—evidently
not seeing that the chief argument against the
scheme is that it might bring down a lot of
people to the Oaldwellian poetic level.

Christmas season has been a regular hea-
then carnival throughout Bananaland. In the
Bundaberg district, Kanaka riots and a fresh
Kanaka murder. On Thursday Island, the
Japs pursued various Polynesians into the
sea and were rewarded next day by a savage
attack from the islanders, who laid out several
adversaries—one fatally. Various minor outbreaks
are recorded, and the season only wanted a canni-
balistic feast to round it off into one glorious
harmonious whole.

The assertion that the Prince of Wales has
left Russia on excellent terms with the new Czar
provides The Age with a new argument in favor
of the monarchical system :
In international relationships the opportunities pos-
sessed hy the throne are of great and peculiar value. In
the intimacy and mutual regard of royal houses there are
abundant means of softening the roughness of official
strife, and restoring a friendly tone to diplomatic inter-
course.
Unfortunately for this pretty theory the mutual
animosities of royal houses have been directly
responsible for most of the bloodshed modern
Europe has seen, and the little differences of mon-
archies, instead of being settled in the backyard,
are fought out all over the continent.
Nervous sufferers will dud it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.

A cold begins with inflammation of the membra-
neous lining of the intricate little passages of the
nise (which is a Hampton Court labyrinth on a
snail scale). Homccea at once subdues the in-
flammation by reducing the swelling, and thereby
relieving the feeling of “stuffiness.” Moreover, it
farms a protective covering for the delicate mem
brane, and prevents cold air, or other irritating

properties of the ointment further come into play,
Not much is known about the way in which mflu
enza originates, but it is quite clear that it is caused
by an invisible germ or microbe of some kind.
Homocearoay.therefore, be confidently recomendedi
both as a cure and a preventive of influenza.

Fred. Belton, when in Sydney, used to relate that when
an Edinburgh panto, manager, he engaged the best
woman in the profession for the fairy. When she turned
up only at the last rehearsal, he was horrified to find her
a doubled-up, mumbling witch, looking like a mere heap
of clothes. At night, she bounded on, a thing of beauty
and a joy for four hours. Geo. Vandenhoff had a similar
experience, after rehearsing with ugly Miss Cushman for
the first time.
“Cronky ” writes ; “Alice Simmons’ is incomparably
the best voice in ‘Beauty and the Beast,’ though she
may be nothing more. On the opening night, the only
song of this Australian girl was placed in the first scene,
and gained a furious encore, while no other piece of sing-
ing obtained a genuine one. So (he song has been trans-
ferred to t,he last scene, while the transformation is being
arranged behind, and the audience are supposed to be
pumped out. Nevertheless, the encore comes again, and
the song is more brilliant than ever, like a refreshing
shower on faded roses. The Australians are so fond of
the English, you know.”

Irving, remarks The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
had a great ovation in Dublin, which, judging by the
newspaper accounts, could only have been equalled by
that received by Madame Ristori, in Sydney, the biggest
thing I ever knew in that line. That occasion will never
fade from my memory, as John Bushelle and “yours
truly,” at the request of the Marquise (who didn’t speak
a word of English), took it in turns to address the
enthusiastic crowd, at the Exchange Hotel, in the purest
Tuscan, as taught by that marvellous old polyglot,
Zlotkowski.

The hero of W. S. Gilbert’s new opera is a Regent of
Denmark, who sneaks away from his court in disguise and
travels round the country as a plain mister to escape the
“everlasting accompaniment of the National Anthem.”
Like the Banbury lady, whom everyone knows,
He’s cursed with its music wherever he goes.
Though its words but imperfectly rhyme,
And the Devil himself couldn’t scan them,
With composure polite he endures day and night
That illiterate National Anthem.
Amen I
Novel playbills for “The Judge,” at Melb. Bijou, are
blue writs issued by attorney Phil Stuart, in the suit of
William Elton and Co. v. “You.” They are quite genuine-
looking articles in their folded form, so much so that a
number of bank-clerks turned ghastly pale when a man
came round with a bag and solemnly served them across
the counter. In several oases the unhappy “tellers”
denied their identity on the spot, and told the supposed
limb of the law to—haw I—be d d. By the time he
had served all the available customers and heaved a
dozen or two up among the ledger-keepers, the writ-man
lost his terrors. Adolphus and Ferdinand smiled across
their lofty collars and thought it a splendid advt. But
imagine their feelings when the real writs arrive, at the
instance of a brutal tailor, or a beastly boarding-house
proprietor! The dear chappies will grab the documents
greedily, expecting more jokes, and O, what a difference
in the morning!

In America, wood-pulp is prepared for
paper-making by an expensive process. In
Queensland, excellent pulp (megass) lies ready-
made to hand, and the only use the planter makes
of it is—to burn it.
India presents a unique example of Free-
trade. When a five per cent, ad valorem duty
was levied, for purely revenue purposes, on im-
ports, cotton goods—which are now largely
manufactured in India —\vi re exempted, lest the
tax should encourage native industry and damage
the British importer. Now, as the result of much
pressure, an excise duty of five per cent, has been
placed on cotton goods locally manufactured, so
that the British importer is protected to that ex-
tent against the native manufacturer ! Evidently
the British importer isn’t a Freetrader save by
necessity. What he would like everywhere, as in
India, is Protection against the local producer,
but not being able to get it he contents himself
with preaching the iniquity of Protection in
general. Everybody is a Protectionist where his
own interests are concerned, only the avowed
Protectionist is willing to protect other people’s
uterests as well.

INSURANCE COMPANY,
346 & 348 BROADWAY, IsTEW YOEK CITY.
JOHN. A. MoCALL, President.
New York, Ist June, 1894.
The Hon. George S. Merrill,
Commissioner of Insurance, Boston, Mass.
Dear Sir,—On the 3Jtb of June, 1894, it will be three years since the “ Special examination ”of
this Company by the New York State Insurance Department. _ ■
Being strong in the belief that great public trusts, such as lifeinsurance companies, should be periodi-
cally investigated, at least every three years, as now required of domestic companies by the law ot your
State, and knowing that the New York State Insurance Department is, and will be, very busy with
other insurance companies this year, I invite your department conjointly with those of Kansas, Ohio,
Illinois, Kentucky, Missouri, and Texas to make a “special examination” of this Company
as of 30th June instant, in such manner and with such expert assistance as you may
deem necessary, with the full assurance on my part that I desire the fullest and freest pub-
licity of everything connected with the Company, and for such purposes all books and all transactions
are open for your inspection and examination.
I desire at this time to mention that the coming year 1895 will be the “golden year ” of the Com-
pany, it having commenced business in 1845, so that it will be undoubtedly satisfactory to our policy-
holders, to the public, and to the insurance departments, to enter upon the year with a certificate of a
“ special examination ” by seven of our great States. —Very truly yours,
(Signed) JOHN A. McCALL, President.
Commissioners’ Certificate.
New York City, November 28, 1894.
WE, the Insurance Commissioners and Superintendents of the States of Massachusetts, Illinois,
Kansas, Kentucky, Missouri, Ohio, and Texas, pursuant to the invitation of the President of the
Company, dated June 1, 1894, do hereby certify that we have been for the past four mouths engaged
in a thorough and searching official investigation into the affairs of the New York Life Insurance
Company of the City of New York.
We further certify that each Stock and Bond owned, each Collateral Loan, each Bond and Mort-
gage Loan made, the Cash and each Bank Certificate of Deposit, was carefully examined, checked ami
verified ; that the Policy Loans and Premium Notes were examined and checked with the Reserve on
each Policy ; that Interest and Rents due and accrued, unreported and deferred Premiums, were also
verified ; that the values of Stocks and Bonds owned, and Real Estate owned, were individually and
closely scrutinised and conservatively made; that the title to each piece of property secured, and
Bond and Mortgage Loan made since the 1891 New York State Ihsurande Department Investigation,
was examined and found satisfactory. That the Policy Reserve was checked and verified by tin
Actuaries of our several State Insurance Departments, and that every Policy and its Reserve, on the book-
of the Company, was checked individually with the Valuation Policy Registers of the Massachusetts
Department; that all Sundry Liabilities were also verified; that each debit and credit entry in th.
Company’s books was checked from the date of the said New York State Investigation ; and that, :e-
a result, on the most conservative basis of valuation, we found the Company possessed of ASSJEki. -
satisfactory to us, amounting to
£31,986,302 4a. 2d.,
And that, after providing for all possible Liabilities, including £27,789,771 16s. 2d. for outstanding
Policy Reserve, as per the “ Combined Experience Table of Mortality,” with 4 per cent, interest, tin-
total of the same amounted to £28,420,650 19s, lOd.
We further certify that, by the severest test, the net surplus to policy-holders, after providing fui
every Liability, and deducting Agents’ Balances, was, on June 30, 1894,
£3,565,651 4s. 4d.
In Witness whereof, we have hereunto subscribed our respective names, in the City of New
York, the day and year above written.

During boom-time a builder erected a row of
workman’s cottages. He wished to sell at £2OO each, but
could not, so got an advance of £225 on each from a
building society, collected the rents for two years, and
never ma le a single repayment. The society took posses-
sion at the end of two years and—still holds the pro-
pe ty. Of course, liquidation has overtaken that society;
O.ie is almost tempted to inquire “ who was the sleeping
partner in the transaction ?”
♦ • * *
Banks are institutions supported by Tom, Dick
and Harry, for the particular benefit of their enterprising
brother Bill. Careful Thomas, who wouldn’t think of
lending his hard-earned cash to a speculator, puts £lOO
into a bank, an I Bill immediately borrows it as an over-
draft. In a good many cases fools build banks that
rogues may live on ’em.
* * * *
Trade Protection Co.’s reports are mostly
graded. If a man be a ten - guinea subscriber,
no report of a damnatory character will come from
the T. P. office. Some weak traders subscribe to all
the T.P. Oo.’s, and so insure their credit; the can-
vassers know this, and make a bee-line for them when-
ever a new co. starts. The knowing traders only accept
mist T.P. Co.’s reports as truthful when corroborated
by their own inquiries ; in fact, many houses are dropping
the bulk of these societies altogether.
* * • «
The English life-insurance companies are be-
ginning an invasion of Australia. The London and Lan-
ershire is now doing business in Victoria, and others are
expected to follow. The L, and L. is a proprietary office,
which sends a large amount of the profits to the share-
holders in dear old Hingtand, and the policy-holders get
what is left, instead of getting the lot as they do in the
mutual life societies of Australia. It issues a moderately
liberal policy aicording to English ideas—a conservative
life insurance policy, according to English ideas, is one
which is void if you commit suicide, if you are hanged,
if you take to drink, if you go to sea without permission
an 1 without paying an extra premium, if you travel on
land beyond the limits of certain specified countries
without permission and without paying an extra pre-
mium, if you engage in any one of a long list of
baza dous occupations without permission and payment
of an extra premium, and if you do a number of
other things. It has a reputation for fairness in the
matter of contesting claim lat law. When it does contest
a claim, however, the fact that its headquarters are in
England gives it first-class facilities for carrying appeals
to the Privy Council and bursting up plaintiff in the
matter of costs. Also, its entire belongings in Victoria
are set down as worth £12,425.

It is well-known that when an undesirable
tenant gets into a house, and has not enough furniture to
diitrain on, it takes the owner three weeks, according to
legal process, to get rid of him ; but in a certain police-
court list week it came to light that there is a shorter
and more effectual method. The agent for a lot of house-
property told an official that he never took out an eject-
ment-warrant, as lie had a cheaper way. “I grease the
fi igers of the policem in on the beat, and the tenant’s
always gone in twenty-four hours !’’
♦ * * *
The law of distraint should be abolished, if only
fir the numberless swindles worked under it. Many
“rocky” tenants have an understanding with the land-
lord (whom they always pay) to protect their effects, by
a bogus claim, whenever execution is levied.

Dear Bulletin, — In a certain jam-factory, the
name of which is appended for your spare and private
ear, boys and girls (many of them of school-going
age and truants under the law) are worked from six
o’clock in the morning until six o’clock in the evening,
with two spells only—J hour for breakfast; f hour for
dinner. Wages, Bs. per week. Time, as above mentioned,
10J hours per day. I forgot to make sure about Satur-
day’s hours; hut call the working period 60 hours per
week, roughly. Wages: Not quite I,id. per hour. Over-
time at night is paid 3d. per hour. - Yours, D.

years ago, in proving that certain Friendly Societies were
unflnancial, being unable to meet the crop of deaths.
Unfortunately these have all been rendered financial by

Simple prospectors are warned off the daily-
paper advts. inviting men with small capital to take vvork-
i ig shares in prospecting parties or wild cat ventures.
Some alleged promoters have proved capable of taking
men’s cash and shipping or railing them off to the Never-
Never to hunt for an absolutely imaginary claim.
* * * *
Wyalong is stirred to its bottom levels by the
pernicious practices of the jumpers, who want to collar
half-a dozen of the best claims for alleged breach of
regulations. As a matter of fact, some regulations have
been broken ; but they were such utterly wooden and
impracticable regulations that the common sense of
Wyalong blew them back to Sydney with a plug of
dynamite. However, a large and indignant meeting has
been addressed by a much-respected and trusted soli-
citor, who denied that he was the jumpers’ instigator ;
and asked for proof, which the meeting was not in a posi-
tion to give. This is the first lawyer that The Bulletin
has heard of at Wyalong, and he is a bad omen. One
lawyer, no matter how good or bad, attending strictly to
business, will cost a goldfield as much as three or four
dozen well-defined crosscourses, with a few hundred tons
of pyrites thrown in.
* * * *
Having had their exciting little flutter over a
three-ton disappointing crushing, the Newbridge, N.S.W.,
mine-owners are now taking out a bulk sample of 40 tons.

Vie. Govt. Geologist Murray has gone to report
on the Bendoo district. There is good gold there, but
the country is frightful and unworkable in winter. There
is a reef at Bendoc which the local people got into their
hands by simply defying the Melb. directors. They
disgusted the shareholders and beught the lease off the
sheriff when it was sold up.
* * * ♦
Matters gloomy at Steiglitz. Many mines with
big titles are not to resume work. In the boom 156
leases were taken up. Less than a dozen are being
worked.
* » * «
The complaint at Gympie (and, to some extent,
on the Towers) is that the big divs. go into the pockets of
half-a-dozen or a dozen people ; while the mass of the
community benefit only indirectly, if at all. This yarn is
old enough to die and be buried awhile. Some men, it is
true, have phenomenal luck, hut, on the average, on
settled fields, a man gets out of mining a reward in pro-
portion to what he puts in, and the fact that profits go
into few hands generally proves that pluck and per-
sistence linger in few hearts. Nothing venture, nothing
have.

column of gush about the gold-mines it is affirmed that
the ’94 output was almost double that of ’93 -viz., 13920 z.
That is about 250 z. a week !

The Australian Gold Recovery Company are
acting sensibly at last by erecting a 5-ton cyanide plant,
at the Austral Otis Company’s ore-treating works, for the
practical treatment of parcels of ore. The practical
management of the Gold Recovery Company now being
in the hands of the well-known American metallurgist,
Mr. Louis Jaunin, jun., recently editor of the Mining and
Engineering Journal of New York, brisk operations will
probably result, providing the validity of the company’s
protest be upheld.*
The Glen Wills goldfield is going ahead. The
Maud Proprietary Company are now erecting a large Otis
Crusher with concentrating appliances capable of treating
250 tons a week. It will be interesting to watch the
result of the treatment by this machinery, and compare
it with that of the stamper battery at the United
Brothers.*
A mistake is made in referring to the Gol-
conda and the Chicago “as the only mines on the
Island ” (W.A.); as the Austin Gold-mines, Ltd., lately
floated in London, has an area of 36 acres and is now
erecting machinery, including a 20-head Californian type
battery made by the Austral Otis Engineering Co., with a
cyanide plant for the treatment of the tailings.*
The 20-head battery for the United Brothers
Gold-mining Company at Glen Wills, Gippsland, is now
being erected by the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
of Melbourne. The battery is built entirely on the latest
American principles, and will serve as a test of the advan-
tage, or otherwise, of the American over the ordinary
type of battery.*

A London paper by the last mail pro-
phesies that another Oleveland-street horror will
be exposed at an early date, with the implication
of a big literary man as an additional advt., add-
ing that “people about town will not be sur-
prised.”
Mrs. Beer, proprietress of London Sunday
Times and the Observer, is a Sassoon, and, there-
fore, very rich, remarks The Bulletin’s London
correspondent. She personally edits the Sundat
Times, and it is quite a sight to see her arrive in
Fleet-street, robed in Russian sable, with an
attendant footman carrying a despatch-box con-
taining her cherished “copy.” Her office is fitted
up in a manner corresponding to her wealth, and
is almost equal to the luxurious boudoir occupied
by Mr. Lika-Joko-Furmss.
