Johnson’s Antidote .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Down along the Snakebite River, where the
overlanders camp,
Where the serpents are in- millions, all of the
most deadly stamp ;
Where the station-cook in terror, nearly
every time he bakes,
Mixes up among the doughboys half-a-dozen
poison-snakes ;
Where the wily free-selector walks in armour-
plated pants,
And defies the stings of scorpions, and the
bites of bull-dog ants :
Where the adder and the viper tear each
other by the throat,
There it was that William Johnson sought
his snake-bite antidote.
Johnson was a free-selector, and his brain
went rather queer,
For the constant sight of serpents filled him
with a deadly fear ;
So h • tramped his free-selection, miming,
afte noon and night,
Seekii g fo some great specific that would
Cm., the serpent’s bite.
Till King B ily, of the Mooki, chieftain of
the flour-bag head.
Told him, “ Spos’n snake bite pfeller, pfeller
mostly drop down dead ;
Spos’n snake bite old goanna, then you
watch, a while you see,
Old goanna cure himself with eating little
pfeller tree.”
“That’s the cure,” said William Johnson,
“ point me out this plant sublime,”
But King Billy, feeling lazy, said he’d go
another time.
Thus it came to pass that Johnson, having
got the tale by rote,
Followed every stx-ay goanna, seeking for the
antidote.
*•••••
Loafing once beside the river, while he
thought his heart would break,
There he saw a big goanna, fighting with a
tiger-snake,
la and out they rolled land wriggled, bit
each other, heart and soul,
Till the valiant old goanna swallowed his
opponent, whole.
Breathless, Johnson sat and watched him,
saw him struggle up the bank,
Saw him nibbling at the branches of some
bushes, green and rank ;
S iw. him, happy and contented, lick his lips,
■as off he crept,
While the bulging in his stomach showed
where his opponent slept.
Then a cheer of exultation burst aloud from
Johnson’s throat ;
“Luck at last,” said he, “I’ve struck it!
’tis the famous antidote.”
“ Here it is, the Grand Elixir, greatest bless-
ing ever known,
T .venty thousand men in India die each year
of snakes alone.
T.iiuk of all the foreign nations, Negro,
Chow and blackamoor,
S ived from sudden expiration, by my won-
drous snakebite cure.
It will bring me fame and fortune ! In the
happy days to be,
Men of every clime and nation will be round
to gaze on me—
Scientific men in thousands, men of mark
and men of note,
Rushing down the Mooki River, after John-
son’s antidote.
It will cure delirium-tremens, when the
patient’s eyeballs stare
At imaginary spiders, snakes which really
are not there.
When he thinks he sees them wriggle, when
he thinks he sees them bloat,
It will cure him just to think of Johnson’s
Snakebite Antidote.
Then he rushed to the museum, found a
scientific man—
“ Trot me out a deadly serpent, just the
deadliest you can ;
I intend to let him bite me, all the risk I
will endure,
Just to prove the sterling value of my won-
drous snakebite cure.
Even though an adder bit me, hack to life
again I’d float ;
Snakes are out of date, I tell you, since I’ve
found the antidote.”
Said the scientific person, “ If you really
want to die,
Go ahead —but, if you’re doubtful, let your
sheep-dog have a try.
Get a pair of dogs and try it, let the snake
give both a nip ;
Give your dog the snakebite mixture, let the
other fellow rip ;
If he dies and yours survives him, then it
proves the thing is good.
Will you fetch your dog and try it 1 ” John-
son rather thought he would.
So he went and fetched his canine, hauled
him forward by the throat.
“Stump, old man,” says he, “we’ll show
them we’ve the gen wine antidote.”
Both the dogs were duly loaded with the
poison-gland’s contents ;
Johnson gave his dog the mixture, then sat
down to wait events.
“ Mark,” he said, “in twenty minutes
Stump’ll be a-rushing round,
While the other wretched creature lies a
corpse upon the ground.”
But, alas for William Johnson ! ere they’d
watched a half-hour’s spell
Stumpy was as dead as mutton, t’other dog
was live and well.
And the scientific person hurried off with
utmost speed,
Tested Johnson’s drug and found it was a
deadly poison-weed ;
Half a tumbler killed an emu, half a spoonful
killed a goat,
All the snakes on earth were harmless to that
awful antidote.
Down along the Mooki River, on the over-
landers’ camp,
Where the serpents are in millions, all of the
most deadly stamp,
Wanders, daily, William Johnson, down
among those poisonous hordes,
Shooting every stray goanna, calls them
“ black and yaller frauds.”
And King Billy, of the Mooki, cadging for
the cast-off coat,
Somehow seems to dodge the subject of
the snake-bite antidote.
BANJO.
Consolation.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There was moonlight on the water. The wind
blew cold from the south east and sent a gleaming
ripple across the bay. Far out the dark outline
of an island stood in relief against the sky; and
from the sands came the plaintive cry of a curlew.
A narrow jetty ran away out to the deep water.
From it a man sat fishing; his line loose in his
hand and a deep frown upon his forehead.
Fate had been cruel that day. The man had
told himself in the morning that he would confess
his love to the most adorable woman on earth,
and ask her to be his wife.
But circumstances had conspired, and he had
found no opportunity to speak with her alone.
And now, as he sat looking down at the shadows
on the water, he heard voices—a man and a
woman were singing, away up at the hotel on the
cliff. The voice of the man was that of his dearest
friend, and the woman's voice was the voice of her
he loved.
Hushed by the distance, he heard the deep
tones of the man as he sang—
one, dost thou love me true?
Tell me true, tell the t rue! . -
And her voice, how sweet it was I
Must I, then, my secret tell?—•
Yes, I love thee well.
Then their voices blended :
We are pledged to love for ever.
Let the world say what it may
Nought but death our hearts can saver.
Love with life shall pass away.
And the man on the jetty sighed.
The music ceased ; there was a sound of a door
opened apd closed ; and the figures of the singers
came down the hillside together. And the fisher-
man in the shadow heard their voices as they came
toward him. They spoke of music, of poetry, of
love ; and he knew as he listened that he had only
dreamed of happiness.
After a while the woman said, “How cold it
is !” and gave a little shiver. And the man by her
side took off his coat and threw it over her shoul-
ders. She protested, but he fastened it about her
throat.
As his fingers—a man’s fingers—fumbled at the
button-he said : “ Let me keep you from the cold
—dear —for always.” And she looked up at
him, and he clasped her in his arms and kissed her
lips.
The man sitting looking into the water felt that
there was nothing left for him t 6 live for. Now he
had not even a friend. But he kept the line in
his hand.
Back up the hill went the lovers with arms
entwined. And he was alone with his grief.
Presently his line tightened with a jerk. He
rose quickly to his feet, and hand over hand he
drew it in. The strain was great, and the frown
disappeared from his brow. Steadily now he
hauled in—cautiously—easy—ah ! Before him lay
the great shining scaly fish.
He smiled. His face was lit up with the smile.
He baited another hook, threw in his line, and
sitting down again looked fixedly into the water.
There are many good fish in the sea.
G.J. V. MACKAY.
When buying The Bulletin at any wharf
or railway - station, make sure, before paying,
that you get the current issue, as a number of
people have been making money by trading off
ancient copies on the unsuspecting.
Dear Bulletin, —A word re your article
on “Imperial Zollverein.” The Farrer-Ecroyd
you mention was sometime M.P. for Preston.
He is considered the ablest and most successful
manufacturer in Lancashire. A few miles away,
on the Yorkshire side of the hill, lives Sam
Cunliffe Lister —now Lord Masham—perhaps the
ablest manufacturer in Yorkshire. These two led
the Fairtrade movement —Protection. On the
other hand, the Freetrade leaders, notwithstand-
ing your article, never amounted to much in trade.
Bright was not an active member in his Roch-
dale firm. I know the little Sabden mill where
Cobden failed when millhands rose to influence.
He ended as he began—a bagman. T. B. Potter
lives on the Freetrade movement. This little cir-
cumstance struck me directly I went to live in
Lancashire, and helped to convert me from Free-
trade.—Yours, V.
“ Young men want to be faithful and are
not; old men want to be faithless and cannot.” —
Oscar Wilde.
There are 250 compositors and 60 jour-
nalists out of work in Sydney alone. The cham-
pion typesetter of a few years ago, afterwards
editor, writer, reader, compositor and publisher of
a well-known country paper, having been long out
of employment, has just taken to hawking fruit in
the suburbs.
A little Yic. detective was specially
appointed to the force because he looked less like
a“D” than anything else. So effectually was
his identity disguised that, the other day, when,
struggling with a “spieler,” he called a uniform
man to his assistance, the latter detained the
“D” as the suspect, and let the spieler go, under
the impression that he was a plain-clothes con-
stable.
One day recently, just as N.S.W. Execu-
tive was about to consider the case of the con-
demned Frenchman Grenon, a black rat was
caught in the Council chamber. The omen was
sure. They decided to hang Grenon. But if it
had been a white rat Grenon would have been sent
to death all the same, under the savage Stephen
cod s, for the commission o' an offence which
would not be capitally punishable in England or
in America.
The French Government comes down on
the absentee with a heavy hand. Under the new
succession duties, any alien who dies possessed of
property in France is taxed, not only on that, but
on all his worldly possessions, no matter where
they may be. The heirs of an Englishman who
dies leaving .£1,000,000 worth of goods in England
and £5OOO in France must pay legacy-duty on the
whole million—which duty would, in some cases,
amount to £3oo,ooo—or else the French property
is forfeited for unpaid duty. They don’t love the
absentee in France as they do in Australia.
A Melbourne chemist, H. H. Browne,
seeks “the bubble, reputation’’ (and gets it, too,
in a local paper) on the strength of the death
of his elder brother, and of being one of a number of
other sons of a virile sire. As thus: “ John Robert
Browne, late of Betworth Park, Sussex, England,”
having recently died in his 81st year, the world is
informed that he was the eldest son of the late
John Browne of Norton Hall, Norfolk, whose
wife duly presented him with 2ti children—
-22 boys and 4 girls. Upon which South Melbourne
is duly informed by the local society paper that
it “should be proud of its chemist.” It is further
recounted that the 22 boys of the original John
Browne, of Norton Hall, were all six-footers.
Another policeman has been officially
killed in Maoriland —an up-country slave, who
did three men’s work and seldom had time to eat
his meals. He was on duty continually in his
own and surrounding districts, was clerk of the
Court, process-server, bailiff, tax collector, labor
agent, and eight other things besides. He applied
for assistance in vain, then asked for leave of
absence, which he didn’t get till it was too late.
When he did get it he had to be arrested as a
howling maniac, the victim of overwork. Then
he was committed to*an asylum, and on the way
there tried to jump out of the train. Before he
reached his destination he was stiff in death. A
mild rider from the coroner’s jury, and a news-
paper paragraph saying that “much sympathy is
felt for deceased’s family,’’complete the chronicle.
Samuel Deering’s C.M.G.-ship on Jan. 1
is generally supposed to be a hit at the Kingston
Ministry. Deering was until lately S. A. assistant
Agent-General, but was retrenched by Treas.
Playford. When Bray fell ill, Deering was men-
tioned as a good man to succeed him ; at any rate,
it was regarded as a certainty that he would be
asked to act temporarily, although then on leave.
But Tom Playford appointed Wicksteed Acting
Agent-General, and went to London himself at
£l5OO a year, although Deering would have been
a better man at £lOOO. Deering was much
aggrieved, and it is asserted he had a sympathiser
in a very prominent Adelaide official, with the
result now announced; But accursed is he who
maketh his brother a C.M.G.
One Victorian Fat Man, Coutts, M.L.C.,
announced, when the Land and Income-Tax Bill
came before the House of the Forty-eight Rich
Landlords, that “if called upon to vote at once
he would have to vote against the Bill, but with
time for consideration he would be able to give an
intelligent decision.” Coutts, you see, knew that
his vote against the Bill wasn’t an intelligent
decision, and, knowing that, he must have known
that a vote in favour of the Bill would be an in-
telligent decision. Yet if he was hurried over a
matter which had already been before the public
for over two months the old man deliberately
announced his resolve to give his unintelligent
decision out of pure cussedness. After all, how-
ever, it is questionable if a person who gives him-
self away so badly as this ever had any intelligent
decision about him.
Editor Bulletin, —Sir, allow me, as a
professional shorthand Writer, to state that, as far
as my knowledge of Sydney goes, the statements
contained in a recent par. are quite inaccurate.
The fee for professional writing in Sydney is and,
I believe, always has been £2 2s. a sitting (occa-
sionally modified to £1 Is. after the first day), and
Is. a folio of 72 words. It is the same in Victoria,
where the charge is regulated by Act of Parlia-
ment, and where anyone can practise in the Su-
preme Court and charge the price named, provided
he can pass a test of 150 words a minute. This,
however, is not the case in Sydney, although it
ought to be. It is quite possible that shorthand
writers may be found who will write shorthand for
£2 2s. a day, without charging for foliage, and be
glad to do it ; but there are shorthand writers and
shorthand writers. For one who can only write
100 words a minute,- £2 2s. a day would be too
much, but for an experienced and trustworthy
writer, who could, if required, pass the test named,
£2 2s. a day plus the foliage is, in my opinion,
none too high. Your correspondent implied that
a judicial decision had been recorded against the
shorthand fees named above, but I challenge
him to name the case.—Yours, etc., Percy R.
Meggy, 24/11/94.
[The decision was given by Mr. Delohery, P.M., of
Sydney, in (Jorlette v. Burcher. Mr. Meggy is arguing
against established facts, inasmuch as N.S.W. Govern-
ment now refuses, in more than one case, to pay “foliage
it merely gives £2 2s. a sitting and pays the type-writer.
Ed. B.]
LIFE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
How far, to tired and bloody feet still seems
The star that flickers far away, then gleams
With scornful light and, mocking, seems to say,
“This height thou’lt gain long ere the break of
day !”
How sweet—when after pains, and wounds, and
tears,
The feet have reached the goal of weary years—
To think : “ Alone I scaled the rugged steep,”
And drink the nectar of eternal sleep !
Yet mad it were to wish that all were bright
And glad ; for darkness ever makes the light,
And rest, sweet rest, is beautiful to-day
Because dark agonies beset life’s way.
Each soul toils ever on and on to reach
Its goal, and by the way the white bones bleach,
And gaunt and hideous faces ever gaze
And taunt the fainting traveller with praise.
Oh, God, on earth, in air, or stars or wind,
If God there be, show to each puny mind
That he who tries another’s grief to leaven
Finds for himself the shortest road to heaven.
A.P.
A Donkey Ride in Egypt.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Evert passenger by P. and 0., Orient, Mes-
sageries, or Nord-Deutscher-Lloyd boats, will
swear to the truth of the following narrative.
After nine days of monotonous roasting between
Colombo and the Suez Canal, on an outward-
bound liner from Australia, we were turned loose
for a few hours at Port Said, and scrambled
ashore in a roomy boat propelled by two dirty
Arab boys.
Port Said is inhabited by the scum of Europe,
shaken up with the scrapings of Asia, and
sprinkled with the seourings of Africa —of both
sexes.
The main street consists of a long, narrow
thoroughfare, containing principally photo-
graphers’ shops, where indescribable pictures are
offered for sale. Further on, there are two
casinos, where the bands, composed of fat Dutch
women and boys, discourse sweet music by the
sweat of their brow. Side-rooms are available for
roulette, baccarat, or jietits chevaux, and the
weary “ Faites vos jeux, messieurs ” and the chink
of coin reminds one of Monte Carlo, or any Con-
tinental kursaal.
H re in the casino, the passengers sit and drink
muddy Turkish coffee, warding off meanwhile the
obscene photo.-vendor, the bagnio-tout, the pro-
fessional gambler, and the insidious youth who
invites you to accompany him to see the can can
danced.
Sametimes the passengers from three or four
different liners are let loose together while their
boats are taking in coal—boats from India, China,
Japan, going and coming.
Port Said is justly celebrated for its donkeys.
They are the longest eared, mildest-eyed, and
most obstinate of their species.
Our ears were assailed on landing with the
shouts of the donkey-drivers
“ Here jei are, best donkey in Egypt.”
‘‘ Two lubby black eyes.”
‘‘ Lottie Collins, here y’are.”
‘‘Want a ride? Good donkey. Mrs. Langtry;
Sir Charles Dilke.”
Then a sharp boy ran up to me and said, “Here
y’ar, best donkey in Egypt. Srenrypark ! Won’t
y’hab a ride on Srenrypark ?”
I am a Protectionist. I said—“ Which is Sir
Henry ?”
He pointed him out, and I gazed on him with:
real curiosity. He was the most melancholy-
looking donkey I ever saw. If ever a donkey
wore a look of abject apology it was this one.
The irony had entered his soul. He looked up at
me with two great sad, pathetic eyes, containing
a soul-repding appeal. They might have called
him Pharaoh, or Moses, or Alexander, or Antony,
or Ptolemy, or Augustus, or even De Lesspps.
But he had been labelled Parkes. He felt it
keenly, and his knees trembled.
I hired him on the spot, and rode him full tilt
through the town, and up to the Arab quarters, >
with two small Arab boys belaboring him from
behind, and my long Australian legs trailing in
the desert sand. We saw the smellfnl mosque. I
had one soul-filling peep at the blue Mediterranean
and—in spirit—at the world of tradition, legend,
romance, gods and goddesses, beyond.
Then we rode back, all that was left of us, for,
one small Arab boy remained behind on the line
of route to fight another Arab boy of equal size.
It was only when I had dismounted and paid
that I discovered the imposture—Sir Henry
Parkes was of the female gender.
When I came through the canal, some six
months later, I saw Sir Henry again. She was
much altered in appearance, and appeared more
settled in her mind. The weight of care was lifted
from her brow, and she seemed as happy as any
other donkey.
This time she was called Mrs. Annie Besant
which, perhaps, accounted for the alteration.
J.D.F.
